The Ways of Many Waters
Collects the dashing ballads and verses of sailor-men’s
and wharf-men’s life which E. J. Beady has contributed
to The Bulletin and the Sunday Times, Svdney. It is
the first attempt on the part of an Australian verse-
writer to chant the life on the sea and the margins of
the sea ; though there is one horse-poem—the story of
“ How Jack Bowlin Steered ‘ Jones.’” The volume is
bound in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will g 0
into a pocket without squeezing; and the price is sL
post-free.

THE RED PAGE.
A Tiny Anthology.
WHEN sad and tired I always make, if
possible, for the river and bush, sure
of welcome and unfailing peace. It
was in this mood that I went down the
mill lane one late autumn day. From
the leaves, swept in discoloured and mouldering
heaps round the roots of the briar and, the
hawthorn, came a scent of decay that brought
with it recollections of the waning year in English
woodlands. On one briar a frail flower trembled
in the wind. The bright, cheerful glow of the
heps contrasted finely with the dark-red of the
hages—heps and hages (0.E.) not hips and haws,
in my youth. There was a soft, clear light over
all, a faint sweetness in the air, and my heart was
set free, delivered from its world of unnecessary
cares.
I went on with feelings as light as the thistle-
down (the “ sugar-steals” of my childhood —why ?)
that floated past, and prepared to find wonder and
beauty in everything. Looking round to see that
I was unobserved I blew a dandelion clock with
great solemnity. The late blackberries seemed
newly varnished, and a leaf against the sun
blushed with blood of queens and kings.
The gold of the poplars was scattered every-
where ; they themselves stood up like a clump of
spears, with a garland of faded, fluttering leaves
round their butts. In this mild light and warmth
life was winsome.
Sure of the good moment I reached the river.
Below the weir there is a little wall of stone built
to direct the flood water, and, sitting on it as on
some old-world deserted wharf, I “put on” a wise
pipe and meditated. Above the fall the water
was so smooth and pure that its surface was be-
trayed only by the passing ripple that shook the
grove of reflected willows—the melancholy gold of
autumn —beneath the bank. But, below' the dam,
the water raced swiftly past in amber floods
on amber pebbles, breaking in the distance to
“resolute ” streams. As the water eddied at my
feet it imaged the massy gold of the sea before the
Throne; withered leaves of the willows fell lightly
into it and sailed away glorified; and lost stars
dazzled and trembled with the ceaseless flow.
The shadow of each leaf fled swiftly below it,
black, but edged with clear brown light. I was
filled with quiet joy: to give one’s self to still
communion with nature is to become more of a
man, acquiescent and compassionate.
Yet in the midst of' this peace I lacked one
thing—two, my dear heart would have said —and
that was, a book. (Here is a trinity for you : a
book, a pipe, and a talking river.) I had looked
through my shelves before leaving the house, but
had not found one to suit my wayward fancy.
Now came the inspiration: let me make a tiny
anthology for these moments of pure living. With
the sinking of the sun a cold wind began to blow,
and 1 went home with the thought warm in my
heart. A perfect one 1 An anthology to suit the
soul’s purest appetite ! Why, fed on such flowers,
it would become, like Marvell’s fawn,
Lilies without —roses within.
And by thus assimilating its beauty I might be
able to cheat the vigilance of Death and carry
through the Gate sweets he should know not of—
more to be desired than fine gold and sweeter than
the honeycomb.
With the delight of this thought came a chilling
fear. If you are your own anthologist you are also
your own judge. Thus the pleasure of gathering
is tempered by the fear of self-reservation in the
choice. I strengthened my heart and sat down to
seek out the most beautiful utterances of sincere
thought contained in my library.
Emotion giving rise to truth and beauty, in this
I sought for my poetry. For what is outside the
essence of the soul cannot be uttered poetically by
it. And emotion has a tendency to run into
beautiful forms. An elect soul, a brooding over
the waters, or the falling of fire from heaven, and
we have that “ delicate creation ” we call poetry.
The emotions roused by war have from time
immemorial given birth to lofty expression. With
this in mind, I wandered through the range of
English war-poetry, pausing over such numbers
as Milton’s bronze-like lines describing the
assembling of the hosts of hell under the imperial
ensign ; delaying with young Harry and his com-
panions, glittering in golden coats like May;
taking a desperate stand with that royal company
on St. Crispin’s Day ; tasting the sweetness and
dignity of Collins’ Ode to the Brave; and lin-
gering over, almost with tears, that noble and
tender heart-cry, “The Epitaph on a Jacobite.”
But I could not choose any : the Christian con-
science has destroyed the delight in war ; we no
longer believe that God wishes it; and-with the
decay of belief goes the beauty and sincerity
of song. No longer is war invested with
a false glory. There was an awful grandeur about
the charge of the French horsemen at Waterloo—
that thundering mass, gorgeous in colour, flaming
with steel, and with the shout of an Emperor in
its gaping mouths —that must have lifted and
transfigured the soul of every man, even that of
the rustic fresh from his English hedgerow, in the
British ranks. But now the victory is to the
groveller and the sharpshooter, and in that earnest
waiting there is time to think. And in the end
thinking will do away with war, even as it has
taken power and gusto out of the war-chants of to-
day. No, I brought not the noise of the captains
and the shoutings into my pilgrim peace.
Songs of love and war ! It is significant that
these are linked together. Are they declining
together ? Is passion dying ? Are there to be no
more cries of the heart ? The world is cooling,
and will soon be as the moon.
It is in the contemplation of that which is
waning, of the evanescent, that the modern poet
finds his inspiration and song. There is infinite
pathos in the very present death of parting, and
in the passing away of that which is young and
beautiful. Perhaps, when the brotherhood of
man is recognised as a glorious and simplifying
fact, our singers will leave sorrow and sighing and
strike a lofty note of hope and joy.
And now to my anthology. O difficult and
sweet labour! to cull f rom the vast range of Eng-
lish poetry a few flowers of light and colour and
solemnity for the solace of an unleisured life.
Here it is—with a few notes. Gan you better it ?
S
A TINY ANTHOLOGY.
The noblest first: its greatness, its sweetness, its
reserved sorrow, will give us a standard for the rest:
I cannot but remember such things were
That were most precious to me.
No further comment : silence is best.
O Proserpine! O absolute beauty of word and over-
word !
Daffodils,
That come before the swallow dares, and take
The winds of March with beauty ; violets, dim,
But sweeter than the lids of Junto's eyes.
Or Gytherea's breath.
Pass on : mar not.
From neighbour realm :
Bitter constraint and sad occasion dear
Compels me to disturb your season due:
For Lycidas is dead, dead ere his prime,
Young Lycidas, and hath not left his peer.
For fading youth and loveliness, a cup! They go flown
the young and beautiful, into Hades, and leave undying-
regret.
This from the wild bleak moorland, the haunted moor
land of lonely tower and ghostly walkers :
It fell about the Martinmas,
]Vhen nights were long and mirk,
The carline wife's three sons came hame
And their hats were o' the birk.
It neither grew in syke nor ditch,
Nor yet in any sheugh ;
But at the gates o' Paradise
That birk grew fair eneugh.
Where is the supernatural better managed ?
This by a curious few is counted unworthy of your first
choice. Heed them not. The pathos of village life is
here—almost to heartbreak.
Beneath those rugged elms, that yew-tree's shade
Where heaves the turf in many a 'mouldering
heap,
Each in his narrow cell for ever laid,
The rude forefathers of the hamlet sleep.
From Collins’ slender store I take his- tender elegy for
his poet friend :
Remembrance oft shall haunt the shore
t When Thames in summer wreaths is d/rest,
And oft suspend the dashing oar
To bid his gentle spirit rest. . .
But thou who oum'st that earthly bed—
Ah ! what will every dirge avail ?
Or tears which Love and Pity shed
That mourn beneath the gliding sail f
This for the lover;
To see her is to love her,
And love but her for ever.
It is all there : that worship we pay to a beautiful
woman ; the anguish of non-possession; and the time-
softened, regretful wonder.
And here’s for your sweetheart dead:
When the lute is broken,
Sweet tones are remembered not j
When the lips have spoken,
Loved accents are soon forgot.
We neighbour Keats with Shelley. Here we sum his
magic in a tiny fragment:
Heard melodies are sweet, but those unheard
Are sweater.
With Wordsworth what difficulty ! He crowds our
brief space. The approach to the virgin spirit of the
hazel wood; the dirge for Lucy ; the perfect vision of
Laodamia; the transfigured science in the Duty ; the
sober joy of the great Ode ; the memoried daffodils and
cuckoos; the subtle grandeur of the Tintern ; the some-
thing deeper than romance in the Keaper; the moon
above the trees burning like his own utterances, with an
unconsumed splendour of light—how great is this poet!
Let us, remembering our purpose, be content with two
quotations. This for its homely sweetness :
It is the first mild day of March:
Each minute sweeter than before,
The redbreast sings from the tall larch
That stands beside our door.
There is a blessing in the air,
Which seems a sense of joy to yield
To the bare trees, and mountains bare,
And grass in the greenfield.
For the loveliness of Yarrow let us say perpetual grace :
But thou that didst appear so fair
To fond imagination,
Dost rival in the light of day
Her delicate creation :
Meek loveliness is round thee spread,
A softness still and holy ;
The grace of forest charms decayed,
And pastoral meluncholy.
Here be music and dreams for tired head :
The island-valley of Avilion ;
Where falls not hail, or rain, or any snow,
Nor ever wind blows loudly ; but it lies
Deep-meadow'd, happy, fatr with orchard lawns,
And bowery hollows crown'd with summer sea.
Would you vision the birth of Spring ?
They watch the dawn of slender-shapen limbs,
Virginal.
Although it has not the sanction of time, I dare not
leave this out. There is faeryland, the true faeryland in
the “ seven woods of Coble.”
How shall I name you, immortal, mild, proud
Shadows?
I only know that all we know comes from you,
And that you come from Eden on flying feet.
Is Eden far away, or do you hide
'From human thought, as hares and mice and coney s
That run before the reaping-hook and lie
In the la.st ridge of the barley ? Dp our woods
And wineis and ponds cover more quiet wood*,
More shining winds, more star-glimmering ponds ?
Is Eden out of time and out of space ?
And do you gather about us when pale light,
Shining on water and fallen among leaves,
And winds blowing from flowers, and whirr of
feathers
And the green quiet have uplifted the heart ?
I have made this poem for you, that men may read it
Before they read of Forgael and Deetora,
As men in the old times, before the harps began,
Poured out wine for the high invisible ones.
Watch that man : he is dangerous, immaterial—he will
draw us into “ faerylands forlorn.”
& jX
There'you have my anthology. I know—none
better —that there are many glorious flowers away ;
but it will serve.
What do they teach ? O foolish one ! It is not
the fact but the emotion emanating from the fact
that is unpriced. By that emotion the poet lifts
us to his level—and shuts us up in content. There
we might lie as in a citadel impregnable to noise,
but we want not that. To be human the “still,
sad music of humanity” must be ever in our ears.
How simple the language charged with these
great passions? Yea, but it is not childlike sim-
plicity, but the last artful (in the best sense) sim-
plicity of great souls. I would say to the minor
poets of Australia: “Cut out these quotations,
plaster them on the Syllabus of the Future, and
live with them.”
For me, please God, I hope to have many
golden afternoons in their fellowship, under a tree
and by a certain pleasant, murmuring river I wot
of.
TASMANIA.
JAMES HERBLETHWAITE.
Besant and Buchanan.
only the coincidence of Death-days, to justify the
conjunction. Besant was an industrious journalist,
useful and unimportant. The weakness of Ins
novels, with James Rice away, showed clearly
that when they collaborated it was Besant who
gathered the sticks and Rice wdio lit the tire. I* or
the rest, Besant defended the author against toe
publisher, and possibly carried the defence to an
extreme: it seemed contradictory that, to produce
the novels which made his living, he himself relied
upon the middleman whom generically he attacked.
He burrowed among the antiquities of London,
too, and lightened the gloom of old brickwork and
fusty records. Perhaps his best work was work
which to-day is rarely read : a series of essays on
French literature, interesting and sometimes
acute. A solid, conscientious, respectable work-
man, he never brought a blush to the cheek of
modesty, or a flash of originality to the light of
literature.
Buchanan’s was a different personality, with
greater power, broader sweep, and higher signifi-
cance. His English birth is irrelevant ; his
parentage was Bcottish : he was characteristically
a strenuous Scot. Difference of generations and
environment did not prevent him from being as
good a type of his nation as Burns was: indeed,
the essential capacity of Burns was very similar
to the essential capacity of Buchanan. These
Scots are all alike. They have heat without
flame ; the True Romance lurks in their hearts,
but never issues from their lips. They clothe
Revolt in Sabbath breeks, remaining both Radical
and Respectable. They are too sane to be imagina-
tive, too fanciful to be stupid. They woo the Muse
with the uncompromising earnestness of the
Shorter Catechism, and through the fields of
gramarye their bowels move magnificently. They
are fine fellows, and far too sensible to be poets.
Buchanan wrote verses, plays, novels, pamph-
lets, and a dozen things beside. He brought
to his work a savage energy, a dogged
persistence, which made him formidable; but
memorable work is in another category. His
literary life was full of apparent contradictions ;
the Time-Spirit fought with Heredity over him,
and the battle was drawn. He was both honest
and prejudiced, both manly and mean, both
jealous and unselfish. Disbelieving religion, he
clung to itj bitterly independent, he accepted a
pension ; uxorious in his life, he professed horror
of Rossetti’s art. And, writing continually, he
left little or nothing that will be read twenty
years from now.
Buchanan’s best literary work is in his verse,
and the best quality of his verse is its .vigour. He
essayed almost every form, and succeeded in
almost every unimaginative form. When he
would be mystic, as in “The Book of Orm,” two
lines by a born mystic like Yeats are worth
Buchanan’s volume. Through his collected
volume of “Poetical Works” you search vainly
for anything original, vital, distinguished.
What you find is a vigorous intelligence
adapting poetical hints. to current themes.
There is no jet, no current; there is a flat expanse
of poetical emotion six inches deep. On the high
plane, Buchanan wrote nothing. On the low
plane he wrote many things excellent in their way,
excellent for one reading, for single publication.
A selection of his work which should include a
little of everything he published, from “Under-
tones” to “The New Rome,” would be good to
keep on the second-rate shelf. Like his prototype
Burns, he was less a poet than a forcible writer
attuned to poetry ; but, unlike Bums, he did not
borrow his lyric emotion, and he has little or none
of his own.

George :
Apropos London correspondent’s warning against Aus-
tralians trusting M.S. to London firms, here’s my experi-
ence. Having been recommended by a Vic. pressman to
publish a novel in London, I sent my M.S. to a firm adver-
tising in The Argus as Neville Beeman Ltd. Receipt was
duly acknowledged ; and when three silent months had
elapsed I wrote for news. A very tardy response de-
clared that “the M.S. had been mislaid in renovating the
office.” No further satisfaction was received, and time
passed. Writer mentioned facts to authoress of “An Aus-
tralian Girl,” who offered to write to the Authors’ Associa-
tion if I would give her names, addresses, and dates. With-
in three months my novel was in my hands (having been
absent exactly' three years), the title, author’s name and
introduction missing. No word of explanation was
vouchsafed. Sequel: I re-wrote the book and entered it
for competition at the AUstral demonstration just con-
cluded at Bendigo, where it was awarded first prize.
Verses by Robert Buchanan.
IF I WERE A GOD LIKE YOU
If I were a God like you, and you were a man
like me,
If from a throne omnipotent I ruled all things
that be,
Tidings of light and love I’d send as far as thought
could fly,
And one great hymn of happiness should sound
from sky to sky,—
And on your brow my gentle hand should shed
the saving dew,
If you were a man like me, and I were a God like
you !
If I were a God like you, and you were a man like
me,
And in the dark you prayed and wept and I could
hear and see,
The sorrow of your broken heart would darken all
my day,
And never peace or pride were mine till it was
smiled away,—
I’d clear my Heaven above your head till it was
bright and blue, . '
If you were a man like me, and -I were a Grod like
you !
If I were a God like you, and you were a man like
me, 1 . v
Small need for those my might had made to bend
the suppliant knee;
I’d light no light in yonder Heaven to fade and
disappear, .
I’d break no promise to the Soul yet keep it to
the ear!
High as my heart I’d lift my child till all his
dreams came true,
If you were a man like me, and I were a God like
ybu!
TOMMIE ATKINS.
Shrieking and swinging legs, astride
On his native fence, the Cockney cried :
“Fee faw fuin ! beware of me !
I am the Lord of Land and Sea ! ”
Out on the fields, where day and night
The weary r warriors strove in fight,
They paused a space to gaze upon
The moat-surrounded fence,—his throne l
And while they heard that war-cry float
From the smug Cockney’s raucous throat,
“ Come off the fence,” they cried, “ and share
The brunt of battle, if you dare ! ”
Yet still they heard him shriek and brag
Waving a little schoolboy’s Flag,
And angry at his martial mien
They tried to hoot him from the scene !
“Ho ho ! ” he said, “if that’s your plan,
I ’ll teach you I’m an Englishman !
Here, Tommie Atkins, —take your fee, —
Go fight these knaves who flout at me ! ”
Poor Tommie Atkins waiting stood,
And heard his master’s cry for blood,
Then held out hand to take his pay,
And drew his sword, and sprang away 1
All day the bloody strife was wrought,
The Cockney shriek’d, while Tommie fought.
Night came, the foe were driven away,—
But Tommie Atkins dying lay.
“Tommie, what cheer?” the Cockney said;
Poor Tommie raised his bleeding head,
“ You’ve licked them, sir ! ” poor Tommy cried,
And slowly dropp’d his head, and died !
Still on his fence the Cockney swings,
Loud in the air the war-cry rings,
And still, in answer to his cries, _ 'T?
Poor Tommie Atkins bleeds and dies.

I end as I began,
I think as urst I thought;
Woe worth the world, u Man
Only of dust is wrought, s |
Only to dust must go
After his life’s brief span ;
I think so still, and so |
I end as I began.
When first I learnt to know
The common strife of all,
My boy’s heart shared the woe
Of those who fail and fall,
For all the weak and poor
My tears of pity ran, —
And still they flow, e’en more
Than when my life began !
I reverenced from thle first
The Woman Soul divine,
(Mother, that, faith was nurst
On that brave breast of thine !)
1 Pointing the heavenward way,
T lie angel-guide of man, *
She seems to me to-day
As when my faith began !
Revolter, sword in hand,
Friend of the weak and worn,
A boy, I took my stand - Iff
Among the Knigh m forlorn ;
- Eager against the Strong
To lead the martyr’d vim,
I strive ’gainst Lust and Wrong
As when the fight began !
Never to bow and kneel
To any brazen Lie,
To love the worst, to feel
The least is ev’n as I, —
To hold all fame unblest
That helps no struggling man, —
In this, as in the rest,
I end as I began !
The creeds I’ve cast away
Like husks of garner’d grain,
And of them all this day
Does never a creed remain ;
Save this, blind faith that God
Evolves thro’ martyr’d Man :
Thus, the long journey trod,
I end as I began !
I dream’d when I .began ,4 ■
I was not born to die.
And in my dreams I ran
From shining sky to sky
And still, now life grows cold
And I am grey and wan,
That infant’s Dream I hold,
And end as I began !

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Attached to the prisoner’s dock at Christ-
church (M.L.) court is a time-table showing when
trains run to Lyttelton—where the gaol is. Cheer-
ful reading for the accused.
Little Blue Byes.
Oh, yes, you are Queen, Little Blue Eyes !
Your crown is your wiggies of gold,
And I am your most humble servant,
Your cavalier, doughty and bold ;
Perhaps I will build you a palace
Out there in the garden some day,
And it shall have windows and curtains —
There you and your dollies shall play.
You are but a mite, Little Blue Eyes !
But then I am strong and so big ;
You know I could kill all the “ Blackies,”
For “ Pleecemans ” I don’t care a fig,
Or Ching Kees—they’re ugly and smelly —
Or lions, or tigers, or bears—-
No, I’m not afraid of the Thunder:
That’s Sleepyhead snoring upstairs.
What’s lightning? Why, Sweetheart, that’s
Sleepy
Just waking, and striking a match ;
The Moon plays all night with Old Sunny,
The game they are playing is Catch.
The Moon is so kind ! All the sunbeams
It catches it throws right down here;
The Earth spins and races, but listen !
It makes not a sound—does it, dear ?
Oh, no one shall hurt you, I swear it —
I’ve put a keen edge on my sword,
And I’ll buy a big gun, yes, and pistols,
And guard you—l will, ’pon my word;
Come kiss me ! your kisses are honey—
Oh, yes, we shall go for a sail,
And take with us all your nice dollies,
And give them a ride on a whale.
And if we can catch a great eagle,
We’ll jump on his back ; ’twill be fun :
He’ll carry us over the water,
And up, so high up, near the sun.
If Sleepyhead dares to start snoring
When we pass by his home in the skies,
I’ll punch him ; but mind, he’s a “ bunting ! ”
And down comes the rain when he cries.
The world is not kind. There are children
Who cry every day for their bread.
The world is not good. There are many
Who wish every day they were dead.
But you shall be always my darling,
As dear to me as you are now—
Is that what you’re thinking of, Silly ?
The moon did not jump o’er the cow.
I love you—l can’t tell you how much—
Your cheeks are like roses full-blown,
Your eyes are two lakes that I swim in :
Why, there I am now all alone !
And sometimes I lie in the rushes,
Or bask in the sun on the shore —
You rogue, take your hand from my pocket !
I ’ll give you one penny—no more.
If ever you leave me, Bright Blue Eyes,
I ’ll find you wherever you go ;
I could not kill God if He took you—
My arm would not reach Him, you know.
You won’t go away ! Oh, my darling,
I want you just here at my side !
If “ Blackies” or Ching Kees affright you,
Come quick to my side, dear, and hide.
She paled, and Death took her. God help me !
I have but a lock of her hair;
A lock of her hair —golden, curly:
Oh, Blue Eyes ! I had not a care
When you came with your laughter and kisses
And climbed for a ride on iny knee :
And now, I have only a yearning
To follow you out to the sea.
V.
ANDREW SARD.
Love in the Present.
O to know how much of your life
Is a brave face donned for the world to see !
How more than much is the life you hide
For your own self’s seeing in secrecy ;
And how much more of your inmost self
You hide from your eyes, lest your cheeks grow
red,
And the shame of the thought that you dare not
view
Strike the flower of your whiteness dead.
And yet, ah, yet! is it worth the wish—
To seek and be seized of your unknown worth ?
Your smiling is sweet: if, under the smile
I saw the purpose that gave it birth—
How much might be lost, how little gained ?
What falseness shown in the guise of love?
So I take you for that you seem to be—
A girl as pure as the skies above.
It may be the world will call me fool
To give my heart for a heart unknown,
To grasp at a rose and mayhap a thorn—
A rose and a thorn on the same stem grown.
So be it! I mind not the world’s wise word ;
Love surely is not for questioning;
And so, with your rose at my heart to-day,
I dread no thorn that the rose may bring,
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

NO NIGGEE!
The Orient Company has at last been compelled to hire Indians as seamen, a clause in its transport-agreement with the Admiralty providing that in time of war the steamers

SATURDAY, JULY 27, 1901.
Foreign Loans, or Local Loans, or No
Loans at All.
Certain rumors about a Federal loan for
public works purposes are already in the air.
They are vague so far, but as the Federal
Government will have many public works to
attend to, and as the loan is the panacea for
all political diseases in Australia, they are
probable enough. Nowadays Australia floats
most of its new loans locally, partly because
the London market won’t supply the money
so cheaply ; partly because the London mar-
ket shows, at times, a certain reluctance to
supply the money at all ; partly for very
shame’s sake, because the country’s foreign
public and private debts already amount to
nearly one-third of its wealth, and it is in
pawn more deeply than any other civilised
land on this globe. During the last twelve
months something like £5,000,000 or
£6,000,000 was borrowed locally. The prac-
tice is much better than the old one of raising
the wind in London, and there is at least
something of independence about it. At
the same time it is a foolish and ridiculous
practice - an absurd process of manufacturing
imaginary paper wealth—a useless taking of
tnoney out of one pocket and putting it and an
I O U in another—a silly method by which
a man professes to become rich by giving him-
self his own promissory note at 3lr per cent,
interest payable by himself to himself, with
the right, vested in himself, to take strong
measures against himself in case of non-
payment. Also, borrowing is contrary to
every democratic principle, for it is a method
by which the community at large becomes the
financial serfs and bond-slaves of a small
class of rich men, either here or somewhere
else.
• * * *
In times of war, when a huge unexpected
expenditure has to be met for a year or two
only, there are excuses for raising the money
partly by loan instead of by taxation. If a
country whose normal income is £10,000,000
per annum wants £10,000,000 or £20,000,000
extra in a single year for campaigning pur-
poses, it might dislocate trade and finance
too much if it took all that gold directly
from its people in one year by taxation,
especially if the money had to be largely
sent abroad, and thus withdrawn from
local circulation. When it takes the gold
and gives paper securities in return, it
supplies a sort of artificial paper currency
which serves to fill the gap. But when a
country borrows, as Australia does, about
£6,000,000 a year as a regular thing—when
the loan expenditure isn’t a matter of sudden
or unusual emergency, but is as much a part
of the invariable order of things as the
policeman’s wages, or the sunrise, or the
Reid deficit—and when the money is wanted
for purely local expenditure on local public
works, then it ought to be raised wholly by
taxation, and the loan habit is drivelling in-
sanity.
* ' ♦ * *
A case can often be expressed more clearly
in a series of propositions than by a general
argument. _ At least Solomon laid down his
proverbs that way, and he was supposed to
be some kind of an authority. The Bulle-
tin proceeds to follow Solomon’s example :
(1) There is no reason why a country can’t construct
all its local public works out of internal loans or internal
taxation, provided it has a moderate amount of cash to
begin with and a supply of surplus labor. It needs the
cash for even a little can’t well come out of no-
thing, and Australia has, lying in its banks, an
amount equal to three years’ average loan expendi-
ture. It needs the surplus labor, for it wants to be
able to build its public works without crippling other
industries for want of labor. Australia has the labor
right enough. It is already spending about £5,000,000 or
£6,000,000 a year on public works, and, if it employed all
the surplus labor, it should spend three times that
amount.
_ (2) When money is borrowed locally, or raised by taxa-
tion locally, and spent locally on railways, tramways,
roads, harbors, telegraphs, and other works, it doesn’t in
the least reduce the country’s available cash. The money
is in the country just as much after it is used for these
purposes as before. There is always about £20,000,000
of the people’s money lying in the Australian banks. If
the Government took an extra £6,000,000 a year of this
by taxation, and spent it on building useful works with
locally-produced material and by local labor, and kept up
this process for 100 years, the £20,000,000 would be in the
banks just the same.
(3) For when a man takes money out of the bank to pay
taxes the Government puts the money in the bank again.
And when the Government takes it out to pay wages the
wages are promptly spent in food, clothes, boots, rent,
and beer, and the grocer, tailor, bootmaker, and publican
put them back in the bank. In a country like Australia,
with £20,000,000 of gold lying in its banks, it is possible to
pay every penny of the public works expenditure out of
local borrowing or local taxation. It would be possible
even if the public works expenditure was £10,000,000 a
year. And it can be done without reducing the cash
resources of the country by a single penny.
» * * *
(4) In fact, the process enormously increases the coun-
try’s wealth. If the State takes from the people in one
year, by extra taxation, £6,000,000 that is now lying idle,
and with it employs 2000 men, who are now standing idle,
in building with local materials a railway or other
useful work—when the work is finished the country has
the £6,000,000 cash and £6,000,000 worth of railway or
other works as well. If it does this process every year
for 10 years, taking the same £6,000,000 in taxation every
year, at the end of that period the country still has the
£6,000,000, and it also has £60,000,000 worth of public
works. The widow’s cruse of oil isn’t a circumstance
to it.
(5) And in addition, by circulating ten times over
£6,000,000 that might otherwise have been lying idle, it
has made £60,000,000 worth of internal commerce that
might otherwise not have existed. Prosperity doesn’t
depend so much on the amount of money in the country
as on the active circulation of the money. In the most
glorious days of the Boom there was much less money in
Australia, so far as the evidence of the banking returns
goes, than in the deadly stagnant days of 1894-5-6—only
it circulated faster. A Government which makes money
circulate to useful purpose can create prosperity.
(6) Of course if the Government raised £6,060,000 extra
in a year by taxation, and spent it on works like the two
transcontinental railways and the Adelaide graving dock,
which might only yield 1 per cent, on the outlay, it
wouldn’t have created assets of a selling value of
£6,000,000. Their selling value might be only £2,000,000.
Even then the £6,000,000 would be still there, and the
£2,000,000 worth of public works would be there, and
the country would be £2,000,000 richer directly, to say
nothing of the indirect gain through increased activity of
trade, and through the opening up of new country. If
the Government increased the taxation 50 per cent, or
more, and expended the money locally on useful and
profitable works, Australia would be a great deal richer
than if it didn’t increase taxation, and a very great deal
richer than if it reduced taxation, and left these useful
works undone.
(7) It is possible to make a country rich by taxing it, if
it is done the right way; also it is possible to leave it very
poor by not taxing it. If an extra £6,000,000 a year is
raised by taxation and spent locally on valuable public
works, at the end of fifty years the country will still have
the £6,000,000, and £300,000,000 worth of works as wtll.
If this isn’t done, at the end of 50 years the country will
also have the £6,000,000, but it will not have the
£300,000,000 worth of public works.
* * * *
(8) But hitherto Australia has depended mostly for its
public works expenditure on money borrowed abroad,
and that pans out very differently. A steady average of
£6,000,000 a year for 50 years borrowed in London at 4
per cent, means £300,000,000 brought into the country.
It also means £300,000,000 of works created. It likewise
means £300,000,000 gone out of the country again in in-
terest, so the country contains no more money and no
more public works at the end of 50 years than if the
money had been raised by taxation. It isn’t one penny
better off at the end of 50 years for all this borrowing.
And it is £300,000,000 worse off, for it owes that amount.
In another 25 years the £300,000,000 will have cost
another £300,000,000 in interest, and there will still be
£300,000,000 owing. Then the country will be £600,000,000
worse off than if it hadn’t borrowed at all.
9. Foreign borrowing means paying, in course of time,
two, or five, or ten, or fifteen millions for a railway that
is only worth one million, and no country can afford that.
10. Local borrowing has this incalculable advantage
(provided the securities issued remain in the country),
that it doesn’t drain the country of money for interest.
The interest compounds just as rapidly, but when a man
borrows money from himself at ruinous interest the
result isn’t so disastrous as if he did it from some one
else.
11. On the surface of things there is a glamour about
the local borrowing idea, as compared with the taxation
idea. When the Government raises £6,000,000 locally,
by extra taxation, and spends it in building, with
local material and labor, a valuable public work, the end
of the transaction is that the community has the money
and £6,000,000 worth of public works as well—total,
£12,000,000. If it borrows £6,000,000 locally to do the
same thing, the end of the transaction is that the com-
munity has the £6,000,000 cash; £6,000,000 worth of
public works ; and £6,000,000 of saleable Government
securities—apparent total, £18,000,000. The community
feels richer that way, for Government securities are cur-
rency—of a kind ; but it is no richer in reality. The
securities only represent money which the community
owes to the community. If a man has £lO he has £lO.
If he has £lO and his own I O U for £lO he has still only
£lO however much it may look like £2O.
* • * *
(12) Still if there was any possible guarantee that the
locally floated securities wouldn’t be sold abroad, or that
the owners wouldn’t go abroad permanently and take
them with them, and thus convert the local debt into a
foreign debt, with the accompanying ruinous drain of
interest, then, regarding the country as a unit, local
borrowing for public works purposes and taxation for
public works purposes would come to the same thing in
the end. But no sufficient guarantee of the kind seems
possible.
• *■ * »
(13) And even if, by the imposition of a heavy absentee
tax or by other means, the conversion of local debt into
foreign debt can be prevented—which is probably impos-
sible—and supposing the securities which are sold in the
country can be made to remain in the country, another
grievous evil remains. The country is a unit in a political
sense, but it is also a collection of individuals. If the
Government raises by taxation £0,000,000 a year for
public works purposes the result, in 50 years, will be (if
the money is judiciously expended) that the country will
still have the £6,000,000 and £300,000,000 (or some other
large sum) worth of public works. These will be abso-
lutely free of debt—the joint and indivisible property of
every individual in the community. With a population
of ten millions every individual’s share will be £3O, and
the share of every family of five, £l5O. This is the most
strongly democratic movement in the way of the equal
division of wealth that can well be imagined.
(14) And by the borrowing process, even if the borrow-
ing is done locally and the holders of the securities don’t
take them or sell them abroad, the money will be there,
and the works will be there, but the community
at large will owe to a few thousand wealthy bond-
holders in the community £300,000,000. The community
at large will be paying every year to a few.thousand
wealthy bond-holders from £9,000,000 to £12,000,000
interest. There will be a constant flow of money from
the community at large to the comparatively small class
of bond-holders. The small class of bond-holders will
really own the works, and the rest of the community will
own nothing.
(15) In short, foreign borrowing, if persisted in, means
national beggary. Local borrowing, if the securities take
to drifting out of the country into foreign hands, or if the
owners take to living in England or other foreign regions,
is just foreign borrowing over again. And local borrow-
ing, if the securities remain in the country-, means the
gradual building up, by the process of compound in-
terest, of a small wealthy class which will own Australia.
The country may get rich in the aggregate, but its riches
will be owned by a very few. France is the only visible
exception to this rule, but France remedied the evil by a
remarkable law of inheritance, which compulsorily sub-
divides fortunes at the owner’s death, and no other
country seems inclined to follow this idea.
The sermon ends here, and it only remains
to make a few concluding remarks and take
up the usual collection.
* * • *
The taxation process is the only true
form of State Socialism. It means that the
public works of the State are equally the
property of every inhabitant. And the bor-
rowing process hasn’t a single advantage or
semblance of advantage over the taxation
process to make up for its obvious draw-
backs and its glaring absurdity. The man
who can lend himself £6,000,000 a year for
any purpose on his own unsupported lOU
can just as well give himself £6,000,000 a
year. So can the State. The amount to be
raised is the same. The community from
which it is to be raised is the same. The
purpose is the same. The country, as a unit,
gains nothing by issuing ridiculous bits of
paper to the effect that it owes itself the
money, and promises some day to take it out
of its back pocket and pay it honorably
to its front pocket. But the country,
as a collection of individuals, loses
a great deal, for by the process of compound
interest a comparatively small wealthy class
arises which has a huge mortgage on the
bulk of the nation. If 500 people in, say,
Melbourne have their Melbourne property
mortgaged for £500,000 to one Melbourne
usurer, it doesn’t make Melbourne any
poorer in the aggregate, but it isn’t good for
the 500 people all the same. And if the
usurer gathers up his mortgages and goes to
live permanently in England, then Mel-
bourne and all Australia do become poorer
in the aggregate—£soo,ooo poorer. If that
usurer’s £500,000 is in Government stock
instead of mortgages, his exit has just the
same effect. But, if there is no public debt
and no Government stock, the position is
different. No man can gather up his share of
the railways and harbors and take that with
him when he leaves. These assets are the
property of the people who stay in the
country, and of no one else.
Colored-Labor: The Kanaka Phase.
The Bui.letin does not pose as an apostle of Sen-
timentality. It recognises that Nature is red in
tooth and claw; and that Man is often redder.
By necessity, too, in the present state of human
things. Whole tribes of the lower animals are
necessarily murdered that Man may live and
thrive; the chariot of Civilisation necessarily
crushes savage races under its wheels in its
triumphal progress. Whether civilisation is
really a different form of barbarism, whether
every triumph brings us nearer to final defeat, are
questions which can be argued till the sun freezes.
The Thing Goes On.
• * • *
But it is one matter to permit destruction which
cannot be prevented, and quite another matter to
encourage destruction which can. Wanton cruelty,
causeless murder, are gradually removing many
picturesque animals, many picturesque human
races, to the land of Memory and Regret. When
all the Paradise-birds have been shot for their
feathers, or for sport ; when all the Maoris
have been killed-off, like the blacks, by
white men’s vices, or diseases ; and when the
continual process of destruction has annihilated
similar beautiful species of animals, similar primi-
tive races; then this earth will be a much less
interesting place to inhabit than if it had fallen
•into the charge of a wiser kind of civilisation.
This has become so obvious to intelligent people
that many societies have been formed, and in
many countries national action has been taken, to
prevent the useless destruction of indigenous
species, and aboriginal races.
* * * *
The generic South Sea Islander included in the
term “Kanaka” varies greatly in his native
homes. Some of him are mild and attractive,
carrying primitive art to a high degree of elabora-
tion, perpetuating some charming old-world tra-
ditions, affording valuable data for scientific in-
quirers ; and, generally speaking, cutting a harm-
less tropical figure in the world. Some of him
are fierce and warlike, and inclined to welcome
the Gospel, in the shape of its missionary repre-
sentative, by roasting it in a stone oven as a pre-
liminary to full acceptance ; yet equally harmless,
for the practical purposes of- civilisation, if left
alone on their islands. There is no reason what-
ever, except the greed of white men, careless of
injury to their own race, why they should be
transported to fertilise Queensland sugar planta-
tions with their blood and their bones.
* ★ * *
They do no less: the edifice of the Q. sugar
industry is built on the bones, and mortared with
the blood, of murdered Kanakas. Just how many
Kanakas have been buried in Queensland since
Bobby Towns’ historic shipment in the sixties, it
is difficult to say ; but an estimate of 30,000 is
somewhere near the mark. In ’O7, the Kanaka
was cheap and plentiful—his price, delivered,
was only £10; now he has been killed off
to such an extent that he is rare and
dear, and costs about £3O. In the early days,
when the Kanaka trade was an unmitigated slave-
trade, the murders on the islands, the murders on
the voyage, the murders on the plantation, were
very numerous. Even as late as the “ Hopeful ”
era, the Kanaka death-rate was tremendous.
And to-day, when the Kanaka has to be com-
paratively well-treated because his death means
considerable cash out of pocket, his death-
rate in recent years has still been from
four to five times as great as that of the
white Q. population—from 50 to 60 per 1000
per annum as compared with 12 to 14 per 1000 per
annum. And it must be remembered that the
Kanaka death-rate is a death-rate of adults almost
exclusively ; while the white figures include the
vastly preponderant infant mortality. The adult
Q. Kanaka is killed-off ten times as fast as the
adult Q. white.
*
Why ? Because the Kanaka thrives in his own
home, and decays out of it. He is really a tender
plant. Unused to hard labor, and reared on a
fish -and - v ege t able diet, he has apparent strength
but no stamina. He is peculiarly subject to lung
complaints; even under the best conditions he
pines and dies without visible cause—seemingly
from sheer home-sickness. The sugar industry
takes no account of these things. There is no
sentiment about the sugar industry. The Kanaka
is the planter’s animal, like his horse or his cow.
When he dies there is little more fuss about him
than when a beast of burden dies. “It is a
damned nuisance!”—that’s all. There is a hole
in the ground, a howl in the huts at night; and
the sugar-mills grind on, grinding out their 55 per
1000 Kanaka death-rate. And our sugar is white
—not blood-red : surely it is wonderful!
* * * *
Even the cheap-labor cry will no longer excuse
the continuous slaughter of Kanakas. The
Kanaka has ceased to be very cheap. He remains
“reliable,” in virtue of the modified slavery of his
three-years’ indenture. The one thing which
makes him valued is the certainty that he will be
on the spot at the vital time of the cane-farmer’s
year—the time when the cane is cut. All the
other operations of the industry can be and are
performed by occasional white labor. The trash-
ing, upon which so much stress is laid, is
not a vital point: many experts think it
unnecessary; probably half the cane in
Queensland is never trashed at all,
and the remainder is trashed chiefly to keep the
“ boys ” employed while waiting for the cutting.
The one essential of the Kanaka’s utility is his
presence at cane-cutting. The farmer will not
employ white men all the year round ; but he
must get his cane to the mill when the density of
the juice is greatest. And, as long as he can get
an indentured Kanaka, he will not pay white men
10s. per day in the season to cut cane—which is
what the job is worth.
« * * *
Yet the sugar industry, properly conducted, can
well afford to pay cane-cutters 10s. per day.
Given good land, there is doubtfully a more pro-
fitable crop in the world. Good sugar-land will
average 20 tons of cane to the acre, with a clear
profit of ss. per ton of cane under present Q. con-
ditions ; so that with 50 acres under crop a farmer
can make £250 a year —given good land. The
trouble with the Q. sugar-industry at present is
poor land and half-time mills —especially at
Maekay. Naturally the half-a-crown per day
Kanaka is preferred to the 10s. per day white—
when even Kanaka labor means loss. As the
Kanaka has to be employed all the year round,
even when he is not needed, lie is little
cheaper in the long run, if the industry were
better managed. But he can’t strike; he can’t
agitate for higher wages; he is “reliable” at
crushing-time. The white man can, does, and
will do the work equally well —if he is paid white
man’s wages. The colored-labor question in the
Q. sugar-industry is not a question of white man’s
incapacity to do the work. It is a question of
better farming, better milling, and better manage-
ment.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More Shocked Cohentingenter.
The plaint of the returned cohentingenter that he
is treated as a mere ordinary being, and paid the
specified sum for his services, and is then expected
to make his own living like any other person, in-
stead of being something extra in the way of a
Joss all the rest of his life, is growing pathetic.
Witness this great lamentation (one out of dozens
of lamentations) by one W illiams in Melbourne
Akgus
I returned by the Orient in January last, having had
the fever, and after receiving my discharge applied to the
Railway department for a position, also to other branches
of the service. I remained in Melbourne for a consider-
able time, doing my utmost to obtain employment I
have had a good education, and am capable of turning
my hand to many things, but failed utterly to get any
satisfaction from the Government, except a card some
eight weeks ago to say that my name had been registered
for temporary employment in the Railway Department,
and that if not called on within six months I would have
to renew the card.
This, then, is the sum total of a grateful country’s
efforts on my behalf, and on that of many more of my
comrades-in-arms. Inquiring one day at the railway staff
offices, Spencer-street, from an official, who appeared to
reign supreme in that quarter, as to my chance of ob-
taining employment within a reasonable time, I was
asked in a most offensive tone what right had Ito work
from the Government any more than the scores of un-
employed who walk the streets of Melbourne
I am sure that did the public know how their proteges
are being treated they would be astonished and far from
pleased.
I am now earning a precarious living by laborious work
in the bush, and after the day’s toil can reflect at leisure
on the fickleness of mankind.
This individual had two pleasant sea-trips and
about 18 months soldiering (he wasn’t compelled
to go soldiering ; he did it because he wanted to)
at about twice the wage which the most lavish
country outside Australia pays to its troops, and
14 times the wage that many countries pay. His
pay was more than a bush-worker can make in
Australia nowadays. He had a fever ; many
thousands of other men have had fevers, but his
pay went on all the time, while that of other men,
under like circumstances, mostly stops short. The
country paid him his extravagant wage to the
last penny. And now this truculent and highly-
remunerated individual insists on knowing why
he isn’t given a good life-billet to the exclusion of
other men who haven’t had a steady 18 months’
job at 4s. Gd. a day, and who had to scramble for
themselves when they had fevers. He is shocked
at the ingratitude of the country because,
after paying him the high price agreed on
for his services, it doesn’t take special care of


him all the rest of his life as well. He is now
(just think of it!) earning a precarious living by
laborious work in the bush, just like any common
mortal. He was asked, in quite an offensive tone,
by some uncivil worm in office, why he should be
regarded as more important than anybody else —
just as a cockroach might ask the Apostle Paul
where he got that hat. He—it is a shuddersome
thing to write, but it seems to be really true—he
has to work for a living. In a word, he is just
like the returned soldier of any other country,
only with less service than many of them, and
more pay than all of them, and he is shocked at
his country’s ingratitude. It is a hard world—a
hard, cold, hustling world !
Tht Hour and the Peacock.
Unless there is a really brilliant fighting capacity
about Victorian Premier Peacock, that elderly
young man with the loud laugh that sometimes
reveals the vacant mind might as well drop his
Constitution Amendment Bill before he starts. It
is a very good Bill, just as a gun may be a very good
g un —but a good Bill and a good gun both require
a good man attached. The Bill' is the simplest
and most logical that could be imagined. Vic-
toria, with so much of its legislative work handed
over to the Federal Parliament, needs a smaller,
cheaper and more portable Constitution in place
of the old three-decker. The most satisfactory
way, judging by the success of the Federal ex-
periment, is to let the people appoint men to
make a new Constitution. So the Bill provides
for the election of a Convention of 23 members,
one from each Federal constituency, to draft
the measure. Parliament is to consider
it and suggest amendments. It is then
to be re-considered by the Convention, and
finally accepted or rejected by popular vote. The
total electors in Victoria are 257,000, and as 129,000
of these, or rather more than half, have votes for
the Council, the Council’s electors will really elect
the Convention. If they are quite satisfied with
their blessed Council (as the Council says they
are) and quite content with a system under which
they—being mostly Democrats —have to choose
their M’s.L.C. from among about 2000 rich land-
lords and whereby about 127,000 of them are
declared unfit to sit in the Legislature which
nominally represents them (as the Council says
they are), they will see that members are
elected who will carefully preserve that beautiful
bulwark of the Constitution. If the Council
isn’t a mean coward and a shameless liar it will
backup its frequent boast that its constituents
are fully satisfied with it by giving them this
chance to say so fairly and squarely—in
other words, it will accept Peacock’s Bill.
But in the debate on the Address-in-Reply
the Council declared with amazing unanimity
that it wouldn’t accept the Bill, and that
the very idea of permitting its constituents to ex-
press their real opinion about it was an outrage.
If Alexander of the Shriek simply means to pass
his measure through the Assembly, and then see
it rejected by the Council, and then do nothing
more except keep on passing it and seeing it
rejected, he might as well leave it alone. If
Roberts had simply requested Cronje to retreat,
and when he didn’t retreat had just kept on
requesting him, both parties would probably be
there yet. Supposing Peacock adopts the same
policy he will also be there yet. There are possi-
bilities in the case if Peacock, when his Bill is
thrown out, will dissolve the Assembly and stump
the country like a demon of unrest with this pro-
position : “If you send the Ministry back to
power the Assembly is going to pass this Bill
again, and if the Council doesn’t accept it the
Assembly will stop supplies and lay up the whole
machine of Government till the Council sur-
renders.” There would be hope in a case like that.
The Council isn’t a fighting body—it is only a
bragging body ; and it looks large and bumptious
merely because it has never, since Higinbotham’s
day, met a Premier who would really fight. If it
met him this time it would collapse like an old man
with a huge corporation when struck by Fitz-
simmons. And the country would back up a
Premier like that if it could only find a
Premier like that to back up. He would be the
greatest man in Victoria if he fought the fight to
a finish —if not the greatest in Australia. He
could sweep the poll at next Federal election, and
get almost any billet he liked to ask for. He
would have the democracy of all Australia behind
him, and all the moral support that the Federal
Parliament could give. Some day, the People’s
House must needs fight the overbearing oligarchy
of the 48 Rich Landlords, and it will never have a
chance to fight it on a more popular issue than
this. Also, it will never get a better opportunity,
for the establishment of the Commonwealth makes
a reform of the State Constitution essential. The
Hour is there ; so is the Man—at least, the Pea-
cock. It remains to be seen whether Peacock is
quite a man or not.

The “Necessary” Nigger.
Every person who has been almost persuaded by
the nigger press of Queensland that the cheap
black, yellow and brown man is essential to that
State because “ white men can’t work there ” is
invited to read the following. It is a slab of
wisdom which Colonel Mackay brought back with
him from South Africa, and it is all the more
precious as being almost the only dividend Aus-
tralia has got out of that country :
Then, of course, following out the same line of
argument, you come to the opinion of, perhaps,
the best-known man in South Africa, who, when
I discussed with him the question of black labor,
said : “ We don’t work ; we ride about and talk.” ....
The white man never will work in any country where
he can get a black man to work for him, and in South
Africa the black man, so far from disappearing before the
whites, is steadily increasing. That he is a satisfactory
laborer is very much open to doubt, but at the same time
everybody has got used to him, and while you continually
hear people deplore the natives’ laziness, and while it is a
fact that if it is a wet day he certainly will not turn out
at all, still no one seems to have any great hope of alter-
ing the present state of affairs. What they say over there
is that, no matter how good a worker a white man may be
when he arrives in South Africa, he very soon ceases to
work himself, and gets the black man to work for him.
There is plenty of good evidence in support of
this view. In sultry India the white man does
his work by deputy, per nigger. Ditto in Queens-
and so far as the supply of niggers will go. Ditto
In the West Indies and the eastern-central and
south-eastern of the United States. While
the nigger was available he did this even in Ken-
tucky and Virginia, which are both colder on the
average than Melbourne, and where the rivers are
occasionally blocked by ice in the winter. Also
he does the same in Cape Colony and Natal,
which are cooler on the average than N.S.
Wales. The wearied, languid, climate-ener-
vated planter and nigger-owner of Louisiana
is historical ; it was always understood that
Louisiana was a black man’s country in
the worst sense of the word. Yet Texas and
Arizona are mainly a white man’s country, and
they are both six or eight degrees hotter than
Louisiana, and 19 deg. hotter than Kentucky, and
16 deg. hotter than Virginia. The rule appears to
be that when a nigger is available the white
man abandons to him most kinds of hard
manual labor. It becomes unusual for the white
man to do that kind of labor; then almost dis-
graceful ; then the theory arises that, owing to the
climate, it is impossible. So cold, bleak Ken-
tucky and Virginia became black man’s countries
where the white couldn’t possibly work, while
in the frightful summer heats of Arizona and S.E.
California (it was from Arizona that there came
the original legend of the lost soul who sent back
from hell for his blankets) the white man has
always worked and thrived. And that is the only
visible fixed principle there is about the case.
The Law and the Liquorer.
Federation will not be an unmixed blessing to
Victoria if it entails a recurrence of the kerosenic
whisky that was commonly sold at hotel bars
when the duty on imported spirits stood at 15s.
per gallon. Just now Victoria enjoys more pro-
tection for its stomach than other States of the
Commonwealth, inasmuch as the Customs duties
are lower, and the spirit, therefore, is rather milder
and less deadly in its results than the “ Fine Old
Scotch ” which the alluring Hebe of elsewhere
dispenses at the rate of sixpence a time, and help
yourself from the bottle. The inevitable all-
round Commonwealth spirit duty seems likely
to be fixed at a shilling or two higher than the
Victorian rate of 12s. per gallon. Importers are
taking their stocks from bond in expectation of
the rise, and consumers await with sorrow the
degraded spirits which the future has in store for
them. To say that a higher duty does not
actually compel a reduction in the quality of the
article is to speak simple truth and talk commer-
cial nonsense—under existing trade conditions.
When the Vic. Customs tax on spirits was suddenly
raised, after the Boom collapse, from 12s. to 15s.
per gallon, certain hotelkeepers tested the feeling
of their customers re the substitution of a spirit-
measure system for the old rule of “ help your-
self.” Also an attempt was made to fix a higher
rate for the usual brandy-and-soda order. But
the public resented innovations. The man who
wants only a little spirit for his sixpence couldn’t
See any necessity for having it measured out to
him, and the man who wants a lot —the swiller
whose every “nip” represents a loss to the sup-
plier—roared vehemently against the proposal to
lessen his consumption. So the hotelkeepers with
scruples concerning their imported liquor brands
dropped their scruples, and did much the same
thing as the traders who never had any. They
kept their profits up to the old level by selling in-
ferior stuff at the old price. Sober customers de-
tected the difference every time they took a drink.
Boozers were mostly too unsober to notice it. And
so things went on until duty was reduced and trade
competition—in the big cities anyhow—restored
the liquor to a comparatively harmless state.
Now that the Victorian dealer is threatened
with a higher duty again, the local consumer
of bulk and bottled spirits on licensed premises is
enti led to get a measure of consideration from the
Com nonwealth Government for his liver, kidneys,
bra.n, and general well-being. An increase of 2s.
per gallon in the spirit duty represents, from the
purchaser’s point of view, a rise of more than 50
per cent, on the manufacturer’s price for a bottle
of “square” gin. If Premier Barton and Co.
will paste that fact inside their hats, and try to
understand its commercial bearing on the
stummick of every respectable voter (total
abstainers being eminently disrespectable in the
sight of the revenue), they will admit the injustice
of leaving an increased duty on. spirits to work
out its own damnation under the Commonwealth
as it did under the Vic. State law of not long ago.
The stummick aforesaid cries aloud for protection
against adulterated liquor. It doesn’t want its
owner to help himself from the bottle at the hotel
bar. It wants the Govt, to measure out a fair
amount of spirit which can be sold for sixpence
by a licensed victualler who is practically paying,
say, 2/4 per bottle to the Govt, for the privilege of
possessing it. The further and greater question
as to entire Government control of the liquor
trade is not more important to public health than
the question of adapting the depth and circum-
ference of a licensed sixpenny drink to the size of
the Customs duty.

Civil Service Appeal Boards.
“Ishmael,” a Maoriland ex-railway official who
has been there, gives his experience :
A short time ago I read somewhere that N.S.W. civil
service was agitating for an Appeal Board. This is
reasonable ; but let the N.S.W. civil servants take warn-
ing from Maoriland, and see that no political interference
or power of veto is allowed to crawl into its articles of
constitution. I am intimately acquainted wjth the work-
ing of the largest Public Service Appeal Board in Maori-
land —the Railway Appeal Board. It was brought into
existence by the Railway Classification Act of Oct., ’97.
Each island has a board of three members. The railway
officers (Division I.) and the wages men (Division II.) each
elect a member to represent them, by universal
ballot. These two elected members, with a Judge
or Magistrate (who is chairman), constitute the
Board. Any railway official having a grievance
has a right of appeal to the Board. On the
first glance things appear fair enough, but the fact that
has turned a judicial body into a howling farce, and made
the M.L. Railway Appeal Board a by-word, is that the
Minister for Railicays has the absolute power to vary or
veto any' recommendation of the Board. The bestmen in
the service do not put up for the position, through fear of
the management, and the consequence is that nine-tenths
of the appeal cases are triumphant verdicts for the
Department. If a case is strongly recommended for con-
sideration, the management graciously instructs the
Minister whether he is to grant the request, vary it, or
refuse it. When any clerk, cadet, laborer, or plate-layer
appeared before the Board during the past 3j years, he
had to face the bullying of the late assistant-general
manager (who was the Departmental attorney-general),
and very often two or three superior officers and chiefs of
his particular department. He was duly impressed with
the heinousness of his crime in appealing, by the severe
aspect of several superiors, many of whom had nothing
whatever to do with the case. Plaintiff was condescend-
ingly permitted to have a fellow-workman to assist him as
counsel, but very few cared to appear, as experience
taught them that they were apt to be promptly trans-
ferred, for the “good of the service.”
Among all the drawbacks, however, the depart-
mental veto is the worst. A workman’s appeal
is an appeal against the department. If the
workman gets a verdict the defendant department
has power to decide whether it will abide by the
verdict, or alter it, or reverse it altogether, and
also whether it will make things hot in indirect
ways for the plaintiff for the trouble he has
created ! When an Appeal Board’s decision isn’t
a real decision at all, then it is better to have no
such Board.
“N.S.W. Shops and Factories Act,” writes a
worker to The Bulletin, “is theoretically one of
the best measures yet passed to ameliorate the
condition of factory employes; but unless more
stringent steps are taken to enforce its provisions,
it will rapidly become a miserable farce. It pro-
vides that no person under the age of 18 shall
have charge of any machine, or work between the
connecting-rods of any engine whilst in motion.
I know boys of 14 who fire and drive steam-
engines, but if the inspector happens to be about,
an older hand is put on for the tune being. It is
also provided that any accident absenting the
injured person from work for 48 hours must be
reported in writing to the inspector. At a certain
N.S.W. foundry recently, a boy, oiling the engine
whilst in motion, had his hand badly in-
jured, resulting in his absence from work for
several weeks. This accident was not reported,
because the boy, instead of being 18, was only
slightly over 14. Dry-grinding in the iron-trade
is also forbidden to be performed by boys under
16, but I have heard a foreman, when setting an
under-aged apprentice to this work, tell him to
“keep his eye open for any well-dressed
coming about ” (a possible inspector). Should
that personage heave in sight, the article that is
being dry-ground is put down, the emery-wheel
is wetted with some waste dipped into a can of
water near by, and when the inspector arrives
upon the scene, the apprentice is simply grinding
a chisel. What is required is an authorised, in-
telligent person, thoroughly conversant with
factory work, to drop in at shops, clad in work-
man’s garb, and nose-out evasions of the Act.
Until then sweating employers will continue to
systematically bamboozle the inspector.
“ Kiarah ” to The Bulletin about the Federal
Postal Bill:
Until the introduction of the Postal Bill Barton and
his colleagues seemed to me a strong team, and I thought
Australia fortunate in having escaped the incubus of the
wily Reid and his following of Calico Jimmies in this
early stage of her nationhood. But this Bill unfor-
tunately is clear evidence that the trail of the snuffle-
buster is over them all. They are too conscientious to be
wholesome. If there is not enough collective manhood
in the two Houses of the Commonwealth to extinguish
the snufflebusters, I want to see the Act enforced in its
entirety. This will mean that, unless the Act is directed
solely at the only straight-going gamble of its kind in
Australia, “ Tattersall,” every bookmaker and every one
of his correspondents will be debarred from using the
P. 0., save on the understanding that their letters will be
liable to be opened. Also, the large number of influential
men who do a brisk telegraphic business with Southern
bookies will find their inclination for a quiet flutter seri-
ously interfered with, if the Federal P.M.G. is the man he
pretends to be. In short, if the Act is to be anything but
a boycott of “ Tattersall,” pure and simple, Federation is
synonymous with despotism, and, as regards private cor-
respondence, we shall have as much liberty as Russians.
And, if our national conscience is pricking us so badly,
let us set our house in order thoroughly. Promoters,
aiders, and abettors of all church bazaars, art unions, etc.,
must be rigorously prosecuted ; the totalisator must be
abolished, and the correspondence of all racing clubs
(and their correspondents’ correspondence), likewise
sporting papers, and papers which publish betting prices
and stock quotations must find “no thoroughfare ”
through the P.O. The Stock Exchanges, the head centres
of the most pernicious methods of gambling, should be
converted into churches. And, by-the-way, the perma-
nent head of the W.A. State postal dept. (R. A. Sholl),
who owns and runs racehorses, is the most flagrant
offender of the Commonwealth, and hardly a fit and
proper person to exercise a censorship over its correspon-
dence. In short, the scope of the Bill is so boundless if
carried out impartially that the requisite eight millions of
revenue would be swamped by the salaries of the in-
creased postal staff necessary to enforce its provisions.

More lovely contingent items from Maoriland :
Premier Seddon is going to declare war against the Colo-
nial Office. He arranged a big patriotic reception for his
returned “ fourth and fifth ” at Invercargill, Southland,
but the transport Tagus was ordered to put them ashore at
Port Chalmers, and Judas and Co., having finished
with the 700 crippled Maorilanders, declined, even
though Seddon telegraphed till the wires were
red hot, to dump the returned empties anywhere
but at the nearest port. Seddon furiously declared
“ it was things like this that had caused England
to lose even greater colonies than Maoriland,” and he
would get even with them, which Dick did by put-
ting down his Hob-nailed Boots in his own way. He
sent out the Government boat to meet the transport, but
latter took no notice and refused to heed the lighthouse
signals ordering her to go to the Bluff. When she
arrived at Port Chalmers, Dick promptly placed
her in quarantine, but so arranged matters that
he could get several hundred troopers released, and
chartering a steamer he rushed these down to the Bluff.
So the Invercargill people got their promised big display,
and Dick is still taking it out of the Tagus in quarantine.
After all it is doubtful whether the masterful Richard
hasn’t made a big mistake. Scores of the men were quite
unfit, and were already intensely weary of the sea, and
would gladly have killed the big voluble Jingo for sending
them wandering round the island when they wanted to
get home. Also the Invercargill people succeeded in
blowing a woman’s head off and severely injuring a
number of people by firing rockets off horizontally,
all of which will cost a pretty sum in damages. Seddon
is still furious, and has set up an inquiry into the alleged
overcrowding of the transport. Two men have died since
arrival. Several died on the way over, and there are
about 40 in hospital with pneumonia and malaria. Those
walking the streets are barking and coughing in a way
that shows the seeds of death to be germinating within
them. Sending these men down to cold Invercargill in
the depth of winter was bad policy. But the sickest
feeling of all is caused by the fact that no one wants
them, no one notices them, and “ there ain’t no bands
a-pla.ving,” and the chief feeling of satisfaction with the
public is-that “ the boss ain’t proclaimed any more d—d
patriotic holidays at our expense.”

On the question—“ Can white men work in the
Tropics?” G-. writes to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin, —The only shred of justification offered
for the continuance of the Kanaka in Queensland is the
assertion, “ White men .cannot do the work.” In view of
this a paper in Alfred Russell Wallace’s new book,
“ Studies, Scientific and Social,” has special value. The
writer raises the question—Can white men work in the
Tropics ? He is well qualified to offer a reply. Though
not a man of originally strong constitution, he has lived
and worked for 12 years within 10 degrees of the equator,
“ for months together, in or close to tropical swamps.”
Under a tropical sun he has taken as much, or more
exercise than when in England, having, in fact, worked
“ out of doors six or seven hours during the heat of the
day.” And his definite opinion that the white man can
work in the tropics if he likes is worth something.
The new dogma that tropical countries are not for the
white man, except as some sort of slave-driver, Wallace
proves to be a myth. The tropical climate is not un-
healthy for the man who works. It is thoroughly bene-
ficial to the man who works intelligently. The high
death-rate among Europeans in some tropical parts is
due to disease, not climate. Disease is often the result
of insanitary local conditions, and these artificially pro.
duced. Many cities in tropical America illustrate this
More frequently the white man is carried off by disease
simply because he will not work. Laziness, leading to
sexual and other excesses, is the common diagnosis of the
trouble. Men who do hard graft there don’t die. The
truth is that the average white will not work anywhere
if he can help it. He likes to exploit the other fellow.
This is the privilege of the capitalist in most countries.
In the tropics nearly all whites can practise it, and do.
The white man is lazy ; the black man is cheap. Result
—nigger worked to death, white man rots in laziness and
lust.
The humor of the thing is that the dogma is only pro-
pounded for agricultural labor ; the white man can do
anything else if it’s twice as hard. He can dig for gold
or work as a mechanic. Wallace cites instances of men
he has seen doing hard work in the tropics, and thriving
on it both in person and purse. Everyone knows that
the enthusiastic sportsmen who work hard at their play,
and the soldiers and sailors, when at work, keep good
health in the tropics. Work is a condition of health
there as elsewhere.
According to the Ceylon Observer men work for even
50 or 60 years in the tropics, enjoying good health. The
descendants of the Dutch who went to the Moluccas 300
years ago are as tough now as the Boer. Referring to
them, Wallace says : “ The Government physician then
at Amboyna assured me that the race is quite as prolific
as in Europe.” The fact is that the white race need not
suffer in quality or quantity by working in hot countries.
Loafing in hot countries, or drinking in hot countries, is
a different thing.
