In spite of his 955 votes Findley had no real
hope at East Melbourne from the start. The
reason lies in the villainous Victorian electoral
system that puts a property-owner on a dozen or
more rolls, and lets him vote wherever he
likes —but only once on one day. In the E. Melb.
electorate are 3500 voters on the ratepayers’
roll and 1227 on electors’ rights. In addition, 125
Government voters’ certificates were issued. Of
these 3500 ratepayers, no less than 1931 are
absentees—property-owners also on rolls in other
districts. (Herein Peacock or Mason or who-
ever was responsible bilked the Laborites to some
extent by not holding the Melb. and E. Melb.
elections on the same day.) Seeing that all the
metropolitan rolls are in a similarly stuffed
state, no wonder it took 10 years’ hard fight-
ing to abolish the plural-voting swindle. Next
morning the Jingo dailies loudly vaunted the
triumph of Loyalty, and paraded the “absolute
majority ” of the E. Melb. electors. Why, E.
Melb. was smothered—stifled. The 1931 absentee
property owners settled the business. The Gas
Co. had 5 votes, and the Tramway Co. 3—all in
the names of reliable employees—while little Tory
Zeal’s name bobs up in each of the four wards.
Sydney Telegraph makes] some remarks re
Sydney betting-shops:—
The suppression t>f the betting shops of the city is ap-
parently a matter to be approached with much greater
care than one would suppose. The last prosecutions were
productive of a considerable amount of litigation, and the
police officer chiefly concerned was subjected to what
some people are pleased to designate persecution. His
companions are chary of proceeding. As they point out,
the prosecution of the shops is assigned to them as a
special duty—unpleasant, like many other duties which
they have to carry out—and they have no desire to shirk
it ; but, as they further point out, if the attempt to sup-
press the shops is meant seriously, why do not the magis-
trates inflict the imprisonment penalty which is within
their power? A year or two back one of the magistrates
announced his intention of sending to gaol the next con-
victed person in any betting-house case that came before
him. No more ever came before him—he was retired. It
is not inferred that his retirement had any relation to his
expressed intention in regard to betting-shops; it is
merely mentioned as a coincidence, and it is also worthy
of note that no magistrate has since done anything more
drastic than to inflict the usual fine.
There seems to be some difficulty in connection with
gaining access to the shops in Sydney for raiding pur-
poses. In Melbourne all the shops were raided on the one
day, and shop betting was practically stamped out in 24
hours. There the police can enter a shop at any time and
without any further justification than well-founded
suspicion. Here the position is different. The police, if
they wish to raid a shop, must first obtain a magistrate’s
warrant—a process which involves some delay, and which
experience has shown prevents action being taken until
the proprietor of the shop has had time to remove evid-
ence of his having infringed the law.
These statements are all doubtless true, and the
inference to be drawn from them is just this:
That the illegal betting men were influential
enough to secure the removal of the only Sydney
magistrate who threatened to fully enforce the
law against them. That the remaining magistrates
find it is a great deal better not to enforce the law.
And that while the police are going through
the slow process of getting authority to raid a
betting-shop somebody in the inner circle gives
the betting-shop man notice of their intention.
Also that the existing law is unworkable, and the
Government doesn’t provide a better one. It is
the case of the winked-at Sunday banana over
again. Either betting-houses should be licensed
and supervised, or, if they are to be illegal, a
workable statute should put its foot down on them
and utterly flatten them out. In N.S.W., when
the powers that profess to be find a thing con-
venient, or difficult of suppression, and aren’t quite
sure whether it is wrong or not, the regular device
is to prohibit it by a law which isn’t enforced.
Thereby the politician tries to satisfy at the same
time the crowd which wants the thing put down
because it is supposed to be a sin, and the crowd
which wants it kept up because it is pleasant and
convenient.
Presbyterian elders and others have wrung from
Vic. Education Minister Gurr a promise to intro-
duce a Bill dealing with religious instruction in
State schools. “ And +!he deputation left,” says
the reporter ; “several of the members muttering
that ‘Members of Parliament will be harassed
until the will of the people of Victoria has been
achieved.’ ” There is no mule as obstinate as the
religious mule. The people of Victoria have
achieved their will by establishing secular educa-
tion ; and the wail of churches who want the State
to do their work gives no reason to suppose that
the people of Victoria want the achievement
undone. A deputation of Presbyterians is about
as far from representing the people of Victoria as
the Tooley-street tailors were from representing
the people of England.
Senator Dawson, as reported in Commonwealth
Hansard
To give the Senate an idea of what opportunity a white
man has of getting work at Port Darwin, I may say that
while I was there tenders were called for the sinking of a
well. Three of my mates and myself went to the office,
where tenders were returnable, for the purpose of having
a look at the specifications. Strange as it may seem, the
manager was particularly frank and outspoken about the
matter. He said : “If you tender for the contract at a
wage to which you have been used you will not get it.
No one can get this contract unless he is prepared to em-
ploy Chinamen, or men who will work for the same wage
as Chinamen.”
These are the experiences which raise and justify
the cry for a White Australia. It is the men who
have seen N.Q. and taken part in its life whose
voices should ring out when politicians attempt to
palter with the question. The desire of southern
capitalists and of those who live on them is to find
a pretext for hanging up the promise of a White
Nation as long as possible, and unless the
Dawsons of the Commonwealth use lung and
vote and pen the wish will be attained.

“Nina Jeane” tells The Bulletin a tale of a
boarding-house—not a pleasant story, but appar-
ently a dismally truthful one :
I bought a boarding-house, and I’ve been troubled in
my mind ever since. I’m going to sell it again, because
the things that happen in it get on my nerves, and it
hurts my conscience to take some of the money. The
bulk of my boarders are “ business ladies,” working in
shops—wages from 12s. 6d. to 255. per week. It does not
pay me to board any of them for less than 12s. 6d. per
week, and some don’t yield a profit at even that. But it
is the poor girls that worry me. The 255. per week one
is a lady ; below that and down to 15s. they are girls ;
while the poor wretches who receive only 12s. 6d. per
week are just females. They have to be—or go to work
without boots, gloves and proper clothes. Shop-girls
who don’t wear proper clothes are always promptly
sacked, so they all get clothes—honestly if they can ; but,
anyhow, they get them. It puzzled me how they did it,
till one morning I found a brass hairpin in Mr. Jackson’s
bedroom. I resolved to-round up that young man at
dinner-time, and talk to him very straight, but he didn’t
come home that evening. There' was only one girl in the
house who had very blonde hair, and she was a twelve-
and-sixpennyer. Jackson not being available, I bailed
her up instead. I got her into my own bedroom, and
demanded what she meant by it. She opened her two
big blue eyes to their widest, and exclaimed in a voice of
surprise: “How could I pay you 12s. 6d per week if I
didn’t? I can’t dress on nothing a week, and it takes all
my wages to pay you.” I was nonplussed, and she fol-
lowed up her advantage : “ We’re all in the same boat,
barring Lady Montmorency” (the 255. per week one),
“ an’ she’s too ugly to get a boy.” I couldn’t say anything,
but got rid of her as soon as possible, and promptly lost
Mr. Jackson, too. I’ve had lots of girl-boarders sines

then. I have to take them or give up my house, and it
seems to me that the whole city of Sydney is full of
women trembling on the brink of bankruptcy, who are
driven by sheer want of sufficient money to live on to be
“ respectable adventuresses.” I regarded Mr. Jackson as
an ogre, a robber, and betrayer of virtue when I found
the hairpin. I regard him—well, less harshly now ;it is
people like him who keep thousands of underpaid girls
from becoming professional women of the town. Can’t
you start a crusade to make Parliament pass a law com-
pelling employers to pay girls enough to live on ! A rise
of 10s. per week won’t make a girl lead a good life, but
it gives her the opportunity if she wishes to do so.

The Women bill-postered as Being Dan-
gerous appeared for the first time at the Sydney
Criterion last Saturday, and turned out to be only
two—Misses Ida Gresham and Helen Gibson.
The drama opens, as might be expected, in a house
that is no better than it should be, and into this
mansion the good girl wanders (after the helpless
fashion of the stage good girl, who is always wan-
dering either in the street or in her mind), to be
immediately imprisoned in an attic, and fed on a
total absence of diet, in order that she may be
persuaded to be one of the sisterhood. She is re-
leased, after a large but uncertain space of time,
by the funny man. So far the alleged “ Dangerous
Women ” display grievous want of sense; they
take an immense amount of trouble to bring about
the fall of one girl, when outside there are
scores of equally good-looking girls who are
ready to fall like a shower of grasshoppers
with no trouble at all. After this, Sylvia starves
irrelevantly on the Thames Embankment, where
an ugly but clever Boxer Burlesque Ballet is gone
through for no specific reason. Here she is
honourably loved by a disguised millionaire who,
having knocked over a man who thereupon died
of heart disease, has been ordered by a priest to
do penance. The Father didn’t seize his oppor-
tunity to get some coin for the church, but merely
told the millionaire to go and earn his living for a
year as an unskilled labourer, and he is doing it
when the story meets him. The idea seems to be
that after a year of this kind of thing he will
only knock down healthy people who can stand it.
Sylvia, the heroine, about this time, is pub-
licly chloroformed in the open air by the two
allegedly Dangerous Women, who want to annoy
her pa, and she is buried alive, and, later on, is
rescued from somebody’s ancestral vault by an
insane doctor, who thinks he can raise people
from the dead. He is so pleased that he wants
to kill Sylvia, and raise her again, but a tre-
mendous inrush of pa and other people comes to
the rescue. Next act, the good girl, whose
capacity for falling into holes is something in-
credible, is found in a lunatic asylum, but, as it is
the sort of asylum where people escape by merely
walking out, the lady, even though she is a
heroine of the simple, long-haired kind, that has
great faith and no common sense, can hardly help
escaping. After this, nothing in particular
happens to Sylvia.
* * * *
Miss Helene Burdette always makes a
favorable impression as the frivolous, flighty girl,
but as the good girl weighed down by the awful
burden of stage virtue, ‘she is even better than
usual. It is much more difficult to be a good girl
interestingly than a bad one, and the fact
that Miss Burdette triumphs, not only over
her persecutors, but also over the efforts of
the author to make her a complete idiot,
which says an immense amount for her
ability. Miss Helen Gibson makes her first ap-
pearance as a quite unremarkable Dangerous
Woman. She looks a nice calm girl who would
be just as pleased teaching in Sunday School as
basely chloroforming the righteous in public
places. G. P. Carey has some good Sherlock
Holmes touches as a would-be detective, while C.
R. Stanford excites several thrills when he pro-
claims that he can restore to life people who have
been dead forty years. He never reflects what
complications would arise in real property if he
made a business of this.
** * *
Bertram, Court magician, who pursues the
surprising tenor of his way at Sydney Palace,
proves, of course, to be one of the gentlemen that
coin money out of the air, and make a garden on
a table. Bertram does his tricks in a clean-cut
manner, but they are the same weary old tricks
we know so well—the tricks that have to do with
cards and gloves and the usual sundries and ap-
purtenances. A magic show always reminds the
E resent writer of old schooldays, only, in lieu of
eing asked questions, you are liable to be shot
at with a card out of a gun, or requested to do
something that will make you look foolish, while
the magician does the same thing and doesn’t look
foolish at all. An entertainment of this sort looks as
if various amiable people paid half-a-crown or three
shillings each in order to let the man on the plat-
form have a good time at their expense. Of
course, it only looks like this, and the cleverer the
entertainer the more it looks so. At the same
time, the cards and the growing roses and the
vanishing handkerchiefs are beginning to be flat,
stale and unprofitable, and the public calls for
newer tricks, even if the things in the newer tricks
do not vanish quite as swiftly or grow to such
enormous proportions.
The bright and cheery “ Casino Girl ” is
not to last long at Her Majesty’s (Sydney). By
the time this issue is in print its days can be
counted on the toes of one of its pretty dancing
girls, with several toes over, unless a very large
Eiece of coal fell on the front of the lady’s foot in
er early youth and amputation was necessary.
The Italian Opera Co. is due with Verdi’s
“Aida” on Saturday, August 3, so the present
show will probably finish its career on Thursday
of next week. Sydney will farewell it with
regret, for it is a happy, airy trifle which doesn’t
wear out the intellect by having any plot, purpose,
or fixed intentions whatever. Judging from
present appearances the “ Casino Girl ” will stand
reviving later on.
** * *
At Sydney Lyceum the career of the dis-
guised burglar, who looks as if he didn’t like his
trade and only burgled from a stern sense of duty,
is destined to be short, though violent. “The
King of Crime ” commences a 12 nights season on
Saturday. Sydney first saw the “ King ”in May,
1894, when the two large, dreadful hands that
reached out from behind the curtain at 12 sharp,
and closed round the neck of the informer, and
dragged him into the recess to be strangled pri-
vately, were the hands of Albert Marsh closing
round the neck of H. Plimmer. There was an
extra thrilling episode one night in ’94, when H.
Plimmer didn’t die in the right place, and A.
Marsh, who is shot in his retreat and falls down
the stairs, fell on Plimmer’s head. Since then the
“King” has been played by C. Holloway in a
small round hat and top-boots. It was a fine
melodrama the first time it came to Sydney, and
the second time it was a melodrama, anyhow.
The Bulletin forgets at the moment when it last
heard of Marsh, but Plimmer is now in the States,
and worse actors than Marsh are probably falling
on his head in a strange land.
** * «
The “ Second in Command ” is now near
the end of its third week at Sydney Royal, and
its successor, “Mrs. Dane’s Defence,” looms
in the distance of August 3. The sor-
rows of Christopher Bingham are not going
very strongly. Christopher ought to take a
high place among the multitude of characters
that Brough has played so brilliantly, but the
two difficulties that the “Second in Com-
mand” can’t get over are, first, its confirmed
sorrowfulness ; and, second, the lonesomeness of
its hero. The author contrived to make Bingham
interesting, but he practically ended there. With
a good plot, and a good company, and good stage-
management the fact remains that none of the other
characters attract the public to any great extent.
And the one person the public is really interested
in has incessant bad luck, barring the fact that an
unnaturally large duke gives him the V.C. That
is the last straw. The public refuses to accept
the duke and the V.C. as compensation for the
other disasters.
** * *
Sydney’s two variety shows are drifting
along the same old track. The latest arrival at
Fuller’s Empire is Daisy Chard, a plump little
girl with a whistle. The alluring Elmores allure
steadily. From Monday next the Empire is to
have a dress-circle, and the man who eats peanuts
while John Fuller sings “Alice, Where art
Thou ? ” will be relegated to the ground-floor near
the door, handy for ejection. Celina Bobe is
back at the Tivoli, extracting music from masses
of firewood ; but the best turn is the Dartos,
dancing on a grey and empty stage with a garden
behind, slumbering in the mellow limelight.
Madame Dartos looks like an ivory nymph in that
act. Later on a man sings ordinary songs in a
powerful bog aocent, and pours desolation over a
pleasant memory.
#* * *
Sydney’s University Dramatic Society last
Thursday tackled “ She Stoops to Conquer,” at
the Palace, with a small-dog ferocity and a vigor-
ous though spasmodic resolve to get there. The
drama was something like itself in places, in spite
of the irrelevant turnips that were cast down before
the players. It even bore up against the fact that,
when one of the two heroes wondered how he
should win his way to a woman’s heart, some
utterly irrelevant person handed him a large
black cat by way of nothing in particular.
No very earnest attention was paid to the
dressing of the period, and one fine old English
personage had a perfectly modern Sydney accent
which defied all disguise. Mr. Jones, as Mar-
low, did the nervous, crushed end of the character
rather well, but was giddy in a serious way. An
amateur company playing comedy usually does
the serious parts rather well, and only labors
heavily in the humorous spots.
** * *
The Vic. Operatic Society, which breaks
out every now and then with an amateur-profes-
sional performance, and takes care to do it re-
spectably, has ventured into Melbourne Princess’s
for a fortnight. On Saturday, the old fashioned
fable of “ Girofle-Girofla ” was revived. Lecocq’s
opera retains its early tunefulness, but the alleged
humor of its dialogue dates back to the British
pun-period, when people laughed on slight provo-
cation, and a little joke went a long way. These
times “ Girofle-Girofla ” leans heavily on the
music for support. Whenever the conductor’s
baton ceases from waggling and the fiddlers are at
rest a tired feeling pervades the front of the house.
The plot, which was probably new and
original long ago, is a very fragile
plot, anyhow. A stout, bald-headed Don,
has a wife who oppresses him, and twin-
daughters who have been respectively com-
mitted for trial in matrimony with a gentle tenor,
and a dusky heathen named Mourzook. The
twins are both the same girl for warbling pur-
poses, but the occasional introduction of a dumb
replica, very like the original girl as far as can be
judged from the back of her head and figure,
enables the one girl to sing in a dual capacity
with some appearance of being sometimes the
other girl who never opens her mouth. And inas-
much as one sister is said to have been borne
away by brigands previous to the arrival of Mour-
zook, the other relative gets married to that im-
patient calamity after becoming the tame tenor’s
bride. She does this to save the family honour,
or something, with the result that the f.h. looks
very shaky what time the turbulent dark gentle-
man is clamouring for his honeymoon. Yet, in
the light of modern civilisation, “ Girofle-Girofla ”
is not even improper. It is simply foolish, and
melodious.
• * * *
Miss Minnie Waugh, the strapping
brunette of the local concert platform, plays the
name-parts in a golden wig, and sings effectively,
as usual. Also, James Wade, the short tenor, is
a musical success. Moreover, James acts passa-
bly, although the soprano is a rather crude ama-
teur who can’t give him much assistance except in
their duets. Harry Ingham, as Mourzook, con-
tributes a strong, rich voice to the proceedings ;
George Leopold and Miss Hesford Angus are at
home in their parental characters; Miss Ida
Osborne is, of course, a valuable Paquita, and the
chorus voices are young and fresh. Take it alto-
gether, “Girofle-Girofla ” is a credit to conductor
Hanstein and t"he management of the Y. 0.5., but
they will probably find their other opera, “ Les
Cloches, ” a more payable show.
* * *
The last nights of Italian Opera at Her
Majesty’s are not getting all the encouragement
they deserve. “La Boheme” was given four
times last week, and the house that ought to have
been crowded every time was not more than half-
full. Puccini’s fascinating work will perhaps get
the ear of Melbourne when the company returns
and the weather is warm, which hope applies to
the whole repertoire of Williamson’s strong, dur-
able co. They supply a capital entertainment.
Their voices never grow weary. They have the
brightest scenery and the best-dressed chorus that
Melbourne has ever known in Italian opera, also_
the theatre is smarter under its new name than'
the Alexandra where Simonsen did big business
for one notable season in days of yore. But that
was a summer success. Public appreciation didn’t
suffer from cold feet.
* * * *
“Un Ballo in Maschera,” sweetest of
Verdi operas so far as concerns the second
and third acts, was played on Saturday.
Unfortunately, there are five acts and four
long opportunities for going out to see a
man, and the women, who haven’t got a
male friend waiting outside, would prefer fewer
intervals for meditation. All the same, “Un
Ballo ”is interesting. The plot is founded on a
comparatively virtuous Duke, a most uncommon
fact in romantic opera. The Duke loves his
Prime Minister’s wife, who is equally gone on the
amorous potentate, but their affection remains at
the platonic stage. Nothing but cold despair
would have come out of it if they hadn’t both gone
to see a witch. This leads to a discovery of certain
circumstances which look incriminating, where-
upon the lady’s husband joins a conspiracy—the
funny old Italian-opera conspiracy that always
stalks abroad in bird-scaring attire —to kill the
Duke. And when he himself has fatally jabbed
his royal master at a masked ball he learns the
innocent truth of the matter, and feels sorry for
his crime,
* • * *
“Un Ballo” is not one of the happiest
efforts of the present company. Larizza and
Signora Benedetto are strong as the Duke and the
lady, and Travaglini, although he lacks animation,
is a welcome chief conspirator for the sake
of his fine basso voice. Other parts are not
very well filled, The second-best conspirator
(Tondini) has no rumbling thunder in his chest,
and the important baritone character wants a
better voice than that of Cattadori, who has been
a weak spot in previous shows. And Signorina
Vaccariis, alas! not an admirable boy Osric in
more than one respect. Her singing will pass.
Her smile is the same joyous, youthful accom-
plishment that made her a proper Musetta in
“La Boheme.” But her knees are a solemn
warning to girls of theatrical aspirations. Seems
that “Rigoletto ” is to be produced ere the season
closes—on Saturday next at latest—with Signora
Sapio as the Fool’s daughter, and Dani as the
Duke who chortles his dissolute lay to the fair
moon. The dook in “ Rigoletto ”is one of the
wickedest.
** * *
Dampier’s latest Friday lapse into Shake-
speare, at Melbourne Bijou, is a praiseworthy
“Macbeth.” When Dampier gives his artistic
mind a chance to spread itself upon a play by One
Who Wasn’t Bacon, the result is always interesting.
The touch of the experienced actor can be seen in
every part of the show. Small details are worked
for more than their surface value, and unfamiliar
tricks of stage business strike one with their apt-
ness to the situation. Somebody who has a big
stock of old dramatic lore at his disposal must be
the prime mover in a respectable production of
any Shakespearian play. “ Macbeth,” as Dampier
puts it through, is decidedly more persuasive in its
main features than the “Macbeth’’which the Nance
O’Neil Co. perpetrated with better spectacular
effect. In his acting of the ghost-stricken Mac.
there are powerful moments. Lily Dampier is
also impressive in several places, and especially
when Mrs. M. walks in her sleep. Alfred Rolf e,
as Macduff, is at least stronger than Plimmer was.
Edmund Holloway plays King Duncan in his way,
and half-a-dozen minor characters are intelligently
filled. The aged monarch will no doubt be done
to death again this Friday. Meanwhile “Shamus
O’Brien ” is trying to rescue Ireland from the
oppressor, and on Saturday the last week of Dam-
pier’s season will be inaugurated with one more
melodrama.
#* * *
Walker and Ida May, who shine in the
reflected glory of Whelan’s imitation of them, are
the newest re-arrivals at Melb. Opera House.
The infant Claude Bain is there for the first time,
and the verdict on that small suggestion of his
father sounds favorable. Bain pbre has a depress-
ing influence on many people, although he achieves
some violently hysterical giggles from the female
portion of the audience, but when he gets born
again, as it were, in the flesh of Bain fils, the
apparition amounts to a joke. An awful joke.
Marie Lloyd is still appearing for a few nights
with more or less success, some of her songs being
not so bright as the others; also, Hurley continues
to coster. There are numerous costers in London,
but thus far only two of them have been brought
under Australian notice —the coster according to
Rickards and the sad-faced bloke depicted by
Lonnen and Hurley, and others like unto these
latter. When the eye of memory has reviewed
the long procession of costers it winks at H.
Rickards to intimate that he can annex the cake.
* * #
The reigning hippodrama at the Royal
promises to reign on for another week or two.
Probably the cheerful manner in which Baker
dissipates his capital on account of a misunder-
standing with Miss Ross helps to make “ A Million
of Money ” popular. Human nature in general is
glad to hear Baker protest that money doesn’t
bring happiness. The sentiment offers some little
consolation to the top gallery, whilst enabling the
bulky employer of labor to bemoan the circum-
stances that have placed him in a private box.
** * *
On Saturday afternoon (27th) Marshall
Hall gives another orchestral concert at Melb.
Town Hall. The programme is diversely German,
and the prices are 35., 25., and Is.
** * *
Edwin Geach, writing from London pre-
vious to Donald Macdonald’s appearance on the
platform there, said:
This crowded old city in 1901 is an impossible place, and
one cannot move a hundred yards in a day. lam busy
endeavoring to create a little interest in the show, but
fear the good time is gone—if there ever was a good time
for S’African War Lectures “ within sight of St. Paul’s.”
A week later, Geach wrote again :
After much hard work, we duly opened to a fair-paying
house, and played an actual London record lecture season
(three nights), and managed to pull through cheerfully, the
“ star” giving great satisfaction and getting as good notices
as the brains of the average London reporters will permit,
which is not saying much. But patriotism in England is
dead tired at present. They are sick of the war, and the
alleged love for things Australian is all moonshine —they
know nothing and care less about us. Mind you, they
had not heard a war story half as bright as Mac.’s, I’m
told. He could be boomed at a considerable cost of coin
and trouble. To sum up the situation Macdonald is good
enough for England, but England is not good enough for
him. The chances are we shall return to Australia via
Queensland, arriving in August.
*» * *
Geach’s sweeping contempt for the intel-
lectual capacity of Cockney journalism can’t have
been inspired by a paragraph in the London
Morning Leader, which somehow got on to his
Macdonald advt. in the Ceylon Standard. Re-
ferring to this rapturous summary of Mac.’s merits
the Leader man said : —•
He has been lecturing on the siege of Ladysmith, and
these delicate little compliments have been paid him by
his business manager. . . I wonder how many men
famous authors and doctors —went out to South Africa
simply and solely in the interests of self-advertisement.
They are all men of the hour, all brilliant and delightful
orators and raconteurs. Take this Ceylon Standard
advt. as a sample. It begins with this proud flourish :
“ The English can conquer anything but death.” And
how does it end? In this sound and sensible style:
“ Secure seats in advance.” There you have the touch of
a showman’s hand, and the sound of a voice that is sane.

The earnings are at the rate of £5 os. lOd. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds as at the opening of
the half-year (capital £1,000,000, reserves £1,031,661).
This is a high rate, yet not quite so good as the £5 Is. sd.
per cent, of six months ago. There is very little to
chronicle about the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney,
save its steady record of progress. During last half-year
deposits increased by £339,371, and now stand at
£11,557,615. Total liabilities to the public are £13,030,454;
and cash and liquid securities held against them are
£4,922,263— equal to nearly 38 per cent., of which
£1,441,670 is in State securities. As 30 per cent, is about
the average holding, the Commercial is unusually strong
in this respect. It has the second largest business among
the purely Australian banks, while in point of solidity
it can fairly rank, to all appearance, even with the great
Bank of New South Wales.
All the same it lias often occurred to the present
writer that such an institution as the Commercial Bank-
ing Co. might try a radical experiment with advantage.
It has £1,032,889 of reserves, £1,000,000 paid-up capital,
and an uncalled capital of £1,000,000. The uncalled
capital is a very remote liability while the Commercial’s
run of splendid good fortune lasts —still, it is a liability,
and even such a solid institution would be a
pleasanter thing for shareholders without it. If £500,000
of the reserve was transferred to capital, and the dividend
correspondingly reduced, it would still leave £532,889
of reserves, and it would diminish the share liability
one-half. And when reserves grew up again to £1,000,000
another similar transfer could be made, thus extinguish-
ing the share liability altogether. The Commercial of
Sydney is one of the few banks which could do such a
transaction without shaking public confidence in any way,
and it would certainly put the shareholders in a better
position. In the case of some banks a transaction of the

The Austral Otis Engineering Company have
now delivered one of the water-ends in connection with
their contract with the Melbourne and Metropolitan
Board of Works. The second water-end will be delivered
within a week or two. These water-ends are a very
massive piece of work and reflect credit on the makers.
They are made of a special mixture of iron, which was
subjected to severe tests before being put into the work
and gave highly satisfactory results. Each water-end
weighs about 70 tons.*

All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on up-
to-date lines include a number of Luhrig vanners. The
South and Junction companies each have about a dozen,
no slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Coolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, andall sorts of iron-work. *

Perseverance: 7270 tons for 12,2310z5. The
figures of tonnage and yields of W.A. mines, as tele-
graphed to the East, are usually hopelessly mixed up.
Take this as an example. The total is given as 7270 tons,
yet if the other figures of tonnage are correct the total
must be over 9000 tons. Anyhow, the yield is the largest
ever got in onq,month from the mine. Every effort made
to sweeten the shareholders at the annual meeting to be
held this month in London, yet the shares fell 10s. in spite
of it, and to-day are £4 lower than when the mine was
turning out much less gold. They are also less than half the
price they once reached—£l7—which conveys some idea
of the madness of people in London at one time in respect
to W.A. mines. They are not too sensible now. Time
has brought them to about half their senses. If this mine
was not encumbered with a tremendous debt when it
started its huge sulphide plant, how is it that no divs.
have been declared since ? Many months ago The Bul-
letin stated that the mine was over £lOO,OOO behind. Of
course it was denied. Since that time it has turned out
gold to the value of over £200,000, and has paid no div.,
and in the face of the highest return it ever had, within
a few days of the meeting, where the shareholders will
be told of large ore-reserves in sight, and of others not
in sight, shares are dropping in value. The only explana-
tion is that the debt when The Bulletin referred to it
was much greater than the estimate given, and that it
has taken the mine all this time to clear it off, and even
now no money remains for distribution. It may also be
taken for granted that nothing will be said at the meeting
of the fact that the lowest levels in the mine are the
poorest, and that with the many additions to the plant
and the enormous output, nearly all taken above the
500 ft. level, it will be impossible to keep up the present
production for any lengthened period. *

The City Bank of Sydney goes on its small,
quiet way with but little variation. Some results are as
follows :
’ Total
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
Dec., 1894 .. £10,004 5 per cent. = £lO,OOO £108,275
June, 1895 .. 10,030 6 „ „ 10,000 108,305
Dec., 1895 .. 10,020 5 „ „ 10,000 108,325
June, 1596 .. 8,108 4 „ „ 8,000 108,433
Dec., 1896 .. 8,070 4 „ „ 8,000 108,503
June, 1897 .. 8,049 4 „ „ 8,000 108,552
Dec., 1897 .. 8,395 4 „ „ 8,000 108,947
June, 1898 .. 8,273 4 ~ „ 8,000 109,220
Dec., 1898 .. 8,053 4 ~ „ 8,000 109,273
June, 1899 .. 8,070 4 „ „ 8,000 109,343
Dec., 1899 .. 8,212 4 „ „ 8,000 109,555
June, 1900 .. 8,041 4 „ „ 8,000 109,596
Dec., 1900 .. 8,223 4 ~ „ 8,000 109,819
June, 1901 .. 8,105 4 ~ „ 8,000 109,924
The earnings represent £3 3s. 7d. per cent, per annum on
the shareholders’ funds —a very low rate, even in these
times. Judging by the continued painful exactitude of
the published earnings it may be presumed that the City
Bank still continues the process alluded to some years ago
by one of the directors—that it only shows enough profit
to pay a small dividend, and applies the remainder,
whatever it may be, to gradually write off deprecia-
tions, the extent of which is also unknown. Un-
fortunately there was never anything to show whether
the amount it was considered necessary to provide was
£3OOO or £lOO,OOO, or any other sum, or whether the bal-
ance of profits applied towards making up this unspecified
amount each half-year averaged £lOO or £2OOO, or any
other amount in particular. Therefore it is impossible to
say when the City Bank may be expected to emerge from
the period of small earnings and 4 per cent, dividends.
During the last half-year the management has wisely in-
creased its holding of State securities by £25,000 ; this
item now amounts to £50,000. The total liquid securities
stand at £342,272, equal to over 29 per cent, of all
liabilities to the public. Otherwise the business remains
about stationary. Since the great drop in 1892 and 1893
the City Bank’s “ deposits and other liabilities” have re-
mained so consistently stationary at a little over a million
that the fluctuation has never been more than £lOO,OOO
either _way. And in 6j years the visible reserves have
only increased by £1649. In almost every respect
stationariness appears to be the City Bank’s strong point.
* * * »
The Bank of Victoria:—
The earnings still maintain their steady increase, and are
the best recorded since 1893. They represent £3 17s. lid.
per cent, per annum on the shareholders’ funds as at be-
ginning of half-year, as compared with £3 15s. Id. per
cent, for previous half-year, which is a very material im-
proved vit. The business has been well maintained ; de-
posits show a slight increase ; and reserve of cash and
liquid securities is £1,638,011 —equal to rather over 29 per
cent, of all liabilities to other than shareholders, which
may be regarded as sufficient for all purposes. But
despite the general prosperity of the institution, there
is still room for doubt whether it wouldn’t be
much wiser to revert to the old 2J per cent, dividend to
ordinary shareholders. The addition made to the
reserves is £5061 this half-year, and £3840 last half, and,
unless the profits increase materially without any cor-
responding expansion of dividends, the process of re-
building the reserves won’t go on nearly fast enough to
constitute an adequate preparation for next panic year,
There is certain to be a next panic year, sooner or later,
and a large institution which only strengthens its posi-
tion to the extent of £B9Ol per annum isn’t building itself
up very fast.
* * * •
The Commercial Bank of Australia (or rather
that section of the institution of which the accounts are
published, as distinguished from the Assets Trust, which
is equally a part of the bank, but of which the accounts
are not visible to the ordinary eye), shows better results
than for any June half-year since 1892. Of course, as
there is no information issued to the public to show
whether the Assets Trust (which represents over £3,300,000
of the bank’s liabilities) has made £lO,OOO profit or
£40,000 loss for the half-year, it is impossible to say how
things really stand, but the outward and visible course of
events has been as follows :
Assuming that the Assets Trust Co. which is owned by
the bank, has exactly paid its way during the half-year
(about which the writer knows of no available evidence),
the earnings of the Commercial Bank are at the rate of
about £4 os. sd. per cent, on the shareholders’ funds as at
the beginning of the half-year. In that case the Commer-
cial has done moderately well. On the other hand, if the
£30,000 transferred to the Assets Trust was all required
to enable that institution to pay interest on its deposits,
then the Commercial Bank of Australia has only earned
£2 3s. 3d. per cent., in which case it would have done
very badly, and there would be reason to suspect an ulti-
mate necessity for another heavy writing down. The in-
teresting feature of the published balance-sheet is that it
doesn’t throw much more light on the earnings of the
whole Commercial Bank than it does on the Chinese
surplus.
During the half-year the visible part of the
Commercial Bank of Australia has progressed on the
whole. Of its reconstructed deposits another £316,829
has been paid off, leaving only £645,989 still due. As
the total deposits have diminished by only £279,651, it is
evident that some advance has been made. Cash and
liquid securities (£1,199,732) are £327,214 less than they
were six months ago, as the natural result of this repay-
ment, but they still represent over 29 per cent, of all
liabilities to the public. The position of the visible part
of the Commercial Bank of Australia is very solid, and it
has the magnificent paid-up capital of £3,250,821 —the
largest paid-up banking capital in Australasia. Also,
when capital and reserves are all included, the Commer-
cial of Australia only comes behind one other institution
—the Bank of N.S.W. —in total resources. Even if all
the assets of its Assets Trust went wrong—an absolutely
impossible supposition, of course—it would still be solvent
so far as its depositors are concerned, however bad things
might be for the shareholders.
As regards the Assets Trust there is, so far as
the writer is aware, no information to show what the
earnings, expenditure, profit or loss, or amount of assets
still on hand may be. What progress is made at realising
the assets is a mystery. What cash is on hand is a
mystery. What the Trust may be making by repurchase
of its deposits is a mystery. The £60,000 a year paid to
the Trust by the bank may be sufficient or insufficient or
more than sufficient to cover losses—there is nothing to
show. The signs may be that in the end the Trust will
wind up with a big surplus that will go a long way to
replace the bank’s lost reserves, or will wind up with a
loss that will eat up most of the bank’s ordinary capital—
the published balance-sheet doesn’t explain. All that can
be gathered is that the deposits of the Assets Trust are
being repaid at this rate :
Half-year to Half-year to
June, 1897 .. £27,907 December, 1899.. £20,682
December, 1897.. 41,949 June, 1900 .. 12,715
June, 1898 .. 45,474 December, 1900.. 25,138
December, 1898.. 56,591 June, 1901 .. 30,606
June, 1899 .. 26,525
Therefore in 4J years the Commercial Bank’s Assets Trust
has paid off only £287,587 of its reconstructed deposits,
reducing them from £3,642,001 to £3,354,414. During
the same period the bank has assisted the Assets Trust by
transferring £293,000 from its profits. Judging by these
proceedings, the process of realising the Trust’s assets
is exceedingly slow, which doesn’t suggest any too well
for the assets. If this rate of progress is maintained, the
final cleaning-up won’t come till 1950. All the Trust’s
deposits fall due in 1916, so there must be an intention to
accelerate things by-and-bye, when the market improves.
Meanwhile all this mystery is bad for the institution.
Also it is a wildly absurd reflection on the Vie. Banking
Act, and suggests an urgent need to fix the Federal
Banking Act very differently. Apparently there is
nothing to prevent a bank putting £11,500,000 out of
£11,600,000 liabilities and assets into an allegedly separate
co. and thereafter publishing no information about them,
and even refusing to say why it publishes no information.
If Gilbert had only written an opera about a Japanese
bank run on these lines it might have amused the public
a good deal, though the idea that it was possible to really
run a bank on these lines [would never have occurred to
the public.
* * * *
The management of the Royal Bank of Queens-
land has a scheme on hand for the enlargement of the
institution. At present Queensland has three banks. The
Queensland National Bank has no paid-up capital at all
if its accounts are put on a logical basis, and it lives on
the concessions of its creditors. Its nominal paid-up
capital (£412,922) and nominal reserve (£21,000) aren’t
nearly sufficient to make good the shortage in its deposits.
(The shortage that is being paid out of earnings.) The
Bank of North Queensland has only £lOO,OOO capital and
£11,860 reserves, so it is but a small concern. The
Royal, with £385,187 capital and £55,402 reserves, is the
largest of the three, but it is also a small concern. There
is room in Queensland for a large local bank which doesn’t
give unsecured overdrafts to shady politicians, to take
up the place once held by the fallen and discredited
Q.N.8., but the Royal requires much more capital to fill
the position. It is proposed, at the end of this year, to
pay off the remaining £45,915 of its reconstructed
deposits. It is also proposed to call up another £1 per
share, which will provide £70,034, and make the paid-up
capital £455,221. The £9 shares will then be £6 10s.
paid up.
With a view to raising still more money with
which to extend the business there are these alterna-
tives : The remaining £2 10s. per share may be called up,
but this would only yield £175,085 at the best, and
Queensland shareholders are none too well able to pay
calls just now. Or there may be a fresh issue of ordinary
shares, but the present dividend is only 2£ per cent, and
the ordinary shareß, £5 10s. paid up, are selling at about
£3. Outsiders would not take up at par ordinary shares
which they can buy in the open market at a heavy dis-
count ; and if the present shareholders took them up
it would be just the same as making a call. The
management, therefore, proposes to issue £300,000 worth
of preference capital (30,000 £lO shares) with a first claim
on dividends up to 3J per cent. If the bank increased its
available funds by getting £300,000 more fixed deposits
(which it probably could get if it looked for them ener-
getically) they would also cost 3J per cent., and it would
be necessary to keep a cash reserve against them in case
of large withdrawals at maturity, so they would cost
more than the preference capital would, and have just
the same preference as against the ordinary shareholder.
The issue of preference capital appears to be the best
possible idea in the circumstances.
* * * *
Progress of the Queensland National Bank since
its last reconstruction :
The nominal net profit (£12,000, after writing off £16,350)
is equal to £5 11s. sd. per cent, per annum on the nominal
capital and reserves (£430,922) with which the half-year
commenced. But everything (including the reserve-fund
of £21,000) is so nominal about this institution that these
are matters of small account.
• * • •
The balance-sheet of the Queensland National
Bank is really only a hilarious joke, and doesn’t even pro-
fess to give reliable information about the bank’s posi-
tion. If it showed the position absolutely there would be
no capital and no reserve-fund, and an enormous shortage
as well. The principal troubles are these : In 1897 one-
fourth of the private deposits were written-off and ceased
to bear interest, and were removed out of the balance-
sheet, and the amount thus provided was utilised to write
off lost assets with. But this immense sum is to be paid
out of profits, so it remains a liability in this sense that
until it is liquidated the shareholders can get no divi-
dends. The amount was never accurately stated, but it
was apparently about £1,038,000, and in four years
£42,000 has been provided for. A sum of about £458,000
of Government deposits also ceased to bear interest and
was supposed to be nominally written off (yet to be
repaid out of profits—amount repaid to date, £21,000)
in order to allow of writing off another £468,000
of losses. But as this £458,000 was never even
nominally written off, but remained in the balance-
sheet, it is presumed that the £458,000 of dubious
assets continued to appear as good in order to square the
accounts. Then, in addition to the £21,000 repaid to
Government and £42,000 to private depositors in the
past four years, £106,445 has been provided to write-off
unspecified losses, as to amount of which there is no reli-
able information whatever. Apparently this has some-
thing to do with alleged assets called “ Private Ledger
Accounts ; Debts in Suspense pending realisation of
Securities.” These now stand at £617,898, and it seems
possible that when they are all cleared off, and the
£990,000 or so due to private depositors (which doesn’t
appear among the liabilities) is paid, the Q.N. Bank will
be about right, unless there are other losses, or unless
further losses are made in the meantime.
At December, 1897, the shadowy “suspense
account ” asset stood at £742,835. Since then it has been ■
reduced by these amounts :
June, 1898 .. £ll,BlO June, 1900 .. £17,925
December, 1898 20,013 December,fL9oo 21,166
June, 1899 .. 16,000 June, 1901 .. 23,036
December, 1899 14,987
This spectral asset now stands at £617,898, a sum of
£124,937 having been written off. Presumably the
£106,415 written off out of earnings has mostly gone to
this account, at which rate the assets in suspense are
doing very little for themselves by process of realisation.
A balance-sheet like this is a shrieking burlesque. If the
object of a balance-sheet is (as commonly supposed) to
show the position of a bank, and not to conceal it, the
“ debts in suspense ” and all other dubious items should
be written down to bed-rock, the amounts nominally
written off the private deposits should be restored to
their place among the liabilities, and the total deficit
should be put in plain figures among the assets. Then
the whole amount by which the deficit is reduced would
be deducted in each half-yearly statement, and the exact
progress of the institution on its way back to solvency
would become apparent. Also, the £250,000 or so of
uncalled capital should be called up; all the capital,
except a nominal amount, written off in reduction of the
shortage ; and the absurd pretence of putting money to
a reserve fund when there is still a huge deficit should be
abandoned, and the reserve also written off. By some
such methods as these the Queensland National Bank
might be put on its feet again before the present gener-
ation is quite dead.

The Trustees, Executors and Agency 00. is slowly build-
ing up its reserves again, but the process is very gradual;
also the decline in the profits as compared with the two
previous half-years is disappointing. Further, the co.,
in its list of assets, values 2800 A.D. and M. Bank prefer-
ence shares at £2BOO and £14,000 worth of A.D. and M.
deposits at £l4,ooo—total £16,800, whereas the selling
value of the lot at present quotations is only about
£14,800. Though these securities will almost certainly
be ultimately worth more than the values set against
them, on present values the reserves are really about
£2OOO less than even the small sum which appears on
paper.
Despite fluctuations, however, the Trustees,
Executors and Agency 00. appears to be making a little
headway. Two continuous troubles are that it holds a
leasehold which, during the last 12 months, cost £550
more in rent than it produced ; also that it has £20,000
mortgage on its premises, and £21,400 invested, mostly in
the luckless A.D. and M. Bank, to meet the liability when
it falls due, and last year the interest going out on the
£20,000 owing exceeded by £349 the amount which the
£21,400 investment brought in. These two disastrous
speculations have been a drain on the co. for a good many
years, and they absorb about one-seventh of last year’s
profits. The other investments, mostly State securities
and mortgages, yield about 4 per cent, on their face value
and appear sufficiently solid. But the earnings don’t by
any means justify the 5 per cent, dividend. Out of the 13
trustee co.’s of Australia six certainly pay a higher divi-
dend, extending up to 8 per cent., but that is another
story. A co. with £90,000 paid-up capital, and, on the
most favorable computation, only £BB5B of reserves, is a
very weak concern to run a trustee business in which it
has charge of over £6,380,000 of other people’s property.
And at the present rate of progress it will take a very long
time to build itself up into a solid concern again.
* * * •
Overflow Mines, N.S.W. : Another booming
report: “ The brickwork is being completed, and the
bricks are of excellent quality ; the ironwork has all
arrived for the waterjacket, also in good condition ; the
lead and copper refiners are going up, also ore-bins, and
everything is promised to be ready for blowing in about
two or three months.” Up to date, alas ! it has been
mostly blowing-out with the Overflow. A good mine that
has suffered from bad management, and the writer is
very doubtful if the present is much better.
* * * •
N.S.W. gold mines: “ Grassy Gully ” buyers,
2s ; sellers, 4s. What has become of this mine ? Something
is shut up—either the management or the mine. Which
is it ? Mount David : buyers, Id. ; sellers, 3d. No busi-
ness-margin too wide. This co. is going into reconstruc-
tion. Liquidation would be much better; it has been
proved too poor to pay. Some people still have faith in
it but that doesn’t produce enough gold. North Bushy
Hill: seller, 2s. 9d. ; no buyer. If they were offered at
that per doz. business would not eventuate. Peak Hill :
buyers, 2s. ; sellers, 2s. 9d. No biz. Many others have
dropped out altogether. N.S.W. is not a gold-mining
country.
* * » *
N.S.W. dredges: Turon No. 1,15 oz. for a week;
Turon No. 2,140 z. for a week—so it is quite evident that
neither has got into “ the good ground around the
corner,” so often talked of by the good old Sabbath tip-
ster. Most dredges, according to newspapers, are ap-
proaching better ground, but never seem to arrive there ;
and, as to gold-reefs, they are always widening and
getting richer with depth. Dredges nearly all seem
somehow to start on the very worst ground. Araluen
Proprietary, 220 z. ; Araluen Central, 340 z.; Oolumbo,
16oz. There are 15 dredges on the Shoalhaven River and
its tributaries. Three or four are paying, half of them
are not; several are closed down, and others soon will be.
* * * •
North Mt. Lyell, Tas. : Owing to the inability
of certain co.’s to bear their proportion of financial re-
sponsibility connected with the erection of the smelting-
plant, the whole expense has now to be borne by the
North Lyell Co. The Board is satisfied that “ the under-
taking will ultimately prove profitable, but no divs.
need be expected until the furnaces are paid for, and in
operation.” Is it understood then, that out of the shipping
of thousands of tons of high-grade ore, and the smelting
of a much greater quantity at Mt. Lyell, nothing is left
to pay for the furnaces ?
* * * *
Vie. gold production for first half of year shows
a decrease of 12,7550 z,, attributable to the falling-off in
the returns from the S. German Long Tunnel, Long
Tunnel Extended, South Glenfine, Garden Gully, and
other mines.
* * * *
Burbanks, a few miles south of Coolgardie:
May return was the record—2o37oz. That of last month
not yet known in Australia, but writer is advised that it
will be equal to this. Mine looks as well as it has done
for years, and Prof. Nicholas, who has had control of this
show since it was opened—first for the old Melb. co. and
then for the English crowd that took it over—is now re-
linquishing the general management. It has been an
anxious mine to work and develop, the reefs being small,
erratic, and in very hard rock. He has done his best to
regulate the output, and has succeeded right through as
well as any other man in W.A., and having made the
mine a success he proposes now to devote all his time
once more to consulting mining-engineer work.

Adelaide has again made the Duke blush.
Kissy Ware did the deed on the first occasion.
The second performance was at the review, where
a gin-case (neatly covered witli red cloth, if you
please !) was placed by the royal charger by some
patriotic official to enable His Royal Shortness to
mount. He ignored the step, however, and
got into the saddle unaided.
* ■ * *
At the Adelaide review, when Royalty left
the pavilion, men and women dashed in to sit in
the chair that the Duchess had occupied. Also
when she planted a tree in the Botanical Gardens
they fought for chunks of the mould which had
been removed in the process and kissed the handle
of the spade that had been used ! They would
have drunk the water in which she washed her
hands. Truly a loyal people !
* * * *
The pea-hens which the Duke of York shot
at Buckland Park, near Adelaide, were fed with
wheat on the morning of the visitation, and were
then driven down into the orangery. It was
really like asking a man into the poultry-yard,
or into a zoological garden enclosure, to invite a

There was dismay among the drivers
attached to what is perhaps the largest livery-
stable in Adelaide whin the order went forth that
they were all to be clean-shaven during the visit
of the Yorks. One man, who had spent half a
lifetime in cultivating a fine beard, was in despair,
It was only the possession of a wife and many
children that induced him to obey. Also, it was
a barberous command to issue in the depths of
winter
♦ * * *
Adelaide’s capacity for Gor’ Save aston-
ished the Jook. On his arrival at the Schools’
display the children sang one verse of the Han-
them, and he doffed his hat and prepared
to sit right royally, but 5000 juvenile voices got on
to the second verse, and promptly brought him to
attention. At the end of this verse the Jook, feel-
ing secure, again tried to seek repose, but it was
ncT go. The youngsters waded into the third
verse. At the end of this York remained stand-
ing, wondering, no doubt, if S.A. had invented
any additions to the national agony,
» * * •
Mayor Ware of Adelaide has absolutely no
luck in the Royal presence. When the Yorks
arrived at the Town Hall His Worship would not
speak till the band stopped playing “God Save.”
The Jookandutch gazed up him with wild-eyed
apprehension wondering wuat surprise he was
going to spring on them this time. As it was,
York (toothache-tortured) refused to leave his
carriage.
* * * *
The worst that happened 'to the Duke in
S. Aus. was the Register’s treasonable
publication of a fac simile of a Royal message
sent per pigeon from Buckland Park to Adelaide :
“We only got about 25 ducks this morning, ex-
pect to get some peacocks this afternoon.” The
“fist” is a schoolboy’s ; the punctuation and the
composition are shocking. The Advertiser did
what it could short of suppressing the Sacred
Screed ; it didn’t expose the character, or lack of
it, in the handwriting, and it fixed up the Prince’s
punctuation.
* • • *
The hanging-up of the Ophir outside Port
Adelaide because the captain would not then
risk taking her up the creek which they call
the Port River, has set some Adelaide people
clamoring again for an outer harbor. Close upon
£900,000 has been spent on the dredging of the
creek, and the expense will have to go on. As
usual they might get harbor works if they could
agree on a site, but they can’t—some want them
at Largs Bay, where the mail steamers now
anchor ; others want them at Marino, further
down the gulf. Curiously, S. Aus. has practically
only one other port where any shipping worth
mentioning calls. That is Port Pirie, only a few
miles north of Adelaide. Port Augusta, though
deserted, has many natural advantages. But
vested interests are mighty,

The Jook presented Col. Madley, S.A.
Police Commissioner, with autograph photos of
himself and his merry duchess. Two days later
he did the very same to the mounted constable
who had been on duty at Govt. House. This, no
doubt, has given the facetious Madley much food
for thought. Some years ago, when Madley left
their ranks the school teachers rose as one man
and presented him with his own shadow. Madley
retaliated by lecturing them for an hour on some-
thing or other m a tone of voice like unto that
assumed by Geo. Reid when about to bait Charlie
Kingston. , . .

They were so anxious at Adelaide about
the Duke’s safety that a reputable citizen, who
happened at 2 o’clock one morning to walk past
Govt House gates, was challenged by the sentry
and had a rifle presented at . him because lie
ventured to walk on the inside instead of the out-

An applicant for an old age pension called
at the Sydney office last week for his first pay-
ment. On being told that his first payment was
not due until the first week in August, he repre-
sented that he wanted it before then. He ex-
plained that he was 72 years of age, and quite
unable to work for a living. His wife had pre-
sented him that morning with a son and heir, and
he sorely needed the pension-money to provide
necessaries in connection with the event.
* * * *
An old Ballaratarian, over 70, has been
sent to gaol for working a fraud on the Old Age
Pension scheme whereby he drew two pensions.
He had only drawn the dual allowance once when
caught. He had a record for minor offences, but
two years is an outrageous penalty for his fraud,
and seems to arise from the opinion of Superior
Persons that the old age pensioner should be Put
Down.

Dear Bulletin.—l have seen much in the
newspapers lately re old age pensioners in this
State (Vic.), being found under the blighting in-
fluence of Drink, and the same influence has been
brought to bear to deprive several old people of
their allowances under the Act. What amazes me is
this : I know quite a number of civil service pen-
sioners who get pickled with unfailing regularity.
My neighbor on the right is a C.S. pensioner, and
“he won’t go home till morning” every sedbnd
day. Will you please publish the names and
addresses of these people and the dates and nature
of their drunks if I supply the facts ? And will you
please ascertain why the pensioners in question
are not se’verely admonished by purple Justices in
language flavored with whisky and the odour of
sanctity ? There seems to be some kind of an
anomaly knocking round here, and I’m anxious to
place it. Yours, C.M.G.

Fully a hundred bank-clerks bet ween 20
and 30 years of age in Sydney and suburbs are
paid less than the minimum 7s. a day.
* * * *
“ V.” : Be the lack of effective fire-escapes.
Hordern’s big building was a haven of safety
compared to some others. Know of a large
Sydney warehouse six storeys high where the
staircase runs up but to the second floor, the five
higher floors being reached by the lift only. In
the event of a fire on either the first or second
floors, the lift, acting as a flue, would probably
be the first thing to become unmanageable, so that
the half-a-hundred “hands” on the top floors
would meet a fate which makes one shudder to
think of. Isn’t it about time the builders of these
edifices were compelled to provide proper protec-
tion ? Also, re the amount of senseless abuse that
has been showered on the policeman who pre-
vented the tarpaulin being held for Clegg to jump
into at Hordern’s fire. He was quite justified in
doing so, experts agreeing that Clegg would have
landed therein as a dead weight of six tons.
Fancy a dozen men with a tarpaulin catching six
tons!
* » * •
Sydney’s recent three big fires teach a few
lessons. The victims of the Hordern blaze were
certainly sacrificed to the economising of ground-
space by piling storey upon storey till it becomes
practically impossible to fight a fire which has
once secured a hold, or to rescue persons from the
upper floors. Quite hopeless, by-the-way, was the
idea of saving poor Clegg’s life by getting him to
jump into a tarpaulin. From that height—l3o to
140 feet—he would fall in three seconds, and
strike said tarpaulin with a velocity of 80 feet per
second (equal, if he was a 10-stone man, to a
momentum of 5 foot-tons.
1 sec. 16 16
2 „ 16 x3= 48
3 „ 16 X 5= 80
144
The use of rockets in such cases might possibly
be effective if the rocket-apparatus were on the
spot, but, at best, rockets are extremely erratic in
their flight, and it is a nice problem in gunnery as
to whether a rocket could be thrown over a build-
ing (say) 140 feet high, from the other side of a
street (say) 2 chains wide. The path of the rocket
would be a parabolic curve, and the “ line of
sight ” would be a tangent to it making an angle
of probably 75° with the horizon. Again, if the
wind were blowing across the rocket’s path, the
missile might carry the line very wide of its mark.
On the whole the “mortar appliance” seems
preferable, since the shell carrying the line is
smaller and heavier, and therefore less liable to be
deflected than the rooket.

One of the sights at Hordern’s fire \va; a
little man with a redundant corporation rushing
around with a slab of burning material fast on his
beautiful tall hat, which'was beginning to smoke
nicely. Everybody carefully refrained from telling
him about it till he passed a fireman whose pro-
fessional instincts overcame his sense of humor,
and he promptly turned the 140 to the square-inch
hose upon the peripatetic conflagration, and
washed the little man clean out of sight.
NOT IN HIS SOUL.
THE LADY'S HACK: “ I saw a poor hawse just
now with a monstrous load on him, Maudie,
dear; and I spoke to his driver and rebuked
him. ”
THE LADY: “ And did he ease his load, dear?’,
THE HACK: “ Oh, no! He just looked at me a
minute, and said: ‘ Gerrout, you big, fat
chump.' Now, I am NOT very big or very
fat, am I, Maudie, dear?”
During his visit to M.L. the Jook*was to
have traversed the province's show railway line
—the Rimutaka incline; but owing to bad
weather and consequent fear of slips? on the hill,
the trip was abandoned. It would never do for
Maorilaud to have killed royalty. The many
thousands of people who use this mountain-line
every year do not, of course, matter, but the
sooner the line is deviated, or closed, the better.
It is not an engineering triumph—it is an example
of brute strength in a mechanical form, and if
that strength failed, as it easily might do, up
would go the price of coffins. By means of power-
ful machinery gripping a centre rail the Fell
engines—always two to a train, usually more —lift
huge excursion trains 1000 ft. in eight miles, and
take about 40 minutes to do it—it all depends on
the state of the weather. At some points the
ferns on the cliffs brush the carriage- windows, and
the havoc a land-slide would work on a train is
easily imagined, especially if the drop on t’other
side is taken into consideration. Had the
engineer who built the line, by ingenious twisting
and tunnelling, secured a fairly reasonable grade,
the work would deserve praise; but, as it is, it is not
only extiemely dangerous, but involves a crimiml
waste of power. The noise caused by the exhaust
steam of several Fell engines is somethii g
hideous, and on a dark night, with the furrace
light reddening the steam and smoke and the
black mountain peaks towering above, a stranger
might easily wonder what he had struck. A
couple of brake-vans accompany each engine.
The brake being composed of two iron shots
clutching the centre rail, a blaze of light is visible
even in daylight, and the shoes are worn out in
one trip. Taking into account the fact that two
passenger-trains each way, and perhaps a dozen or
more freight-trains are hauled up and lowerel
down that awful hill daily, it’s no wonder that
those eight miles are reckoned in the train-rates
as 24.

Dear Bulletin. —Hard to imagine a grosser
piece of military injustice than the refusal of
S.A.’s monocled haw-haw commandant to confirm
to De Passey his lieutenancy —fairly earned upon
active service in S’Africa. De Passey showed
himself a man all the way up, but now it seen is
he’s not so ornamental as his gilded superior
thinks necessary for commissioned rank. S.A.
is about the only State that would stand such a
piece of howling snobbery.—A.B.T.
An enthusiast ic Ballarater struck the bed-
rock of bathos during the recent jookfooling in
that city. In a wail to the local papers he
deplored the fact that the Jook passed the Sturt-
street statue of his late grandma “ without taking
the slightest notice of it ” —which dreadful occur-
rence he attributed to the refusal of the councillors
to accept a former suggestion of his own, viz., that
said statue should be draped in mourning on the
occasion of the Royal visit. His idea, he proudly
explained, was that the procession, when passing
the statue, should halt, and the various performers
therein should get out of their vehicles and
“ stand reverently around for a while.” Impres-
sive pictui'e—a drove of fat aldermen “standing
reverently around” and “sayin’ nuffin” till they
got tired of it.
THE GULF BETWEEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ The Day is white,”
The black Night said ;
“And I grow sad
And would be wed.
Heart sick am I
For bridal-bed.
“ Times past it seem’d
The gulf between
More narrow grew ;
And stars have been
Beside the sun
At midday seen.
“It may not be.
Night cannot wed
The bright white Day.
Yet, all hope dead.
Heart-sick am I
For bridal-bed.”

The latest edition of the lover who goes
crazy because he cannot have the girl who serves
out the drinks all for his very own, appeared sud-
denly in the dress-circle bar of Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) last Saturday night. There he flourished
his revolver, as the baffled pirate used to wave his
sword before he vanished in the pale moonlight,
exclaimed tragically, “ If I can’t have you, no one
else will! ” and fired three shots at Hebe Jenkins.
One bullet passed through the side of her head,
one penetrated below the breast, and the other
struck the other Hebe somewhere in the leg.
Interviewed by the Telegraph the woman in the
hospital said: “Beale” (the man under arrest)
“is an old sweetheart of mine, but I have other
enemies who might have fired the shots ; and con-
sidering I did not see the shots fired, I could not
possibly say who is the man.” “ Other enemies
who might have fired the shots ! ” More baffled
competitors for the lily-white hand which serves
out the drinks !
* * * *
No sooner had Findley been expelled than
pretty well every third back-block Council in
Victoria resol u ted a cordial commendation of
Peacock’s “backbone.” Not one in a thousand
of these mediocrities had read the Tocsin article,
which had an exceedingly small circulation at best.
Writer has met dozens of Melbourne men of the
bell-topper and frock-coated class in the last few
days, not one of whom had the slightest idea of
the alleged libel. Most of them had never heard
of either the Irish People or the Tocsin. But all
these respectable gentry were quite solid that
Findley was a scurrilous, disgraceful libeller, who
had said “something,” and a man to be kept out
of the House at all hazards. What hypocritical
frauds these Jingoes are !
* * * *
N.S.W. chess championship is being
played for the first time this month. Eleven com-
petitors entered, including Jacobsen and Wallace,
two ex-champions of Australasia. The issue was
supposed to rest between them, but owing to the
latter unaccountably losing all his form, the tour-
ney has been shorn of much of its interest.. At
the end of round 4 Wallace had not won a single
game. Jacobsen should win the event comfort-
ably.
* * * *
Another Melbourne victim of plenty to eat
and little to do was Colonel Kent Johnston, sec.
of the Australian Club. Seems that recently
there had been a club stock-taking, and because a
few forks and spoons were missed the Colonel,
“very sensitive,” and “very lame from gout,”
got it on his mind that he would be discharged for
gross negligence or something. So he wrote a
farewell letter, denouncing himself as a coward,
and then made his exit with a dose of cyanide
of potassium. A nice, clean means of departing

* * * *
A much bigger job than was expected will
be the removal of the remains from the old
Devonsh ire-street cemetery, Sydney. In order to
avoid the possible overlooking of any remnants of
mortality, every foot of the ground is to be turned
over; and it is expected that, in addition to the
GOOO bodies already removed, at least 50,000 will
yet have to be shifted to the new cemetery at La
Perouse, to which a special tram has been con-
structed for the purpose.
From the Australasian Medical Ga-
zette :
Diphtheria in N.S.W., Victoria, and Queensland shows
a death-rate in the four years preceding the introduction
of antitoxin of 44’4 per 100,000 of the mean population ;
while in the four years after its introduction the rate was
only 18'7 per 100,000. On a population of nearly
3,000,000, this represents a saving of about 3000 lives.
In 1875 diphtheria killed 1 in 2808, and last year only 1
in 11,054.
This one item is a partial answer to the question:
“Is the community any better for the advances of
medicine ? ” On the other hand, the deaths from
cancer have, awful to relate, increased from 1 in
4421 in 1875 to 1 in 1685 last year !
* * * *
A feat suggestive of Jeanie Deans has been
performed by two nice-looking little Invercargill
(M.L.) boys (sons of a dustman) who ran away from
home to see the Dook. They walked nearly 200
miles, took short cuts through one or more railway
tunnels, lived on turnips, and slept in haystacks !
Arriving in Dunedin with no assets but two pair
of sore feet, they were taken over by the admiring
city fathers, who boarded them in an hotel free,
appointed a nurse and matron to cure the injured
extremities, and finally clothed them in new white
flannels and drove them in a hansom to see
the Dook. The Royal pair praised and head-
patted them and otherwise behaved in a way cal-
culated to make other small children annoy and
harass their parents by running away to see
foreign visitors. They are so celebrated by this
time that their father will probably be afraid to
spank them when they get home. It will seem
almost as sacrilegious as spanking the Duke him-
self, or the Archbishop of Canterbury.
* * * *
The China Inland Mission (Protestant),
which owned 58 of the 134 missionaries killed in
the late outbreak, declines to seek compensation.
That is good. The man who takes religion to a
people takes his chances. If the people don’t
want him, and kill or eat him, that’s in the office.
* * * *
Maoriland has a ‘‘ universal ” penny post-
age system now, but the fly in the ointment is
the refusal of Victoria to accept M.L. letters
bearing only a Id. stamp. It is apparently
done out of pure cussedness, for each State keeps
its own postages, and if M.L. is content with a
penny it seems to be exclusively its own affair.
But Vic. post office made quite a little revenue by
levying 2d. fine on the recipients of all such letters
for “deficient postage.” The difficulty has now
been surmounted. The Maorilander posts his
letter for Melbourne with a penny stamp on it,
and the Government sticks on another stamp at
its own expense. It only costs the Government
the paper and gum.

A 17-year-old damsel was lately before
Ballarat Bench for vagrancy and, while awaiting
trial, her fancy Chow brought her a toothsome
breakfast, after enjoying which she rewarded
him with kisses and hugs to the great scan-
dal of the blushful beholding bobbies. Ques-
tioned by one constable as to “ why the divil she
took-up wid a dirty Chinaman ? ” she nearly
paralysed him by remarking “that she’d rather
marry a Chinaman than a copper any day ! ” It is
understood that when the young lady has per-
formed her allotted “ moon ” she will become the
bride of the aforesaid slant-eyed idolator.

FOND AUNTIE (who has just been telling a fairy-tale about a mermaid):
“And now, Billy, what do you see in the water? ”
LITTLE BILLIE : “Barmaids.”
The N.S.W. Imperial “ Bushmen’s ”
Welcome Home to Sydney was reported in
Saturday’s newspapers as a most enthusiastic
success. As a matter of fact it was a dreadfully
tame affair ; livening up at the tail end under the
influence of beer and lemonade. The Mayor and
the Premier both spoke to the reporters—they
were so uninteresting in their hideously stereotyped
remarks about Empiah and spilt blood as to be
inaudible to the inattentive audiences. Colonel
Mackay’s reception by his men was an intensely
cool demonstration. Lieut.-Gov. Darley was
whole-heartedly cheered when he rose to speak,
and smilingly went through the hand-
shaking with the 400 warriors who returned to
receive the half-crown medal. Thereafter the
thirsty alleged bushies retired to the refreshment
booths under the -galleries, and after liberal beers
began throwing biscuits and oranges to longing
maidens giggling in the gallery. Noise increased
in proportion to the imbibition of beer; and when
the musical programme was announced only three
artists had the courage to face the din. Miss
Fanny Walker warbled complacently and in-
audibly while the warriors chucked oranges to the
gallery girls. Laurence Campbell caine out, and
was understood to recite a verse of “Fighting
Bobs,” after which he turned on his heel and went
off. Albert Whelan attempted a turn or two, but
gracefully gave way to the noise. And yet the
dailies announced that the programme was gone
through by well-known artists, and the inaudible
speeches were reported at length. By the way,
when is Premier See going to hire a ghost capable
of prompting him to say something at least semi-
interesting ?
* * * *
Australia’s returned cohentingeter is grow-
ing to be a bumptious and insufferable nuisance.
His latest development occurred at Melbourne.
A quantity of warrior was deposited at that city
from South Africa, and was provided thence with
free steamboat passages to his various destina-
tions. The aggregate military person, however,
had an idea that he would prefer to proceed by
train —for no discoverable reason except that rail-
way travelling is more expensive, and he felt it
befitted his dignity to go by the more costly con-
veyance. So the troopers systematically missed
their steamers, and when they had had this mis-
fortune several times they descended on Federal
Premier Barton with a demand for free railway
tickets. And Barton, instead of telling them
either to catch the next boat so else walk back,
gave them what they asked for. Military disci-
pline is in a fine, healthy state in Australia.
Fancy a squad of British soldiers, who had orders
to proceed somewhere by sea, not only failing
to go, but bailing up Salisbury for pauper railway
tickets instead ! And this country thinks it has
really been going to war “allee same great
Eulopean Power.” But luckily Barton’s spine
stiffened after a while. The first batch of appli-
cants got their gratis passage, but when more and
more batches, all of whom had missed every avail-
able boat, came along, they were told that the
price of their tickets would come out of their pay.
