llie reputation of Pentridge as a safe de-
has been badly shaken by the smart escape
of Sparks and O Connor. But prisoners tell an
even more curious story of Pentridge, dating the
incident back about two years, and warders corro-
borate the tale unofficially. It is said that a
noted criminal actually broke into Pentridge, re-
mained m the place two nights and three days,
wearing prison clothes, and eating prison fare,
and then made his way out again, having obtained
,7 ln kinndation the facts he was after concerning
the whereabouts of a valuable swag that had been'
hidden securely for over three years.
**. * *
Vic. Government, in its anxiety to re-
capture convicts Sparks and O’Connor, who
walked out of Pentridge a few days ago, displays
revolting callousness. The Govt. Gazette and
the Police Gazette both contain extended de-
scnptions °f the men, that of Sparks winding up
with : Sparks’ proper nm 3 is Patrick M'Guire ;
his father resides at Blank ; he has a sister named
Mrs Blank, residing at 70 Blank-street, Blank,
and brothers mining at Blank.” Connor’s relatives
are more fortunate; all that the police know is
he has a wife living in Blank-street, Blank.”
JNow, although the two journals named are not as
vvideJy read as, say, The Bulletin, the publication
of the above particulars is a cruel slap at the un-
fortunate relatives,and amounts to holding them
all up to public obloquy as connections of a
desperate criminal. The only persons interested
in their relatives are the police, who could be in-
formed privately. By the way, it wouldn’t be
surprising if Sparks turns up to prosecute the de-
partment for libel, as he is described as having
moles on the small of the back, boil-marks on
right hip, and crooked mouth.” Anyhow, his
sister has the best of reasons for complaining, and
Minister W A. Trenwith, whose signature
appears at the tail of the notice, should be
thoroughly ashamed of himself.

Percy assumes a lofty attitude, these days,
in regard to the marriage question. “ Bai Jove !
you know, there are such crowds of pretty girls
trying to take a chap by storm.” But the P.D.
corset enables a smart girl to treat such preten
sions with scorn. The P.D. figure brings Percy
aforesaid to his knees every time. It is the clever
girl’s device for conquering the male cynic, ere
leading him away in silken bonds. P.D.—Percy’s
Delight.*

A WOMANS LETTER.
% Sydney, July 22, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
If you wear a hat like a folded pancake, and
wool in your ears, and smell of eucalyptus, and call
Jones “Jodes,” you’ll be really fashionable.
Everyone has a distressful cold, although the
weather’s not wet. It’s that way in Melbourne,
where they are carrying little hot-boxes to warm
their hands. The golfers who came up from there
and from M.L. to play for the championships are
taking back champion colds.
These golfers—they are a hardy-looking, ruddy-
faced set —have had a great time on Bondi links,
where Mrs. Hugh Macneil gave a big tea on
Saturday afternoon. By now, all the players will
have bogied back to their own links. Mr. Gillies,
an Auckland solicitor, who held the champion-
ship until Mr. Howden, a Melbourne man, wrested
it from him the other day, is a tall, lazy-looking
personage who has also “ worn the belt ” for foot-
ball, rowing, and cricketing. And he has written
books on fishing. The professional man who can
do all these things thoroughly must be addicted
to the pernicious habit of early rising. Little red
coats worn by the golfing women were smart and
bright. Miss Guthrie and Mrs. Monte Douglas,
the Geelong representatives, had blue facings on
red. Mrs. Trapp, the Misses Brown, M‘Kellar,
Calder, and Baird were of the Melb. team. Miss
Darley presented the prizes at the club-house in
the afternoon.
A few marriages occurred last week, among
them that of Miss Gertrude Raphael Rosenthal
to Mr. William Macintosh. The bride, who
married out of her ancient faith, is the grand-
daughter of a man who was so conservative about
it that he owned a private cemetery. From
“Home’’comes the news that the Hon. Judith
Harbord, sister of Lady Carington, was married
to a clergyman named Sullivan last month. Bride-
groom is son of Admiral Sir Francis Sullivan.
Hon. Judith was Maid of Honor to the late Queen,
who, before her death, gave as presents a jewel
and the usual Indian shawl. Such a lot of ladies
holding Court billets—Maids of Honor, Readers to
Her Majesty, Strokers of the Royal Maltese Cat
and suchlike—marry, somewhat late in life, parson
persons. Talking of marriages, a lady up North
has just married her sixth husband. Her previous
spouse died a year ago. Now she is the woman who
might (if she could) give us a volume of real love-
letters—not faked ones. Also, “Husbands I Have
Known ” is a book she might write. What oppor-
tunities Providence gives some women !
There was more “stooping” than the players
reckoned upon at the Palace when Sydney Uni-
versity Amateur Dramatic Society produced
“She Stoops to Conquer” last week. The men
in the cast had to do it to lift fauna and flora
which humorists in one of the boxes flung upon
the stage. The flora, it is true, took the form of
nothing prettier than an elderly cauliflower,
carrots and turnips. Fauna was represented
by a dead cat. It was a glossy-coated, beauti-
fully - marked cat and it came on promptly
at the cue (Marlowe was reading something about
hare soup from the bill-of-fare), but cat-lovers like
myself were sorrowful throughout the remainder
of the performance. We were convinced that the
dark young man in the red fez, who, I think, threw
the dead meow, had killed it for dramatic pur-
poses ; for it was a fresh cat, and it’s not probable
that he could get a full-grown, pretty, just-died-
a-natural-death cat at an hour or a day’s notice.
A bunch of turnips gave Mr. Norman Gough (Mr.
Hardcastle) a chance to score. He was on in a
scene with Tony Lumpkin when the roots arrived,
and, looking up at the box, he entreated “one
fool at a time.”
Of the players (fancy me giving the cat and
vegetables first mention !), Miss Amy Mack as
Mrs. Hardcastle, Mr. Arthur Faber as Tony
Lumpkin, and Mr. Gough as old Hardcastle, were
conspicuously good. The audience was a cool
one, but Miss Mack and Mr. Faber got whatever
spontaneous applause was going. Miss Mercy
Murray in her difficult part of Kate Hardcastle
looked nice, and played gracefully, and her sweet,
clear voice is a big asset in her favor. Miss
Murielle Stratford as Miss Neville does a mock
flirtation well, and tosses her curls prettily. And
it was most altruistic of Miss Blanche Boland to
make herself look a fright for one night only.
The audience, which included a few professors’
wives, had a scholastic look, and was evidently of
value to the opticians. Mr. Tweedlepunch, of
“Florodora,” would have been delighted to put
its bumps under review. And the orchestra—
now, if cats and cabbages had been aimed at that
drove of Zitherites, I could understand it. I
thought of the notice put up at dances in Bret
Harte’s country : “ Please don’t draw your revol-
vers on the band. It is doing its best.”
Off with Goldsmith, on with Robertson. Sydney
Comedy Club played “ School ” at the Palace the
night after the Uni.’s show. It is confessedly the
best all-round amateur company here, and it
played capitally to the most ice-bound audience
I ever sat amongst. The Free Kindergarten
Union’s committee could not have worked the
tickets very well or there would have been a
bigger audience. Bits of “ School ” want bringing
up to date. Lord Beaufoy’s philippic about chig-
nons, woman’s suffrage, and tailor-dressed women
fell flat as thin porridge. Very few of us who
now infest the planet were alive in chignon time.
Very few object to woman-suffrage, and not to
wear tailor-mades is a sign that you are either
deficient in figure or taste. Miss Queenie Doyle
made a oharming grey-robed Bella ; Miss Mabel
Hogg and the pretty little girl who says that
Chimborazo (or some mountain) is four hundred
miles high, have a lot of promise as actresses.
The Euroka Band deserves a loud timbrel note of
thanks, and the man who corneted the solo,
“Lost Chord,” did it so beautifully that I wish
he’d do it again every evening in my suburb.
A correspondent writes to me of a levee held by
Duke George in an Australian city. The man
presented before my correspondent was a small
carpenter in an antique dress-suit, apparently a
haven for moths. He was announced with
severe accent on the last three words as “ Mr.
Smith, Mayor of Kooparatu.” Also about the
ducal visit to another Australian city : “ Its
Mayoress confided to the Duchess that one of her
sons was very ill in the neighboring province, and
that she was waiting for a train to go and see him.
The Duchess suggested coming up in the Royal
train with Us, and so arrive at the sick-bed
earlier. Taking the invitation literally, she had
her portmanteau put into the Royal carriage. The
Duke, coming in, saw the bulky package and
ordered it out, and it was finally deposited in a
carriage with the maids and flunkeys, whose com-
pany the Mayoress had to endure to her journey’s
end. ” But surely the Mayoress and her carpet-
bag would not be put into the flunkey’s carriage.
The Yorks would not be so vulgar. She and it
might travel with the private secs., the newspaper
correspondents and the Court painter.
Someone complains that at Buckland Park,
S.A., where the Duke shot tame pea-hens, the
Adelaide Club, which ran the trick, put the re-
porters who accompanied the party to dine with
the Royal servants. Well, perhaps those menials
were, after all, not quite such flunkeys as the
Adelaide Club men themselves.
Did you see a letter purporting to have
been written by a Boer girl to the mother
of one of the dead Vic. contingent ? It has
been copied into dozens of Australian papers, and
at least a dozen clippings of it have been sent
to me. The clippers seem to take it quite seriously
and have no doubts as to its genuineness. I have
doubts. Would not be surprised to learn that
“ Edith Matthews,” V the Boer amateur nurse,”
is an Englishwoman—romantic, and with a turn
for sentimental letter-writing. The young Vic-
torian was brought, mortally wounded, to a hos-
pital belonging, as she says, to “the so-called
Boers ” —that’s a give-away, isn’t it ? Real Boers
don’t resent the name, but are proud of it. Edith
takes the young Vic. under her charge and calls
him “My Laddie,” which is indeed verra Scoatch.
He fixes his “large blue eyes on her ” and says,
“To-morrow I’ll be on the way to the happy
land.” That’s very unlike what a Vic. contin-
genter would say. It’s very like a good Tommy’s
remark. Miss Matthews goes on—“ Tell his
sisters that I tried to be a sister to him ” (which
is a very Annie Swanish wheeze), but doesn’t
state if she succeeded. And then she kissed him
for his mother—-and several other people.
Mr. Willie Beattie, who has just left for Europe,
was fare welled in Mr. Sid. Long’s studio by a few
friends, and on their behalf presented with an
illuminated address and something in an envelope.
Mr. Goring Thomas, who made the presentation,
said the envelope contained “sea-joi.” The address,
done by Miss Thea Proctor, showed two peacocks
spreading their tails On either side of a classic-
draped figure of Comedy holding a mask.
“About Girls,” “Aches,” and “The Human
Voice ” look attractive texts for lectures. Sydney
Ladies’ Sanitary Association announces them
amongst others for its winter course, which
began at Town Hall on Monday last.
A ball in aid of the funds of the Montefiore
Home will be held in Paddington Town Hall,
Aug. 6. Mrs. Loewenthal and the Misses Harris
and Alexander are hon. secs.
A.N.A. will hold its first annual ball at the
Oddfellows’ Temple, Elizabeth-street, August 9.
Tickets, ss. each, can be had of the general sec.,
289 Pitt-street.
An “ Old Subscriber ” writes to an Adelaide
daily paper: “ My wife has in her possession a
biscuit which she got, when a girl, from the Duke
of Edinburgh’s ship, when here in 1867. It is in
perfect order, and a real ship’s biscuit.” And the
paper solemnly adds (“ Old S.” is surely a heavy
advertiser !): “ We shall be happy, as requested,
to give our correspondent’s address to anyone
wishing to see this curiosity.” I wonder if the
Pilgrimage to the Biscuit has set in yet in the Holy
City. If a ship’s biscuit is a “curiosity,” then a
curiosity is within the reach of all persons and
most dogs. But Old S.’s biscuit has probably a
brass tablet affixed guaranteeing its Galateaism.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent.:—■
“Just now few Melb. Naicest are seen in town
by daylight, for they’re all mad on bridge whist and
meet every afternoon at one house or another to
flog their hard-ridden hobby to an early grave.
“ This week ‘ Cliveden ’ runs the swell nuptials
of the season. The youthful Frank Clarke and
little Nina Cotton are to toe the line at St. Peter’s
on Wednesday (24th) in the gaze of a swell multi-
tude. Why St. Peter’s, and liot the neighboring
Anglican church to ‘ Cliveden,’ where the family
ordinarily say its prayers, must be a matter of
the bridegroom’s taste. He has studied, or is
studying, for Orders, and ought to know all about
it.
“Owing to the mourning on both sides, the
marriage of Dr. Eustace Keogh and Miss Winnie
Rowe, fourth daughter of Mr. Casimir Rowe, of
South Yarra, went off without floral arches or the
usual green accompaniments. Besides, St.
Patrick’s Cathedral is too cold and too large for
floral trimmings. A feature of the bridal attire
was the overdress, of renaissance lace. Mr. M.
O’Loghlen (son of Sir Bryan) helped the bride-
groom over the Rubicon.
“The feminine world wonders why Minister
Drake deserts the ducklings of the service. In
the Postal Department there is weeping and
gnashing of teeth at the poor opinions expressed
by the American-looking Drake re the employ-
ment of women. One man is said to be worth
two of them, and the wail of these lesser mortals
is heard in the lobbies of the House of Reps.
Vida Goldstein, with the Public Servioe Acts
under her arm, interviews Ministers and mem-
bers, scribbles notes during the debate, and sends
them along to sympathetic Reps., or lends a criti-
cal ear to the cross-fire of the members of the
Govt, and the Opposition. Telephone Trilbies
quake while Parliament sits with its considoring-
cap on discussing an Act that has to do with their
salary and service. They sigh for the days when
Byron Moore was boss of the Exchange, and
among the perqs. of office was a nice hot dinner
for the artless little things. Think of that ! and
now ginger Drake taps his pince-nez on his palm,
and talks of clearing them out of the service !
“At Vic. Artists’ Exhibition feminine industry
is much in evidence. A praiseworthy worker is
Violet Teague, an ‘ old ’ Melb. Gallery girl with a
European top-dressing. Her portrait of an elderly
lady (No. 57) —features frozen, figure stiffened
with the shock of the undivulged scene behind the
curtain —sets spectators wondering. Is ‘Master’
kissing the cook, or is it only Sonnie proposing to
the parlor-maid? Another Teague portrait de-
picts the Maddens’ cousin, Miss Riddell-Stanley,
a pretty, picturesque, albeit comfortable and placid
‘subject.’ The artist, by working-up her sitter’s
eyes, accentuating her features and robing her in
bright green, has served up a rather dramatic,
hectic, high-nosed version of the original.
“ ‘ Welcome news from the front,’ by the same
brush, shows an evening-frocked belle boo-hooing
on Papa’s dress shirt-front. Apart from the want
of consideration for Dad’s toilette, starched linen,
punctuated with studs, is not an ideal site for ‘ a
good cry,’ though there might be worse—as I
realised on seeing Sapio, as that smooth, brown,
altogether charming Aida, make an ill-judged
attempt to weep on the coat-tail locality of her
sooty sire, what time she knelt pleading to his
offended back. Bozzoli shied so abruptly when
he felt his stage-cheeild’s warm tears falling that
his scarlet rear-draperies came untacked in the
mourner’s grasp, and observers felt quite uncom-
fortable till they saw that, except for dragging a
sash-tail after him, he was sartorially unharmed.
‘ ‘ Another busy bee is Flo. Fuller, sister of singers
Christie and Amy Fuller. Miss Fuller is the
best local authority on Cecil Rhodes, in his private
capacity, thanks to having done the big man’s
portrait and executed other commissions to his
order, while visiting relatives in S’Africa on her
way to London. Two taking Fuller exhibits in
present show are a sunny coastal bit, near Melb.
(No. 110), and ‘Summer Breezes’ (No. 36), a
sylph-like blonde—poppies in her hat, and white
frock and yellow sash —out in the wind.
“Portia Geach’s ‘Australia’ depicts a typical
poster-maiden, whose arm looks tired with hold-
ing up her well - painted white satin. An
attractive, interesting Geach design for mural
decoration is ‘ Procession of the Hours’ (No.
169), depicting twelve graceful damsels. Two
tiny tots stand for the ‘weesma’ hours’; morning-
time has for representative a virginal young thing
in green ; noon-tide glare is suggested by the
robes, crimson and gold respectively, of a glowing
pair—one raven-haired, t’other Titian-dressed.
Drowsy afternoon, amber sunset, soft dusk—all
find expression in appropriately-garbed beauties.
“ President J. Ford Patterson’s ‘ Fernshawe ’ is
shrouded in faint mist or haze. Fernshawe folk
should sue the artist for pictorially libelling their
district as a damp, unhealthy spot. M‘Cubbin,
too, strikes a chilly note in two of his pictures,
each of which represents a belle in a low-cut
bodice and picture hat, sniffing the cold air in
shivery autumn surroundings.
“ At a recent Princess matinee, Dani, petticoat-
pet of the moment, sat by himself in the d.c.
bashfully holding a newspaper up to his face
to hide his blushes from the scrutiny of the
surrounding women. In private life he’s
a fattish little man, with a sort of unshaven com-
plexion-tint, which doesn’t matter much, consider-
ing he’s a benedict, with his wife in tow, and two
bamhinos at home in Italy. At the matinee, lady
friends repeatedly but vainly beckoned the en-
gaging tenor to sit beside them and trot out his
broken English. Then they 3ent a 10-year-old
miss to entertain him, and you should have heard
young Australia, a handsome kiddie, with black
eyes, fair hair, and scarlet coat and skirt, take on
the contract, bashfulness, broken English and all.
With the aid of few words and many signs, a
brisk interview was soon in progress, and atten-
tive neighbors gleaned interesting data such as
the names and ages of the pair’s respective sisters,
&c.
“ Till I saw ‘La Boheme,’ I had no idea that
Parisians of 60 years ago were so addicted to tar-
tans. Mimi lives and dies in huge checks, and
the Bohemians are comic in plaid peg-top pants.
Even the gamins are all cross-bars.
‘ ‘ Signora Marchi, the tall, handsome duenna and
confidante of many of the operatic heroines, sees
‘La Boheme’ from the stalls. She’s a fine big
woman with raven hair, shown up by scarlet hat-
trimmings, but off the boards is not astonishingly
beautiful.
“ First night of ‘ Ballo in Mascbera ’ (July 20),
a dressy crowd filled Her Majesty’s d.c. Papa
Madden, who deserves credit for encouraging
theatrical and operatic enterprise, piloted a
double-quartette party. Daughter Leslie, back
from Brisbane, wore powder-pink cloak and
cluster of big white blossoms on top of her head,
more cap-like in effect than any of the top-thatch
fal-lals, affected by surrounding dowagers.
“Each of the Lieut.-Governor’s daughters is a
shade more demure than the sister just above her
in age. The first-born, in her spinster days, was
a rollicking damsel, but as her juniors, though
sedate, are all more or less pretty, they ‘ get
there just the same.’ At the opera, the pick
of the basket, a shade thinner thanks to
influenza, aired blue Venetian beads and
gave her patronage to the very newest
coiffure —just as fashionable hairdressers were
beginning to lament that the novelty meant to
hang fire in Melbourne. The back tresses are
built on a group of small, detached coils, low on
the neck, while the rest, not drawn back loosely
in picture style with a flower tucked behind the
ear, is padded-out according to recent smooth,
substantial methods. A neat queue, resting on
well-groomed undergrowth and girded with tiny
black-velvet belt and steel buckle, runs down the
back of the head to the neck-knot. The whole
structure, caged in invisible net, is over stiff and
studied for general appreciation.
“By-the-way, the pretty daughter of a high
official is actually regarded in certain alarmist
circles as a disturbing inter-State force. There
are Adelaide people, for instance, who blame said
charmer because the Royal peep-show visited
their city minus a Duke (Roxburghe) and a
Viscount. No use suggesting that the absentees
came to Melbourne for the races; unmollified
Southstralians dub the Victorienne aforesaid
‘Jack Horner in petticoats,’ because, so they
say, she diverted two big plums from a neighbor’s
pie.
“ Elsewhere in the D.C., the Cliveden dowager,
a be-diamoned spike standing up in front of her
stiff spire of black hair, chatted to Drs. Chas. Ryan
and Stawell, and to the girls she brought along
with her shy, slim second daughter. A tall,
raven-haired French belle, with handsome eyes
and nose and vivid coloring, lit up her white frock
with touches of scarlet.
‘* If the ‘ Bohemians ’ are death on tartan, the
‘Ballo’ crowd (Neapolitan) are given to striped
shirts, exactly like ‘Crimeans,’ and wide little
pants, much shorter in one leg than the other. As
Governor Riccardo (Larizza) stood at the witch’s
door listening to Amelia’s self-reproaches re her
guilty affection for him, he looked the image of an
angry Australian standing at the bath-room door
in girded-up pyjama trews, colored shirt and red
tie roped on anyhow. One could fancy him bel-
lowing complaints re ‘no soap in this blooming
shop!’ I wish stage Neapolitans wore kilts;
those cutty wee breeks would make ideal dessous
for plaid petticoats.
“People speculate about Larizza’s title, ‘ Com-
mendatore.’ According to ‘ltaly To-day,’ the
big tenor’s name-handle is something like Fitz-
gibbon’s ‘ C.M.G.’, only such frills are desperately
common in Italy and madly sought after.
“ Most of the male masqueraders at the operatic
ball look regular oafs in womanish pale-blue silk
hoods and cloaks. Cattadori, with his fierce
black moustache bristling from under his granny
bonnet, would do beautifully for the wolf posing
as Red Riding Hood’s grandmother. Signorina
Vaccari makes a dear little page, in pink and
white haberdashery and curly golden wig.
“In the manager’s box Malcolm MacEacharn
sat with the Prime Minister and Mrs. Barton. At
least, the house took the eagle nose and raven
head that was seen now and again, and then sil-
houetted against the wall, to be that of the
Political Boss. His figure was invisible all the
evening.
“Mrs. Booth—the fine-featured and clarion-
voiced wife of the Salvarmy ‘ Commandant ’ —
made an impressive oration at the opening of the
‘Army’s’ Federal Training Garrison t’other day.
The Countess of Hopetoun was stirred out of her
functional apathy—the usual air of forced patience
—and watched the strenuous speaker with a
kindled eye. Prime Minister Barton and others,
too, caught the impassioned glow.
“At that Hallelujah Rally and official muster,
Mrs. Geo. Turner, who has never been known to
fail a social ceremony, supplied any laugh, joke,
hint or information wanted by any of the political
brethren. In the Frou-Frou world, Mrs. George
stands for the Ministry, rules the officials to a
point of order, obtains a division or adjournment
for Mayoress Gillott when needed, and generally
plays the part of Parliamentary Whip. A more
incongruous figure at that lassie festival was Mrs.
Isaacs, who dresses with negligent grace, talks
languidly, and only seems thoroughly at home
among the Eastern cushions of her own drawing-
room.
“The Dook o’York ‘ordered’ that the little
speech he read to the public-schoolboys in Mel-
bourne should be printed, and a copy given to
every boy present. He did not, however, send
with his ‘order’ any cheque to defray cost of
printing 'and distribution. The schools have to
do it at their own expense. Royal generosity
means receiving a ‘ command ’ to do at your own
cost what you don’t want to do.
“A curious but useful sort of sew-it-on-while-
you-wait club is being started in Melbourne by a
woman who appreciates the needs of busy people.
It is to be a club where women may work, or sew
on a loose button, or use the powder horn on a hot
day, and all that sort of thing. Men are also to
be able to get a button sewn on by a lady attend-
ant for a penny. According to the promoter,
every man’s button, off any garment, will be sewn
on.
“A strange exhibit has lately been on view in
Collins street E., in the shape of an auburn
wig hanging from one of the branches of a tree in
the street avenue. The scarlet counterfeit of
man’s crowning glory, which had evidently blown
from the window of the owner, sways to and fro
in the wind to the amusement of thousands of
passers-by, a considerable number of whom re-
cognise the article and are fully aware of its
origin. The wig is still there and it is to be hoped
it will remain. Anything that will make people
laugh this weather, when the Legislative Council
is not sitting, is deserving of perpetuity.
From Brisbane:—
At the opening of Parliament Lieut.-Gov. Griffith’s fire
eating appearance in uniform and cocked hat was cruelied
by his spectacles. There were two important omissions
from the Speech—the Prickly Pear pest and Woman
Suffrage ; a possible connection between the two is not
thought of. Mrs. Griffith was not a competitor in the
feminine battle of styles that raged. All skirts trailed,
but only one lady wore her veil at half-mast. The mane
and tail of Home-Secretary Foxton’s cab horse hung in
long ringlets. Persons not quite so high up in the social
scale wore short jackets with two buttons at the back.
Kyrle Bellew carried a shower-bath tohisN.Q. mine.
The miners allow a man to carry a lOOOgal. tank on his
back, but a shower-bath ! —it is anathema. They promptly
threw mullock and decaying organisms down Romeo
Bellew’s shafts.
Grand opera found Brisbane audiences a trifle mixed ;
when one half clapped the other half told them to “ shut
up ! ” People shut up without provocation right through
the operas. One heathen woke up to hear the tenor
singing “ Thou forest one !” to the soprano. “ The mag-
nificent blonde beast roaming wantonly about in search
of victory and prey. Female, too,” he murmured, as he
dozed off again to dream of Nietzsche’s ideal man. The
Italian way of singing “ Thou false one ” had beaten him.
Yours affectionately, Akenehi.

A Puzzle in Figures.
If you take the figures 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9
and arrange them in three rows, each of three
figures, every row, if properly arranged, will
when added together, make a total of 15, no
matter whether taken horizontally, perpendicu-
larly or diagonally. The puzzle is to correctly
arrange the figures to obtain this result, and the
solution is as under :
2 9 4
7 5 3
6 18
This is one of the most remarkable combinations
of figures, but it is not more wonderful than the
exact scientific combination of the ingredients in
Warner s Safe Cure. This, remarkable medicine
eicercises so specific an action upon the kidneys
and liver that it invariably restores them to health
and activity. The principal complaints arising
from a diseased or inefficient action of the kidneys
and liver are indigestion, rheumatism, gout
neuralgia, biliousness, gravel, ansemia, debility’
most blood disorders, and Bright’s disease.
Warners Safe Cure cures all these, as it acts
scientifically by attacking the seat of the disorder
and enabling the liver and kidneys to remove the
waste products of the body, the presence of which
in the circulation is the cause of so much suffering.
Station Avenue.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
There are 39 Colonels in Victoria. Most
of them have scarcely ever seen a shell —except
on the sea-shore.
“ Banjo ” Paterson again goes roaming.
He leaves this week per Changsha en route for
England via Siberia.
Labor member Andy Kelly alone excepted,
not a single member of the old Sydney City
Council or the new Council was returned to the
new N.S.W. Parliament.
Jack Murray, of Vic. Leg Assembly, is
possibly the first man in any Parliament who- has
been bold enough to discuss the subject of intem-
perance with reference to himself as a cured sub-
ject.
Senior-Constable Sutton, who now retires
from the N.S.W. force, was shot in the neck by
Gilbert, one of the G-ardiner-O’Meally gang, in
1863, when the gang tried to stick up the Carcoar
mail. Gilbert was afterwards shot dead.
TO H.R.H.
Dear George, farewell, we’re glad you came,
And happily the bill will pay.
They call you mean, but all the same,
You freely gave yourself away !
Next to the late Parkes, dead Premier
Byrnes, of Queensland, stands most dishonored
among statue-admirers. The bulky trophy
they are now sticking up in Byrnes’ honour
saddens his friends.
There are now 11 lawyers in N.S.W.
Assembly—there were 13 of them in the previous
Parliament. The fatal number eliminated two.
Journalism, directly and indirectly, accounts for
18 members of Assembly.
Tas. historian James Fenton died at
Launceston the other day, in his 81st year. Mr.
Fenton was born in Ireland and landed a mere
boy in Hobart ever so many years ago. His
father, Captain Fenton, was buried in the Bay of
Biscay on the voyage out.

Is it a record ? Two days after (Sydney)
Mayor Graham’s knighthood was announced in
the press a big brass plate on his door announced
it to the public. A few days later the public
announced to the knight their appreciation of his
bauble by firing him out of Parliament. Moral:
Plain Jim.
The lollies that Premier Peacock chewed
amidst the tumult of the anti-Findley meeting, at
Melb. Town Hall, were cough-drops, “given to
him by Mrs. Peacock, for a sore throat.” Wed-
lock brings its compensations. The married man
gets a relaxed throat, but his dear little wifey-
pifey succours it with lozenges.
Died recently at Penola (S.A.) John
Riddoch, S. A.’s wealthiest squatter.' Away back
m the sixties Riddoch and poet Adam Lindsay
Gordon sat together in S.A. Leg. Assembly, and
Riddoch was the poet’s life-long friend. Riddoch
was one of the pioneers of the rich S. E. country
of S.A., which made his fortune.
William Byrnes, a foundation-stone of
early Parramatta, moved off last week in his
73rd year. What would Parramatta be without
the Byrnes root and stock? They came with Gov.
Phillip, and from father to son have pegged away
ever since, occasionally flowering out into politics,
but always plodding at municipal affairs.
W. H. Groom, of the House of Represen-
tatives, has been longer in Parliament than any of
his Federal colleagues. He was legislating 27
years before Turner began, and 25 years before
Holder got a seat. He was member of Q. Assem-
bly when Reid, Barton and Kingston were at
school, and before many other Reps, were born.
A man walks the streets of Adelaide called
No. 89. Years ago he was induced to stand for
Parliament and spent his little all in the venture.
Out of thousands of possible votes he got 89. His
loss and failure preyed so much upon his mind
that it bent under the strain. No matter upon
what subject he is approached, he simply mur-
murs, sadly, “ 89 ! ”
A certain Australian University professor,
who lectures on English and other foreign lan-
guages, was once riding in a railway carriage
where there were two people conversing on pri-
vate subjects in a foreign tongue. The professor,
being obliged to listen, politely said, “I feel
obliged to inform you that I understand French.”
“ But we were talking Italian, sir,” was the
answer !
Vic. Premier Peacock has made many exhi-
bitions of his incapacity lately, most strikingly in
his dealings with the unemployed. Because the
deputations refuse to regard him as a little tinpot
Joss he gets fierce and splutters like an angry
fishwife. “Loafers and boozers” was one of his
most recent outbursts, and a mad crowd waited
upon him with unfriendly intentions in conse-
quence thereof, but Aleck got out through a sky-
light, or down a trap-door, and was invisible for
many hours. Peacock, despite his deafening
smile, .has no humor, and his big head is getting
him down. He was very well as an appendage to
George Turner, but he is a failure as the boss-
cocVie of Vic. politics.

THE TURNING WORM.
“ Look here, that’s your last cheque. I’m going
to give up business.”
“ Why, are things so bad?”
“ Oh, ' no, things are all right; but all I seem to
get out of them is my board and lodging.”
Sid Mack, the youthful-looking barrister
who defended Mrs. Talune Smith on her second
and third trials for alleged murder, and whose
speech to the jury on the last occasion
was characterised by Judge Owen as a
“most able, eloquent and exhaustive address”
is the brother of Louise Mack, the well-known
Bulletin writer. Also G. T. Martin, Mr.s
Smith’s junior counsel on her trials, is the sixth
surviving son of old Chief-Justice James Martin.
John Alex. Dowie, Chicago Christian
Scientist and erstwhile Sydney preacher, holding
forth in “Zion” Tabernacle: “I am Elijah, the
prophet, who appeared first to Elisha and then to
John the Baptist. Now, all who believe in me
stand up.” Three thousand people rose. “Now,”
he said, “you have listened to the first message
of the prophet, you must pay tithes and offerings
unto the storehouse of God ” (otherwise Dowie).
Then the plate went round. Even the prophet
Elijah has a plate !
Major-General Andrew Clarke, Agent-
General for Victoria, completes his 77th year this
Saturday. He is entitled to a big pension from
the State for Ministerial services performed ages
ago, but the sum now merges in his official pay.
Attorney-General Gordon (S.A.) was 51 yesterday
(July 26). He has held office under four Premiers
—Cockburn, Holder, Kingston, and Jenkins—and
he would now be Premier himself if he were in the
Assembly instead of the Council, for he is by far
the ablest and most popular man in the present
Cabinet.
John Lancelot Stirling—Conservative,
squatter, and Adelaide Club man—succeeds
Chaffey Baker as President of S.A. Leg. Council.
Like Baker, he is a son of a pastoral pioneer of
the State ; and, like that gentleman also, he is a
native of S.A., a graduate of an English Univer-
sity, and a barrister. Like him, once moie, he
represents the Southern district in the Council,
having beaten Kingston in a contest waged while
that popular tribune was representing Australia
with the Federal delegation to England last year.
Stirling has West Indian blood in his veins, but
he is good Scotch on the father’s side.
The late Mark Moss, “ the father of Vic-
torian bowlers,” has been eulogised more than he
deserved. In his wealthiest days he was not a
comparatively charitable Jew even among his own
people. He merely looked charitable, and posed
as a Great Patron of Bowls, a dignity that didn’t
cost him much. Of late years, after descending
from a “half-millionaire” height to the level of
ordinary Hebrew affluence, Moss chose to re-
gard himself as a ruined Mark, although his
establishment still sported a carriage. He ceased
to contribute to the support of his church. And
when it came to a question of burying Mark Moss,
the incidental circumstances made it clear that he
had earned very little Jewish reverence, outside
of Gentile paragraphs.
Cable tells that “ Mr. Gilbert Parker ”
(M.P. for Gravesend) “considers that the colonies
ought to contribute to the maintenance of the
British navy on the basis of their share of the
world’s commerce.” Mr. Parker, by the way,
when he first struck Sydney, years ago, was
known as the “ Rev. Horatio Gilbert Parker.”
He was a nice-looking curate sort of personage
with lovely whiskahs and blue eyes that reminded
you of Lady Audley’s father; and he distin-
guished himself by supplying to S.M. Herald a
wondrous description of Flemington ; cecourse
on the occasion of a certain Melbourne Cup. But
that is another story.
Obit: “ Smith.—On July 8, at Shoreham
(Vic.), John Thomas Thorold Smith, K.C., late pro-
secutor for the King.” Deceased was son of the well-
known John Thomas Smith, seven times Mayor
of Melbourne. J.T.T. was a barrister of Lincoln’s
Inn, called in June, 1868, and in Victoria, his
native State, in 1870. He held the post of Crown
Prosecutor for 30 years; and made his last public
appearance of any note when the Boot Trunk
Tragedy came into the Criminal Court. Of late
poor Smith was quite unfit for work, and shortly
before resigning his appointment he broke down
whilst conducting a case. Begging leave to
retire from court, he told the Judge that in
going through a bundle of documents on the
table before him he had come unexpectedly ir.on
a letter written by his deceased wife.
Cold-water Atkinson, Maoriland M. 11. it,,
has raised his voice in protest against the insuf-
ferable verbosity of Emperor Seddon. “ I felt
positively ashamed of my country,” he said,
when at a banquet in the presence of distin-
guished visitors the toast-list was monopolised by
a single man, the Premier, as though there were
no other Ministers present.” The following day
the Premier again proposed the toasts, and every
man who sat out the banquet missed the farewell
to the Royal party, whilst Sir John Anderson had
to be pitched on board the Ophir. The Em-
peror’s reply, “The fact is that it galls the hon.
member, as it galls others, to find me where I
am, but so long as I am here I will maintain the
dignity of the position.” In Maoriland, as in
London, Seddon confuses Dignity with Dreary
and Interminable Gab.

from tower to basement.
Rapid elevators run day and night, and
an efficient night-staff regularly patrols
the building.
Hot and cold water are in every room.
Private suites, combining bedroom,

and cigar-divan, are included in the
building.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
According to a sheep-farmer near Robe,
S.A., the fox is by no means the pest generally
supposed. A few years ago his property was
overrun by rabbits; Reynard came along, and
the bunnies gradually decreased, until now their
number is practically harmless. The place swarms
with the supposed connoisseurs of lamb’s tongue,
and yet, out of some 15,000 sheep, the squatter
and his manager both assured writer they had
never known a .lamb to be killed by a fox. They
consider the much-abused fox the salvation of their
holding, and have never yet destroyed one.

“ Oharky ” : Out ’roo-shooting recently, I
called at a selector’s hut for dinner. The usual
damper, dripping, and golden syrup were on the
table, and the mother noticed some syrup on the
dripping. Turning to boy next her, she said:
“You young -! What d’yer mean by puttin’
yer sticky knife in the drippin’ ? ” “ Wasn’ me,”
answered accused, with his mouth full. “ I licked
me knife ’fore I took any drippin’! ”

Maori, entering dentist’s shop one night;
“Me want toot out—how much?” Dentist:
“With gas, ss. ; without gas, 2s. 6d.” Maori;
“ Py kripe, I tink me wait te taylight kum ! ”
Dear Bulletin, — Re your par. about
Hindus in the Orbost district, Gippsland. I was
there during the 1900 maize-picking, when there
must have been 200 or more of these aliens. I re-
member the local trooper turning 13 of them out
of an old hut, not more than 12 x 10. They are a
frightful crowd, the majority suffering from loath-
some diseases brought from Little Bourke-st. In
the district is a station owned by a man who em-
ploys 15 niggers all the year round, and only
occasionally gets a white man to do fencing, &c.
The overseer is a nigger, and when he gets the
chance he makes things hot for the unfortunate
white fellow, who never remains longer than three
or four weeks at the outside. Some of the nigs
cannot speak English, and the only whites on the
station are the owner and a relative or two. And
this in White Victoria !— Swaggie.

“ Speckled Trout ” to The Bulletin :
The constable stationed at the Great Lake (Tas.) lives
with his wife and three children (youngest, aged three
months) in a corrugated-iron dwelling full of cracks,
chimneys all to pieces, doors and windows all loose. He
has to hump his tucker eight miles up a 4000 ft. rise, or
pay for packing. Np horse found, and generally the
place is a foot deep in snow. Wages, .£1 per week.. The
cost of Mulcahy’s Arch would just make these quarters
habitable. As they are at present they’re not fit for a
cow, and are a disgrace to any Govt, claiming to be
civilised.

Out prospecting, Ted got a coarse color of
gold, and, putting it under the
called New-Chum Griffin to see it. For a moment
Griffin’s face was a study. Then, as Ted was
withdrawing the glass, Griffin seized his wrist and
exclaimed. “ For God’s sake, Tid, howld it that
size/”

“ Woady Yallock ” on agruesome'subject:
Re Melbourne slaughter-houses (8., XS/5/’Ol). Why not
giveN.S. Wales a show? Writer knows of a slaughter-
house or two that can give points to anything
discovered in the city by the Yarra. The Board of
Health, in its wisdom, has appointed one inspector to the
charge of two places, situated a considerable dis-
tance apart, and although said inspector is competent
and painstaking, he unfortunately is one of the common
or barn-door variety of men and can’t be at both places
at once ; whereby comes the chance of one of the above-
mentioned meat-shows. Writer has known cattle fairly
rotten to be killed at 3 a.m., shoved into a meat
waggon, and taken to a tinning establishment before
and ylight; likewise pigs from which bags of tubercle as
big as a man’s fist had to be cut. At some such estab-
lishments sinews, &c., are carefully kept by being'piled
one upon the other and dried under a shed. A
casual inspection of this heap will show a moving mass of
maggots, green beetles, and other horrors. When they
are sufficiently dried they are taken to Sydney, whence
they emerge in the shape of gelatine, to be manufactured
into lollies of the jelly type.

“ X.” : At Mount Morgans(W.A.) I came
across two women having a slanging-match. One
had the moral support of her
small boy, the other was single-
handed. Suddenly the small
boy lifted up his voice; “Call
her a useless cow, mother, afore
she calls you one.” I believe
among the lydies who lift up
their voices in public expostu-
lation an epithet is considered
to have vastly more weight when
used for the first time —if it is
simply thrown back it is a
second-hand epithet and com-
paratively harmless. And the
small boy, acting as an am-
munition-waggon in the rear,
noticed that neither party had
remembered to call the other a cow, so
he hastened to pass the epithet along.
His mother promptly called the other
woman a cow, and that being the only
thing that neither party had previously
called the other the second woman
was thereby routed with great
slaughter.

kiss’em book, blow ’em out match,
bleak’em saucar, cut’em roostah ’ed off,
anytingyou like —but him liah, alleesame.”

“ Itan ” : Was in a country town once
when new telegraph poles were being erected.
At one point there was a double turn, and to allow
the poles some play to straighten up when the
wires were strained on them, they were erected
with a backward list at each corner. Before the
day was out, 297 people called at the office to
confidentially inform the post and telegraph boss
that the poles weren’t straight. This either means
that the Australian takes a great and intelligent
interest in publio affairs, or that he spends a great
deal of time at corners.
* * * *
“V.”: Saw some large circular holes in
the dry bed of a creek on the upper Burnett
waters (Q.). Subsequently ascertained they were
the work of brumbies, who came out of the scrubby
ranges at night and pawed out these holes until
they struck water.
* » * *
“S.”: The recent adventures of Percy
Pegg, of Junee, N.S.W., with a tiger-cat which
killed his dog and was willing to do as much for
him, recall an experience of my own with a tiger-
cat (“ mountain cats” we called them asboys)in the
State Forest near Bungaree, Vic. The tiger-cat
is the only Australian animal I have ever seen
show a desire to stand his ground and “fight it
out ” with man. There were three of us; we had
shot a small kangaroo, and, to save haulage, hid
the body in a log. On returning, we found the
’roo dragged partly into the open, and a villain-
ous-looking mountain cat settling down to supper.
The brute was about the size of a water-spaniel,
and, although it might have escaped easily, it
stood to its feast, glaring at us with feline male-
volence. One enterprising youth went forward
and poked the cat up with the barrel of his gun.
We hardly saw what happened : there was a sort
of cat and boy convulsion, out of which the boy
issued almost trouserless, bleeding copiously from
about two miles of scratches, and howling madly
as he ran. The cat went back to meals. We
killed the beast with ten shots, and I have never
since cared to stir up an adult tiger-cat when
wearing my new suit and my best pink tie.
“ Occy ” : Wilson and I were quail-shoofc-
ing on Dinny O’Brien’s
estate. When Dinny heard
the sound of the guns he
came galloping over, appar-
ently to slay us on the spot.
W lien he saw who we were
he changed his mind and
asked us up to the house to
have some refreshment in-
stead. He took two bottles out of
a cupboard ; one was whisky, the
other wasn’t. The one that
wasn’t whisky he alleged
to be some wonderful
hop-beer of the wife’s
own brewing. I took
whisky. Wilson was
persuaded to take the
beer. Dinny poured
it out from a great
height to get a head
on it. Wilson sipped
it with a look of
horror on his face.
He was encouraged
on until he got half-
way through with his
undertaking. At last
Dinny took a mouth-
ful himself and as
promptly shot it out
with that want of cere-
mony that characterised
the great Dr. Johnson. He
gargled his throat direct
from the water-bottle,
and, looking with amaze-
ment at Wilson, ejacu-
lated : “ G reat heavens,
d’ye know what you’ve
been drinkin’, man ?
Why, horehound bitters
for washin’ the horses’
feet! ”
“ Quex ” : Travelling, one day, near
Dunolly (Vic.), I was asked by a young fellow for
a lift. After driving two miles in silence, he said,
“I’ll get down here, mate, and get up again, if
you’ll wait a jiff.” He descended, entered a house,
and presently came out with a girl. “ It’s no use,
Min. I find I don’t love you any more, and I’m
not going to hang on any longer.” Girl burst into
tears and endeavoured to fix her ear on to his
button-hole, but, gently laying her down, he
crawled over the fence and into the buggy.
“ Drive on, mate —I never could stand a scene.”
I drove.

“G.F.” : Re the practice of some bush-
men in ringing a horse’s neck with a strong
leather band, whereon to hang hobbles, &c. I
remember a horse being found dead, one morning,
having during the night got his hind foot through
the ring of the hobble-chain. In his efforts to dis-
engage the foot, the maddened animal had
gouged out his eye, and left the flesh hanging in
shreds from neck and cheek. The ground was
soaked with blood, and the poor creature pre-
sented a sickening spectacle.

“C.H.R.” : Dropped on a humorous milk-
vendor, the other day. Had reason to doubt the
respectability of the morning oowjuice, and re-
quested the youngster who brought it round to
tell her parents that, latterly, the milk had a
bluish tinge. Next morning, she explained :
“Please, father says that there milk eomed from
the blue cow ; all the others is run dry.”
» * » »
Things look gloomy round the Western
N.S.W. shearing-sheds. The squatters, or some
of them, are presenting a Pastoralists’ Union
agreement, and the Union shearers may not
accept it. Many of the local men thought resist-
ance might have been deferred till a better season;
but they were over-ruled. Already strike-camps
have been formed at Cobham and other early
sheds.

HIS ONLY READER
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
J'HE Poet woke at
dead of night :
He heard a voice
within the room
Where he was wont his
verse to write ,
And rose up quickly
in the gloom.
He seized an axe and softly crept
In his pyjamas from his bed —■
And he beheld a man who wept
As one ivho weeps above the dead.
He ivas a rugged, bull-necked man,
A man of violence and sin ;
Biot down his cheeks the tear-drops ran,
And over his protruding chin.
A lantern dark ivas by his side;
Its light with cautious glimmer shone —
He worked it with a patent slide
A tattered manuscript upon.
It was a tale in metre told,
A gentle tale of old Romance ;
The Poet recognised the cold
Rejection-marks with half a glance.
It ivas a travelled manuscript,
Well-known from Brisbane unto Perth ;
For it had trained, and coached, and shipped
All o’er and round Australian earth.
No editor so full of gin
That he that manuscript would use;
But, each and all, to take it in
Unanimously did refuse.
The Author loved it well, and he
Unto his friends would broadly hint
There was a base conspiracy
To keep the poem out of print?
At last it had a Reader found
Who saw at once its merits true ;
The Poet dropped his axe, and round
That reader’s neck an arm he threw.
They sobbed awhile together; then
The Poet said, “I fain would know
The cause why you, alone of men,
Should love my little poem so ?”
The burglar wiped his eyes. Said lie —
That beetle-browed and bull-necked elf —
“ Before I took to burglary
I used ter write like this myself! ”
OREEVE ROE.


A Water Diet.
Considerable interest has been taken in Mrs.
Mary McArdle, of Brewarrina, who, when inter-
viewed, was staying in Taraworth with her friend,
Mrs. Mary Carpenter (says a reporter). This
lady has actually endeavoured to exist on a diet
of water, and it was really on this account that
the reporter recently interviewed her. She ex-
plained the reason of her extraordinary action as
follows :
“It is nearly ten years ago since, as far as
my inclination went, a drink of water was
sufficient for meals. Indeed, the eating of
solid food brought on such severe pains of in-
digestion . that .1 was positively frightened to
eat proper food. At .times of course, I forced my-
self to eat something solid, otherwise I should
have died, but those who have suffered from the
pains of indigestion will understand how I
abhorred meal times. I had also severe pains
right across my forehead and over my eyes. I
used to get quite dizzy, and sometimes would have
fallen had I not clutched at the nearest article.
At night I could not sleep well, and the slightest
sound would wake me. As time went on I grew
terribly nervous, weak and thin ; sometimes 1 was
too weak to walk about the house.”
“ Your experiences have been particularly dis-
tressing,” said the reporter.
“Yes,” replied Mrs. McArdle, “but they at
last came to an end. I had tried almost every
remedy thought likely to benefit me, without
success, when, after suffering for eight years, a
lady friend induced me to give Dr. Williams’
pink pills for pale people a trial.”
“ The result is evident in your sprightly appear-
ance,” said the reporter.
“Yes,” she replied, “it is. Iliad only been
taking them fo r a fortnight when I felt consider-
ably better, and as I continued using them the
headaches and nervousness left me. 1 regained
my appetite and ate without ill-effect; I was able
to sleep well at night, and soon began to put on
flesh. Dr. Williams’ pink pills are a reliable
medicine, and I will always recommend them to
my friends.”
Lack of blood is the direct cause of physical and
functional weakness, headaches, indigestion, heart
palpitation, backache, cold feet, and ti.e general
wretchedness of “delicate” people. Dr. Williams’
pink pills increase and enrich the blood and tone
up the nerves. Thus they cure such diseases as
the following; which have for their foundations
vitiated blood and unstrung nerves : —rheumatism,
neuralgia, sciatica, bronchitis, consumption, pa-
ralysis, locomotor ataxia, nervous disorders, etc.
Sold by chemists and storekeepers, and by the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place,
Sydney, two and nine per box, six boxes fifteen
and three, post free. Describe your symptoms
and write for a special letter about your case.*

“Viking” : Late in the Seventies
Sydney barque with a general cargo and a
Formosan crew was thrown on her beam-
ends, in a gale, off the Celebes. The crew
dived into the foc’sle. The second mate
went below, and found them in various
attitudes smoking rank cigars. With
much vigorous language he urged them
to go on deck. “No fear,” said the
Serang, “no more work. All go down
soon. S’pose we work, who pay ? ” The
infuriated European concussion inflicted
with a belaying-pin overcame their reluct-
ance, and the ship was eventually saved.
The story illustrates a curious mental
attitude of the Chinese. Death, appar-
ently, did not matter; but the idea of
working when they didn’t expect to sur-
vive to collect the wages struck the
economical Cninese mind as absurd.

“T.0.H.”: The Hindoos on the
Northern (N.S.W.) rivers have a curious
one-stringed musical instrument. It con-
sists of an empty billy-can, with a small
hole punched in the bottom, through
which a piece of string is inserted and
held by a knot on the inside. The can is
held under the left arm, the end of the
piece of string is held taut in the fingers
of the sime hind; while, with the finger
of the right hand, the performer plays
the accompaniment to some weirdly
heath,erish chant. The “music” is due
to the “(giving and backing ” of the can-
bottom, according to change of stress.

Dear Bulletin. —N.S.W. Premier See
E remises to help the shopkeepers who have lost
usine3s by the erection of barricades around
Hordern’s burnt-out emporium, if he can find a
precedent for Govt, compensation under similar
circumstances. The barricades are presumably in
the public interest. The “ Tick barricade ”up
North is also in the public interest, and
many cattle-breeders have lost far more
than these clamoring shopkeepers will lose
by a temporary check to their usual volume of
business. If Mr. See is looking for a precedent
for not allowing any compensation, let him inquire
what assistance has been given to cattle-men, who
are suffering for the public benefit by the total
stoppage of their business. If See gives com-
pensation in this case, surely he can’t refuse to
compensate men on the land for the loss of their
stock—starved out by Government rabbits.—
Gripps Clark.
* * * *
“D. 8.” : The tale of an up-country news-
paper conspiracy. When the new manager came
to take charge of the paper he started like the pro-
verbial new broom, and the reporters’ room suddenly
assumed an unwonted air of discipline. We ex-
pected trouble with the editor, and it came. The
almighty “we” signified his opinion of the new
manager in the usual way—went on a howling
drunk. The new manager raved mightily, but
the editor came not. As a last resort all the re-
porters were sent in search of the chief. Not a
man was left in the office, barring a distraught
sub-editor and the raving manager. After an
hour or two the reporters began to return, one at
a time, and quite incapable. They explained that
they had searched in many places for the editor
and were utterly exhausted. The paper got out
somehow next morning, but one such night was
enough for the new manager. He resigned forth-
with, and the old manager was re-instated. Then
we had a celebration —it lasted a week.

“Vulcan”: Watched an “ancient grey-
beard” rat sneak into a guinea-pigs’ box t’other
day. -After selecting a fine, fat little “pig,” he
clasped it to his chest with his fore-legs, and, thus
handicapped, turned himself into a log, and rolled
over and over till he reached his hole —where a
brick fell on him.
“ Quart-Pot ” : Re divining
rod. In the early days of the Bar-
rier, at the late W. R. Wilson’s
camp in the Apollyon Valley,
Surveyor Barlow, who had implicit
faith in the rod, volunteered to
prove its efficacy. Cutting a rod,
he circled round the camp, hold-
ing it in orthodox fashion, with the
result that it persistently pointed to
a number of 400 gal. iron tanks
which stood close by. The trial
7as perfectly convincing until
one of the audience examined the
tanks and found them empty!
Writer knew Stewart, who located
several wells successfully in and
around Silverton by means (alleg-
edly) of the rod, and is quite satis-
fied he worked on geological know-
ledge and simply used the stick
as a joke. This is probably the
case in other instances.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.
L.A. : To any Australian jealous of the good
name of his country it now makes little difference
whether that Boer-sniper yarn be true or not. That so
many Australians have written glorying in a dastardly
act of savagery towards a brave if foolish man is the pain-
ful reflection for all who would see the men of their nation
manly in the best sense of that ill-used word. If the yarn
be true, the men who did it have at least the palliation of
anger and blood-madness. True or untrue, what can one
say of the cowardly brutes who, in cold blood, exultantly
applaud it? Henry Martin : Explanation is simple :
You have been looking at the exports and imports of the
seven States of Australasia in Coghlan’s “ Seven Colonies
of Australasia.” The figures quoted were the exports and
imports of the six States of the Commonwealth from
Coghlan’s “ Statistics of the Seven Colonies ” —quite a
different work. In the first work Ooghlan doesn’t in-
clude India, Canada, &c., with “other countries”;
in the second he does. If we quote one authority, and
you persist in looking at another, that isn’t our funeral...

“ C.W.” : Let me point out that the term
“balmy,” as used in The Bulletin, should be
spelt “barmy,” because, according to the New
English Dictionary, the reference is to “barm”
(or yeast). The term is an English one, of fair
antiquity, and not, as many suppose, a bit of
Australian slang.

A caucus of members of the Legislative Councill
was held at Parliament House, Exhibition Biild-:
■ng, yesterday, for the purpose of electing a leader
to succeed Senator Frederick S irgood, and oN
considering the question of Parliamentary reform.;
Henry Cuthbert presided over a large attendance. :
The question of leadership was soon settled.
The caucus then proceeded to disouss the :
’question of parliamentary reform, in both its:
Igeneral aspect and its bearing upon the CounoUi :
line proposal of the Government to bring about :
reform through the agenoy of an outside body was :
generally condemned. Eventually it was deoided i
appoint a oommittee of ten members to prepare f
scheme qf reform, whioh will be considered at a :
iture meeting.
y Mr. Barton, who was cordially received, saidN
he believed no movement for the uplifting of
humanity had such good results as the Salvation
Army. Ho would attempt to make no speeoh
to add to the pathos of the simple words
of Mrs. Booth. He would content himself with
an expression of sympathy on his own part, and
an invocation to sympathy on the part of all of"
warm heart and white soul. The movement was
growing more powerful, and the more it grew
powerful the more he wished it greator power. 1
(Applause.) The ueed of an organisation to help
the sad and weary had long boon felt. There
were many religious organisations, but this was it
religion without sect. It took no account of olass
but only of misery. (Hear, hoar.) Surely there I
oould be no prejudice ; surely there could be no
jealousy of a movement like this. Surely every
religions denomination and every dogma must
euffer in comparison with the whole-souled Chris
v tiauity of this endeavor- y

Y A CONCESSION.
To a certain extent the Governmentia prepared
to relax from the firm attitude it has hitherto
taken op in connection with the clauses in the
Postal Bill affecting the transmission of letters to
‘ Tattersall’s ” in Tasmania. The clauses wilPy
not'become operative, assuming the Bill to pass *
the House of Representatives without vital
amendment, until regulations have been drafted
effecting their purpose. There is a disposition on
the part of the Government to refrain from taking
steps to draft the regulations until the question as
to States Rights raised by the Tasmanian Govern-
ment baa been settled by the Federal High Court.
This means that Tattersall’s is safe for another
couple of years at least.

MELBOURNIANA:


A thing called a “Ghost Shark ” has
been found at Manly. The only way to
avoid seeing these unpleasant things is
to shun Manly whisky.
THE UNEMPLOYED.
The shelter sheds on the Bunnerong-
road are now completed, and already
15 inmates are there. The grounds
around these sheds will be worked as
kitchen-gardens, in which the in-
mates will be expected to earn
their keep. Books and news-
papers will be provided.
And, of course, The O’Sullivan will
see to it that the Sydney D.T. is
on the list.

“ The police were personally pre-
sented by the Duke of York with.'
autograph photos, of himself and-
the Princess.” —Daily papers;
Mr. O’Sullivan, in acknowledging a presentation*'
by Labor organisations of a gold watch and purse of '
sovereigns, thanked the donors for the handsome gifts
they had made him—incentives to higher achieve-
ment as they would prove. The watch would ever
remind him of the time when he was an active mem-
ber of trade organisations, and the purse of sovereigns-
•would be devoted to the purchase of a piece of land
•in the eastern suburbs, where he had resided over 21
years. Upon that land he would build a residence,
over the door of which would be inscribed the motto/
“ Labor omnia vincit.”
Our artist here gives an architectural detail’ of
the door of said house, and suggests (without extra
charge) some Latin to go with it. The house is
guaranteed waterproof and the Latin will wash.

13 CENTRAL ARCADE, CEORQE STREET, SYDNEY.
“The sinecures are all filled by the in-
efficient friends of the efficient.” — Century Mao.
The rain was driving along the streets and
eddying at the corners. The wood blocks were
slippery, and all the depressions of the very much
depressed Sydney streets were filled with water
and slush, and everyone who could afford it had
taken to cabs and broughams. The large, fat
woman stood at the corner of Barrack-st., and
looked longingly across at the Post office. Then
she held her skirt up, and showed an elegant red
underskirt, which she trailed through the slush m
sweet unconsciousness. It was a wonder she held
up anything, for she carried four parcels, a hand-
bag and an umbrella, and led her dog by a chain.
The tyke got impatient, and started across the
street. She followed. The tram-driver clanged
his bell loudly ; the dog got trodden on by a boy
who was rushing past; and, as she fled to get
off the tram-line, a cab bore right down on her.
People shouted, “Look out! look out!” The
woman stuck to the dog, and, turning her face
down the street, scuttled along in front of the car
right between the rails. The car was stopping,
and in another moment she would have been safe,
when down she went, right in the track, and the
small boy with the time table yelled,. “ What ho !
she bumps! ” The public held its collective
breath as, with screeching wheels, the tram slid
along and gently pushed her in front of it,
through all the slush of George-street. She
wasn’t hurt, but her yells shook the Post-office.
They picked her up, and after a workman liad
scraped her down with a shovel, they put her in. a
cab, and it was only then they found that she still
held tightly in her hand a chain, attached to
the other end of which was a small dog, in a
most disreputable state. The woman who hangs
on to her dog in such circumstances isn’t so far
removed after all from the three Romans who
held the bridge under trying circumstances in the
long, long ago.

Tto.es In. dependent order of Foresters.
What a distinguished Insurance man in liondon, England, thinks of the Independent Order of Foresters.
Mr. William Schooling, known to most insurance people as the eminent editor of Bourne, the great insurance journal, shows how he
feels with regard to the financial stability and the wisdom of the methods pursued by the Independent Order of Foresters, in the following
letter written to Oronhyatekha, Supreme Chief Ranger of the Order, on the first day of the new century: —
BOURNE’S INSURANCE INSTITUTE LTD.
Wm. Schooling, Editor and Manager. Granville House, Arundel Street, Strand, W.C•
January 1, 1901.
ORONHYATEKHA, M.D., J.P., G.C.R., Toronto, Canada.
Dear Sir and Brother.—lt is with peculiar satisfaction that, on the first day of the new century, I write to tell you that
I have become a Beneficiary Member of the Independent Order of Foresters. My pleasure is only mitigated by one circum-
stance, which is that, by becoming a member now, 1 cannot avail myself of your very gratifying offer to personally
initiate me into the Order. •
You will readily understand that my work leads me to criticise pretty keenly all matters connected with insurance, and
I have not formed niy present high opinion of the 1.0. F. without looking very carefully into the facts.
Permit me to express the great pleasure I derived from your al!-to-short visit to me a few weeks ago, and to express the
hope that I may have some longer talks with you when you are next in London.
I have told Brother Cronyn that I want to help the Order in every way that I can, and have asked him to let me know
when any opportunities of doing so arise. To-day I had a visit from Brother Reynolds, with whom I had a very interesting
chat, and I am already beginning to experience the pleasant results of fraternal membership.
Please accept my heartiest good wishes for the happiest prosperity for the Order, and for yourself personally, and
believe me, yours fraternally in L.B. and C.
(Signed) WILLIAM SCHOOLING.
The above letter, from so eminently distinguished an insurance expert as Mr. Schooling, is the best possible evidence that the Independent
Order of Foresters is an institution of the first class.
Rates much lower than the Old Line Companies. Absolute safety. Prompt payments, and many advantages not offered by other companies. Over
£900,000 of surplus assets. £1,800,000 already paid to widows and orphans. Surplus reserve increasing monthly at the rats of £12,000 per month.
Representatives wanted in every community. For full information and literature, address C. A. FRASER, Head Offices for New South Wales, 283 George
Street, Sydney. ORONHYATEKHA, Supreme Chief Ranger.

The monster public deputation in favor of'
Co-Operative coupons (introduced by Mr. Duncan
Gillies, M.L.A.) established an Australian record ;
over 500 people attended. The speakers were
limited by Mr. Peacock to five, but they rep-
resented an enormous number of the public. Mr.
Nelson (representing 2000 workers in the railway
service) said : “This coupon system has taught us-
thrift and brought pleasure _ to a great many
homes.” Mr. Davis (President Brickmakers 5
Association) said that “ The public realised that;
it is the only system by which they can get dis-
count on all small purchases.”*

A red-hot Orange weekly paper is to be
issued at Sydney by a £lO,OOO limited liability co.
Recent by-elections in the Northern State
leave parties practically as they were before the
Federal fray. The Philp party has gathered in
“ Father ” Groom’s successor.
Talk again of Australian representation in
the British Parliament. Australian representa-
tion !
High time for that when we have shown
That we can get it in our own.
The Q. “Independent” politician is a
parlous creature. Some of him were recently
elected on thp off-chance of a little backbone, but
as two are moving the Address-in-Reply, the Maw
has evidently “ scooped ” them in, as usual.
Both the Melb. morning dailies bitterly
cursed L. L. Smith for daring to split the com-
bined Tory-Liberal vote against Findley, and
urged the electors to forfeit his deposit and throw
him into outer darkness. Yet the discredited old
battler, without any programme and with very
hazy ideas on present politics, managed to get
496 votes.
WANTED, A BOSS.
Copies of cables relating to the Tocsin case were laid on the
table of Vic. Leg. Assembly. The Lieutenant-Governor (who is
also Chief Justice I) cabled to the Secretary of State as follows :
“ Secret and confidential. A newspaper in Melbourne of no great
influence, but ill-disposed, has republished a libel on His Majesty the
King, lately suppressed by the Irish Government. Doubtful whether any
local authority to seize and to suppress here in the ordinary course of
law. If I, with the advice of Ministers, cause the newspaper to be seized
and suppressed here as an act of State, will the Imperial Government
adopt my act as such, because otherwise I have not, I think, any
authority to do an act not otherwise warranted as an act of State.
Ministers are quite willing to advise me and to act. Reply as soon as
possible."
The Secretary of State replied :
“ Secret and confidential. In reply to your telegram of to-day’s date,
the adoption of seizure by His Majesty’s Government as an act of State
would not help if no local authority to seize. The defence of an act of
State is not available against a British subject. For this and other
reasons His Majesty’s Government do not advise any action of the
nature proposed.”
Apropos the suggestion that the State
should take up the business of selling liquor in the
resumed area of Sydney—it contains 29 pubs.,
some of them not of the sweetest—why shouldn’t
the Commonwealth do the same thing in the
Federal capital ? Public ownership and control of
the business is so obviously advantageous that it
would be soon accomplished if the vested interest
<ii In’t bar the way ; wherefore every chance
s hould be snapped before Bung steps in.
AFTER EAST MELBOURNE ELECTION.
How soon on Freedom’s boon they’d seize
If men for their own precious right
Would strive as fiercely as they fight
The battles of their enemies!
Cabled that Lord Raglan, British Under-
secretary for War, announces that the Govern-
ment is sending a committee of ladies to report
upon the refugee camps. If you can’t get the
truth from partisan men, you won’t get it from
partisan women.
Judging by recently-inspired remarks, the
Philp Govt, is going to fill the financial gap by
selling huge tracts of Queensland to wealthy out-
siders. The birthright is going for the mess of
pottage, eaten yesterday. Perish the thought of
a land or absentee-tax.
On the strength of numerous anecdotes
it may be mentioned that the formal prayer in
the House of Representatives is just the Phari-
saical farce it was so largely expected to be. Some
members pay no attention, others study the notice,
some even tick-off the questions that interest
them.
Sydney Telegraph’s financial authority :
If the Australasian Governments can raise £35,000,000
a year locally in revenue it is not impossible to secure
locally one-seventh of that sum (£5,000,000) on loans.
Also, if the Australasian Governments can raise
£35,000,000 a year revenue locally, and £5,000,000
a year loans locally, they could raise £40,000,000
revenue and have no loans.
Dear Bulletin. —I am sickened by the
skite of some N.S. Wales M’s.P. about having
helped to make the Old-Age Pension Bill law,
because since, in many cases, their mothers and
others near to them are going to benefit by it,
said M’s.P. had a direct interest in the measure.
I could name several members whose people are
reaching for the pension, and I think it an insult
for such men to remind the people of what they
have done for their (the people’s) poor.—Alpar.
All goods made by prison
labor are prohibited imports
under the Commonwealth Cus-
toms Bill and rightly so.
Every trade that can be profit-
ably practised in gaol should
be practised, because it helps
to pay the cost of the convict’s
maintenance, and if the re-
leased convict leaves gaol with
a good knowledge of a trade,
whereby he can earn an honest
living, he is more likely to be-
come a law-abiding citizen
thereafter. Further, the Aus.
tralian who is locked up has
the same right to labor as the
one who isn’t locked up, but
possibly ought to be. And
even if prison products are sold
extra cheaply though why
they should be it is hard to say
—there aren't enough Austra-
lians in gaol for their output
to seriously affect the labor
market. But if the cheap out-
put of the gaol population of
half-a-dozen countries came
here it would seriously affect
the market. Every country
should absorb its own criminals,
and their products.
The Victorian carriage-
builders are trying an insidious
dodge to work up a bogus
public opinion against the mini-
mum wage. A printed form
has been issued to each em-
ployer to be filled in. He is
to collect the opinions of all
his employes, and state how
many regard the proposed mini-
mum of 545. per week as too
high, and how many don’t.
The man who doesn’t think
it too high will, of course, be
the first to be sacked when a
slack time comes or when he
can be replaced by a less out-
spoken citizen, so a good many
will say that they regard the
wage as excessive. And then
thO bosses will raise a shriek :
‘ ‘ The wage fixed by the Board
is so outrageous that even the
men are against it.”
Ata small westernN.S.W.
town during the late State-
elections the local postmaster
E laced the hospital collection-
ox in a prominent position in
the polling-booth. An elector,
whose sight is defective, mistak-
ing the box for the ballot-box,
dropped his marked voting-
paper therein. Question then
arose as to how the paper could
be recovered, and after much
debate the postmaster took the
responsibility of opening the
box and thereby possibly turned
the political situation in favor
of the successful candidate,
who led by a majority so microscopic that a
movement is on foot locally to have the votes re-
counted.

spent on the consolidation of N.S.W. Statutes,
begun by 0. G. Heydon, now Judge? In what
stage of completeness is the work at present ?
How many junior barristers have been employed,
and at what rates ? A brief Parliamentary return
would probably set at rest some queer rumors.
