A good many people would like to know
why “ Tattersall’s ” advertisement, which is and
has long been prohibited in Queensland, should
appear with impunity in Progress, published at
Brisbane, and owned by Queensland ex-Minister
and Federal Postmaster - General Drake. As
Drake is in charge of the virtuously-indignant Bill
for the abolition of “ Tattersall,” this is somewhat
remarkable.
Chamberlain’s haggling over an extra
£lOOO for Secretary and extra A.D.C. to Vic.
State Governormeans that he wants to preserve the
Vic. and other Governorships as first-class Crown
appointments. In Crown jargon £5OOO is first-
class, less is second or third, but the £5OOO must
be clear of necessary official expenses. If the
State Governor pays for his Secretary and A.D.C.
the appointment ceases to be class 1.
Out of the 125 members of the newly-
elected N.S.W. Parliament, 13 may be taken to
represent practically the whole of the votes in
their respective constituencies, being returned un-
opposed ; 80 represent a majority of the votes ;
and 32 got in on a minority vote through the
multiplicity of candidates. In 1898, three members
were returned unopposed ; 101 represented a
majority of the votes polled ; and only 21 were
minority representatives. Things certainly have
not improved.
The Peacock Ministry is “ cooked.” The
Labor-party is awaiting its demise. Findley will
be avenged. Says a leader of the Party : “Pea-
cock gained his popularity by his laugh ; he is
keeping it by nothing better than a hysterical
shriek. He has sold his friends to his enemies,
and the latter will make the most of their chance.
When he wants our help he won’t get it, nor will
we ever trust him again. He never had much
judgment. It was Turner who kept him ballasted.
We only await our chance.”
The Political Labor League has had a bad
knock-out in the western (N.S.W.) electorates,
where hitherto it has been supreme. The only
“selected” men to win seats were Cann and
Scobie. The former practically had a walk-over ;
in Wentworth, Scobie’s district, the P.L.L. is un-
known. All the other seats were captured by in-
dependent Laborites, including Ferguson and
Sleath. The whole explanation is that out there
the P.L.L. executive has been collared by a few
tricky self-seekers, and this was the rank-and-
file’s protest.
Hints by an irreverent gallery-hand :
The Federal Senate, which won the respect of all Aus-
tralian democrats by its uncompromising stand on national
questions, is evidently suffering from swelled head incon-
sequence, and is becoming boresome. In committee,
hour after hour is wasted in discussing the most trum-
pery clauses of Bills ; and the prominence given to the
remarks of Stewart of Queensland in the early stages
of the session has converted him into a timesome
blatherskite, who feels it his bounden duty to orate by
the hour on things he in no wise understands. S.vmon
has also been complimented on that clear, incisive man-
ner of his a trifle too often, for there is not a subject now
that he doesn’t incise upon until everyone yawns.
The corpse and the kopje and the cannon
and such like are apparently going to be the Tory
man’s stock properties at elections now. Deegan,
at the East Melbourne election, went on about the
corpse and the kopje and the cannon in an alarm-
ing fashion. Among other things he told the
electors that if Findley was returned it would
“ wipe away all the brave Australian blood that
had trickled down the kopjes,” and also, that it
would “bring into disgrace the dead bodies of the
gallant men who slept under the veldt.” The
property-vote put the Dead Body Candidate in by
over 800 majority.
Dear Bulletin. —A clear proof that the
delay in printing rolls for Gympie (Q ) election
(B. 13/7/’01), was due to the successful scheming
of the Philp Govt., lies in the fact that the same
thing occurred at Rockhampton North election.
Twenty-six labor votes, out of 28 names on the list,
were barred, as the supplementary-roll had not then
been printed. The loss of these 26 votes has im-
perilled the labor-seat, the polling being : Turner
(L.) 401, Linnett (M.) 400. Latter has lodged a
petition alleging dual voting on the other side;
and the betting is “evens” that he goes in after
all. The Filch Government. —G.A.P.
After every N.S.W. general election listen
for loose talk about “the splendid organisation of
the Labor party.” But where is it? Outside
Newcastle and Broken Hill it is pretty certain
the P.L. Leagues couldn’t muster 1000 members ;
and the best wins, not only this year, but hitherto,
have been in districts where there have been either
only dummy leagues or none at all. Hard battling
by the candidates and the popularity of the
Labor platform are responsible for three-fourths
of the seats. But the popularity of the Labor
policy has to be depreciated somehow, and so
the crowd fall back on the “ splendid organisa-
tion.”
The Jenkins Govt, goes to S.A. Parlia-
ment, opened last week, with the main items of
the Kingston and Holder programmes as its
policy. Chief planks are : Reduction of Members,
by one-third, thus making Assembly 36 Members
and Council 16 (latter to serve for six years instead
of nine and be elected bv the whole State voting
as one constituency); Household Suffrage for the
Council; Early Closing, and compulsory purchase
of large estates. The Ministry opposes Snow’s
unspeakable tramway-grab, and favors conversion
by Govt, in trust for Municipalities. Whether
Jenkins can get any part of this programme
through the .Council, now bossed by an Ass.
majority, is doubtful.
Westralia’s railway strike ended in a resort
to arbitration. With a little better management
there would never have been any strike, and a
permanent court of compulsory arbitration, before
which all such cases would come as a matter of
course, would have settled the whole trouble long
ago. The great iron-workers’ strike in Sydney
also went to arbitration, as most strikes do in the
end nowadays. The only difference between the
party which doesn’t believe in compulsory arbi-
tration and the one that does is just about this :
Under the first party’s system we have a big
strike, great excitement, disorder, loss of money
—and then arbitration. Under the second party’s
system we would just have arbitration.
Findley’s supporters made a good fight for
that honest Labor representative in East Mel-
bourne, and they also, in a pleasant way, infused
some humor into the contest. Their only hope
lay in the multiplicity of their opponents, for
under the system which allows Eat Man to vote
in any constituency where he has property, pro-
vided he only votes in one constituency on one
day, hundreds of non-residential Tories, whose
votes at a general election would be needed else-
where, poured in to boost the cause of adipose
tissue. So many of Findley’s crowd attended the
enemies’ meetings, cheered them wildly, passed
unanimous votes of confidence in them, and other-
wise encouraged them to go to the poll. Apothe-
cary Smith and others are still lost in amazement
at their widespread popularity.
“ See-Lee ” : Political leaders, at recent
N.S.W. State election, made some remarks about
bringing in a Bill for the abolition of fees in State
schools. As a side issue on one of several ques-
tions, this would mean a bonus of 2 \ per cent, to
the man who is trying to serve his country by
drawing the minimum wage of 7s. a day, and
raising a large family on it. He will save a-
shilling a week for the four or more children at-
tending the State school. The other fellow who
draws the screw and refuses to produce the future
voter will have to help pay the bonus. The
said population-builder might also be assisted in
other ways : By being given preference in Govt,
employment —the larger the family the bigger the
preference, as in France; by allowing the afore-
said p.b. to travel at reduced rates (or free would
be better—the writer is a p.b.) on State railways,
as in Denmark ; or in any other way that may
suggest itself to the democratic instincts of the
men who make our laws.

HIE STATEMENT that the Afolian will
play any piece of music ever composed
is so broad and sweeping that it many
times fails to impress the reader with
its wonderful significance. As a means
of affording a better idea of the enormous
repertoire of the vEolian we present herewith
partial list of the works of a single composer,
BEETHOVEN, that are included in the Hfolian
music catalogue.
These are all arranged from the full orchestral
scores, complete and unabridged. Many of them
are never attempted upon the piano, and are played
only by large orchestras ; yet it is possible for any
one to play them on the Afolian correctly, and with
all the changes of tempo and expression.

Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —It is with much gratitude I send you this testi-
monial regarding the great benefit I have received from
the Great Herbal Remedy, W. WEBBER’S VITADATIO.
[ am positive I could not have existed much longer but
for taking it. For 16 years I had been suffering from
some unknown disease. Having a good knowledge of
herbs and their uses, I never consulted a doctor ; in fact,
although I have a large family, and have had the usual
amount of sickness among them, by constantly relying on
herbal treatment I have never had to call in medical aid ;
yet in my own case I was completely nonplussed and
helpless. I may say here that I have been treated by a
noted herbalist, from whom I got but passing relief. He
said “my lungs were filling up.” I have found, since
aking Vitadatio, that what he said was true, for it has
brought from my lungs quantities of hydatids, and also
from other organs of my body ; they even came from my
eyes and head. On account of the stabbing pains in my
eyes when I would stoop, I became afraid of losing my
sight, as it was then very defective. I had lost all sense
of feeling in some parts of my body, and in both my index
fingers and thumbs ; but since taking the Vitadatio my
feeling has been restored. I have been informed since
my recovery, that hydatids are a cause of paralysis,
which has made me still more thankful to you for bring-
ing this efficacious medicine to Australia, and I have
much reason to thank God that I was led to try it. A
good Samaritan, who told me I was in a decline,'bought
me a ss. 6d. bottle of Vitadatio, and when I had taken the
third bottle the hydatids began to appear, and then I
knew what my disease really was. I felt alarmed, but
kept on taking the medicine, as I recognised there was
no other hope for me. After taking ten bottles I found
that I had increased two stone in weight. Previous to
this I only weighed 6st. 41b. The effect it has had on my
nerves is marvellous. I look back on the state I was in
with horror, and I pity any poor sufferer who is in like
condition, and can only recommend them to try
WEBBER’S VITADATIO, for it has cured me, and mine
was an extreme case. If I was to mention the quantity
of hydatids removed from me by Vitadatio, it would be
looked upon as an exaggeration. Before taking the Vita-
datio my household duties were a burden to me, but now
I can fulfil them with pleasure, and everything wears a
bright aspect. Thinking, perhaps, this testimony may
induce other sufferers to try this far-famed remedy, I give
you full permission to publish it if you think fit.—l am,
dear Sir, vours gratefully,
(Mrs.) M. COXHEAD.
For further particulars,
S- A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

There was a queer case heard in Western
Queensland lately. A local Chinaman was
charged with keeping a half-caste girl who was
not his wife. The Chow asserted (with perfect
truth) that he had been to a local parson to get
married and that shepherd had refused to tie the
knot. The Chow was heavily fined —shouldn’t
the parson have been fined ? Some members of
the cloth imagine that a half-caste girl is better
on the streets than legally married.
“ Old Cudgen Resident ” writes : —
In B., 15/6/1, is a par., “The Black Streak in Corn-
stalk Land ” (reprinted from Clarence River Advocate).
This, although full of misrepresentations ( as far as Cud-
gen is concerned), might be ignored as regards its original
publication ; but a very different matter is its dissemina-
tion far and wide per medium of The Bulletin. Let me
say, then, that the assertion, “ There is no Sunday at
Cudgen,” is a deliberate untruth, for no one, either white
or coloured, works on that day at their usual employ-
ment. Indeed, nowhere in N.S.W. is the Sabbath more
strictly observed than at Cudgen. It is true that the
Kanakas generally are so quiet and law-abiding that there
is only one police-officer stationed here during that part
of each year when there are more Kanakas than white
men in residence, but a second constable is always sent
as soon as there is a temporary influx of whites. Again,
the wage paid to Kanakas by Mr. John A. Robb is £1 per
week during crushing-time, and 15s. per week during the
slack season, with rations same as supplied to white men.
The Kanakas also live in barracks, and not in huts, as
stated ; and the yarn about the dirty billy-cans, &c., is a
pure invention.
Writes Conan Doyle : —
Take a community of Dutchmen of the type of those
who defended themselves for fifty years against all the
power of Spain at a time when Spain was the greatest
power in the world. Intermix with them a strain of those
inflexible French Huguenots who gave up home and for-
tune and left their country for ever at the time of the
revocation of the Edict of Nantes. The product must
obviously be one of the most rugged, virile, unconquer-
able races ever seen upon earth. Take this formidable
people and train them for seven generations in constant
warfare against savage men and ferocious beasts, in cir-
cumstances under which no weakling could survive, place
them so that they acquire exceptional skill with weapons
and in horsemanship, give them a country which is emi-
nently suited to the tactics of the the marks-
and the rider. Then, finally, put a finer temper
upon their military qualities by a dour fatalistic Old Tes-
tament religion and an ardent and consuming patriotism.
Combine all these qualities and all these impulses in one
individual, and you have the modern Boer—the most
formidable antagonist who ever crossed the path of
Imperial Britain. Our military history has largely con-
sisted in our conflicts with France, but Napoleon and all
his veterans have never treated us so roughly as these
hard-bitten farmers with their ancient theology and their
inconveniently modern rifles.

Twice last week S.M. Herald referred to
Kingston as “ Sir Charles,” although he has firmly
declined to be so labelled by Chamberlain.
Australian friends of Buchanan, the dis-
tiller and owner of Epsom Lad, are drinking (in his
“B. and W.”) to his recent wins of the Princess
of Wales Stakes, £lO,OOO, and Eclipse Stakes,
another £lO,OOO. Twenty thousand in one week!
They are both weight-for-age events. In the
first he beat the King’s “ Diamond Jubilee.”
Such disloyalty (after Ed. YII appointing him
special whisky-furnisher to the Court) merits a
long and painful execution, such as scraping to
death with oyster-shells. Buchanan’s friends here
are talking of getting him to try an Australian
brumby—a real top-sawyer.
Captain W. T. Wawn, the gallant cheery
skipper who died in Sydney the other day at the age
of 66, owed his death directly to a cab accident,
but he suffered from severe heart-disease and
could not in any event have lasted long. He had
spent most of his life in the South Sea trade, and
even Bully Hayes knew no more than he about
the history of blackbirding. He was a most
accomplished navigator, and a not inconsiderable
artist; also, he had an uncommon gift of graphic
literary expression, and altogether was one of the
most genial companions and best and bravest of
men. Some years ago the bulk of his South Sea
sketches and MSS., representing years and years
of work, were lost in the s.s. Quetta while on
the road to England for publication. But the
veteran bore up bravely under the calamity,
though it wiped out all hope of the fulfilment of
his dearest ambition.
Two members of Vic. Leg. Couucil—
Abbott and Dr. Embling—have had the gall to
propose that the Federal Parliament shall ex-
change domiciles with Vic. State Parliament. The
councillors can’t sleep in their newly-furnished
apartment at the Exhibition Building, and they
pine for their old home. Also, they resent the
idea of any body of men being treated as a matter
of more importance than the 48 haughty Proputty-
owners who block intelligent legislation in Vic-
toria. But they don’t put their grievance in that
way. Abbott, M.L.C., the mouthpiece of Fat-
man’s discontent, professes to be moved by a
desire to rescue the Commonwealth members
from a trying position. They have had the use
of that luxurious club-house at the top of Bourke-
st. “thrust upon them,” and they “find the
courtesy oppressive.” The weight of that courtesy
hurts them, says Abbott, M.L.C. Some of the
Fed. Parliament fellows must have been pulling
the dreary gentleman’s leg.
The Maoriland Federal Inquiry Commis-
sion—consisting mainly of Tories and notorious
anti-Federalists —was appointed to bring in a
verdict that Maoriland shouldn’t join the Com-
monwealth, and it has done so. Being mostly
Tory, it also puts in its report an utterly irrelevant
statement to the effect that Queensland’s sugar
industry can’t survive without black labor—the
perfectly new-chuin members of the Commission
are quite solid on this point. The names of the
persons who pulled the Commission’s .verdant leg
in this matter aren’t supplied. The Commis-
sioners further state that Federation wouldn’t
help M.L. in the conversion of its loans —a subject
a wiser commission would have left alone. Maori-
land, by an awful act of confusion, paid over
£1,600,000 for the privilege of converting a lot of
miscellaneous loans into £21,325,000 of 4 per cent,
stock, fixed till 1929. So for 28 years it pays at
least g' per cent, above the value of the money—a
loss of about £106,000 a year, which, with com-
pound interest, makes a loss of about £5,000,000
more befoi'e the transaction expires. And then,
with over £21,000,000 falling due at once, Maori-
land stands every chance of being badly squeezed
when renewal time comes, if it happens to strike
a bad market.
“ Oh, off to Japan again, of course ! ” was
the universal Sydney comment upon the fact that
Barrister Jack Want had gone North by a Nippon
Yusen Kaisha steamer. And J.H.W. was only
going as far as Brisbane, after all. A notable
case of the “ dog with the bad name.”
“Piles and Their Cure.”—The pamphlet
by Albert Sharp, entitled “ Piles and the Way to
Cure Them,” without operation, sent free, on
application to the Sydney Depot, 257 George-
street.*
Loudly remarked by the landlord that a
big political party in one of the States has for-
gotten to pay its central committee’s rent-bill.
Everybody’s responsibility is the landlord’s
trouble.
When J. 0. Watson, in the House of Re-
presentatives, inquired whether the Federal Govt,
would protest to the Imperial Govt, against the
public execution of rebels in S’Africa, Premier
Barton replied : —
No doubt it will soon be made clear whether the state-
ment is correct. In the meantime the adoption by the
Government of the Commonwealth of the course sug-
gested, with a view to protesting, would amount to the
assumption of a right to inquire officially into the actions
of the Imperial Government in affairs which are not in
the self-governing jurisdiction of the Commonwealth.
Unquestionably the correct view; but what a
slap in the face to colleagues Lyne, Turner, and
Forrest, and to Speaker Holder, who, when Pre-
miers of their respective States, sent a conjoint
cable to Chamberlain requesting that the war be
prosecuted to the bitter end, and intimating that
any terms of peace agreed upon, short of the ex-
tinction of the little S’African Republics, would
be received with indignation in Australia ? Also,
what a snub to Premier Peacock for inviting
Chamberlain, in re Findley, to interfere in “the
self-governing jurisdiction ” of Victoria !
Dawvid Syme was Scotchly cautious in his
dealings wi’ the Findley case, dreading to play
the Argus’ game, and yet fearing to lose a chance
of posing as a prop-and-stay of Tummy’s throne.
This was his only preachment on election morn-
ing
We make no further animadversions on Mr. Findley.
Someone committed a heinous fault in the publication
for which he was responsible. That disgusting blot on
journalism could be punished only in his person.
So Findley Was punished for outraging journalism!
The Findley Blot was not on the Throne and
Person, but a smudge upon the Great White
Press. What Peacock was really inculcating
when he cast the devil out of the swine was re-
spect for Age.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Punters sustained a heavy set back in Canter-
bury (Sydney) Handicap Saturday. Great track galloper,
but utterly unreliable Leap Frog was backed down
to 7to 4 against. Heaps of coin also went on Brazen Lad
(fours; and Ariana (fives), otherwise Leap Frog would have
gone out odds on. Favorite had things his own way until
last I mile, when he cracked up. Lady Darling (6.9) and
Flashlight (outsiders) were first and second, as named,
with a neck between them. Ariana filled third place.
Winner’s performance was full of merit. She tailed right
in the rear ere the first half-mile had been traversed, and
not until last 3fur. did she streak.
* * * *
Lady Darling, winner of Canterbury Park
(Sydney) Handicap, Saturday, is by Nordenfeldt from
Grace Darling, who (starting 100 to 3 against) annexed
mournfully-memorable Caulfield Cup of ’B5, when the
record field (41) lined up. Near the home turn three of
the leading section fell, bringing 13 others atop of them.
Darra was killed on the spot, while Too Too, Tarpeia
Sardius, Prince Imperial and Tom Brown were badly hurt.
Crack lightweight jock Don Nicholson, on Lord Exeter,
died subsequently; and Frank M'Grath. Twomey, E.
M’Grade and Sam Cracknell had a particularly bad time
of it. Grace Darling ran second in following Melb. Cup,
and in ’95 threw Lady Darling at Mr. S. Hordern’s
(N.S.W.) stud farm.
• * * *
Sam Fielder’s fetching-looking Dunlop gelding
Abington (favorite all the week in shops) closed on
Canterbury Flying Handicap (Saturday) so nicely, that
his A.J.O.' Epsom Handicap prospects look rosy. Pro-
vided he strips fit and well, a bit on him would be well
placed as investments turfy go.
$ .V » *
Touts voted Murrumbeena Handicap (lm. and
a fur.) a moral for Royalty, who was 5 to 2 against at
flag-fall, but failed to get placed. A 20-to-l chance, in
the Pilgrim’s Progress nag, Peeress, won, with Fairyland
(10 to 1) second. Caulfield Cup favorite (Sagacity) fell
soon after the start and had to be treated with a friendly
bullet. Only t’other day owner Ike Foulsham refused
£5OO for his speedy galloper. Bay Eagle and Pirate were
hot pots for Ulapna ’Chase. Pirate came a cropper early
and Riecarton (10 to 1) romped home. Scottie, recently
secured by Mr. S. Miller for 205g5., broke his neck.
*•* . *
Amera, winner of Park Stakes, at Canterbury,
Saturday, takes the eye well, is finely muscled on the
back, and evidences unusual power. By Martini-Henry
out of Vain Glory, Amera is full sister to Vanitas and
Dandy. Previously, Messrs. Moses raced her in the
Northern section of N.S.W. Present owner (Mr. A. E.
Anderson, Menangle) will ultimately use her for Stud
purposes.
* * * •
The racing public are queer cattle. At Canter-
bury Park (Sydney), a few weeks back. Peppercorn ran
early in the day, and was beaten, but, in the last race,
came out and won handsomely. Whereupon the crowd
made perhaps the biggest demonstration of disapproval
that has yet been seen on a Sydney suburban course.
Last Saturday, at Canterbury, Dalmore started in the
Flying Handicap, unmentioned in the betting, and ran
nowhere, Royal King finishing second to Abington. In
the last race the public punted on Royal King, on the
strength of his running in the Flying, but Dalmore, who
was heavily backed by his connections at 7’s and B’s, beat
the favorite pointless. And the public took its gruel
without a murmur.
* • • •
Eden George (who biffed Mayor Graham out
of Belmore) owns Far Niente nag Kenley—now top of the
market for coming A.J.C. Epsom Handicap. Stable
“ commish ” worked t’other day secured about £lO,OOO at
hundreds at five. Kenlej r has 7.7. V.R.C. Maiden Plate
and Flying Stakes fell to him at 2yrs, but ten times sub-
sequently he failed to get a “ drum.”
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin.—What shop-betting in Sydney
has really come to. In last Friday’s D.T. a par. appeared,
stating that a horse (which nobody could mistake for
Kenley) had been backed for what money could be got at
5 to 1 for the Railway Stakes at Rosehill before the
weights came out. In this race are 45 entries, and in the
big handicap 22. Yet the “ shops ” are laying the double,
120 to 1 (still before the weights come out), and before
putting their cards up mark off Kenley with everything
having a chance in the other race, and some of the
smaller fry even scratch his name off with everything.
And this is the sort of thing the Federal Parliament
wants to encourage by wiping out a fair deal like Tatter-
sail’s !—G. F. Barrie.
* * • *
English stallion Vendetta, secured for Russley
(N.S. W.) stud, is by Orme (sire of Flying Fox) out of
Nemesis, and was foaled in ’96, so is still a youngster. He
only shaped as a 3-year-old. In 16 starts he got away
with Hurst Park w.f.a. Plate, Folkestone Maiden Plate,
and Rothschild Plate ; was placed in Newmarket Graven
Stakes, Chester Roodee Maiden Plate, Gatwick Marl-
borough Stakes, Winter Manor Plate, Derby Kedleston
Plate, Leicester Melton Plate, and Liverpool Warbreek
Plate. He was therefore 1,2, or 3 ten times. Ormond
(Orme’s sire) credited himself with the 2000g5., Derby,
and St. Leger. Vendetta’s dam—Nemesis—is by St.
Gatien, who dead-heated for the ’B4 Derby, and sired The
Meddler, an ultimate 18,000 gs. purchase. Her dam (Mos-
quito, sister to Musket) gave the world Trafalgar, who
foaled Dreadnought. Vendetta was the only son of Orme
at the stud last season.

In the National Hurdles, Powell, with a head-
wound threatening blood poisoning, rode Shoddy against
medical advice.
* • * •
“Remember Clean Sweep” is this scribe’s
first warning to any person who is hesitating over the
question of an early “ double ” for the Caulfield and
Melbourne Cups. How many thousands of “ doubles ”
were picked last year before the name of Clean Sweep
had been ever mentioned in connection with the 2-mile
race ? The 3-year-old was in the same stable as the sub-
sequent Derby winner, whom he beat easily in the
Melbourne Cup (Nov. 6th), and yet the trainer and owner
of Clean Sweep didn’t think it good enough to pay a 5
sovs. acceptance fee for his Derby chance on the second
Monday in October. “ Remember Clean Sweep ”is good
counsel to the sanguine selector of early “ doubles.”
And a second warning is “ Remember the popular Paris
and Patron pick in ’94.” That was the year when
punters all over the country threw up "their hats
and got drunk. Also they paid for the in-
ebriation of their friends after Patron’s victory
was duly registered. They had picked the right
“ double ” at last. Nothing remained but to sober up
and collect the money. But that was where the trouble
came in. Ikey Mo, in a general way, makes no provision
for the landing of a popular “ double,” and, inasmuch as
every Ikey was hit and none of them could borrow money
from a pal to pay Paris arid Patron pickers, it followed as
a matter of course that the smaller books mostly went in-
solvent and expatiated to their grief-stricken clients on
the foolishness of hoping to get blanky money from a
blanky stone. Of the two evils, backing a popular wrong
double or backing a popular right double, the former is
preferable, because it doesn’t entail a further loss in vain-
glorious refreshments.
« » * *
The Tasmanians, in making their great fight on
behalf of “Tattersall” because his sweep, while it is
located at Hobart, adds £20,000 a year to the Tas. re-
venue, badly failed to understand the probabilities of the
ease. “ Tatt.” only went to Hobart because other States,
In various spasms of morality, declined to deliver his
letters. If the Federal Parliament decides to deliver or
not to deliver “ Tatt.’s ” letters, either decision will pre-
sumably apply all over the Commonwealth. In the first
ease, “Tatt." will probably go back to Sydney as being
larger and more central. In the second case he will pro-
bably go to Europe with his fortune (his occupation
here being gone) and become the Duke of Tattersall, or
the Earl of Sweep, or some such dignitary, and spend the
rest of his days in elevated retirement. There is very
little for Tasmania either Way.
Return Bananaland-N.S.W. toe-ball struggle at
Sydney Agricultural Ground (Saturday) drew 20,000 on-
lookers and a big “gate.” Home side triumphed with a
surplus of 3 -11 to 8. Halloran and White sustained
broken collar bones ; White’s the recurrence of a simple
fracture experienced a year since, and Halloran’s a com-
pound fracture. Initial half, like that of first match, was
comparatively uninteresting, and much below inter-State
rep. form ; but second period made ample amends, and
elicited roars of applause, particularly during the last
half hour. Result was in doubt right to close and excite-
ment waxed hot. N.S.W. forwards won the day by well-
timed and effective work, particularly in the open.
Queensland three-quarters made a better showing than
those of N.S.W., but were more favored by opportunities,
through the fine services of halves Gralton and Evans.
The visitors let many simple chances slip, principally
through Dickson’s mulling at torrid stages.
“ Punt ” : Q. Rugby team, last week in Sydney,
lost both inter-State events, but beat Metrop. Union by
29 to 16. List of matches played now totals 46, of which
N.S.W. has gathered in 28 and Q. 15 ; other three have
been drawn. I tip that next month’s play in
the Northern State capital will produce slightly different
results, especially as a N.S.W. team is at the same time
due in M.L., and the material available to draw upon is
not thoroughly representative.
• * * *
The most popular player engaged in the N.S.W.
v. Q. football contests was young Finley, who played
“ behind the scrum ” for the home team. He is a mere
lad of 18, and was discovered among the players from New
England during country-week. His father is a keen ’ball
enthusiast, and came all the way from Tamworth, where
he is chairman of the local Land Board, to see his boy
play. Finley, jun., is one of the best scrum halves play-
ing at present, and to him N.S.W. is much indebted for
her two successes against the Northern State.
* ♦ * *
Boer example has stimulated ’Ome rifle-shoot-
ing. In the Elcho Shield contest, between volunteer
. teams representing England, Scotland and Ireland, the
scores showed all-round improvement on last year’s
figures, when Ireland Won with 1537 points. This year
England got 1609, and Ireland, with 1585, was beaten
into third place by Scotland.
* * * *
Tho League of Victorian Wheelmen has granted
patronage to the “ Dunlop Road Race,” which is to be
run on Saturday, the 12th October. This fine road con-
test Will be held over the famous Warrnambool to Mel-
bourne course, a distance of 165 miles. The event will be
a handicap one, the starts for which will be allotted by
.the L.V.W. Kandicapper. No pacing will be allowed, ex-
cepting the assistance one competitor may render another.
The prize list is a very extensive one, no less than 10
prizes, of a total value of £llO, being given by the trade.

* • • •
Second Cribb-Dunn bruising match, Monday
night, filled Gaiety A.O. (Sydney), at double prices. One-
time amateur heavyweight Harry Beckett made the best
referee seen in Sydney for years. Cribb was favorite, and
had the better of first four rounds, when he fell off and
never recovered ; while Dunn, who frequently appeared
about to break-up, pulled himself together remarkably,
and, in spite of a maimed left from the fourth, won in
the ninth round. Oribb was counted out. literally
dead-beat, through tremendous but futile efforts to land
on t’other, who fought far better than in first fight.
Winner’s grit and condition stood to him well in hot
corners. He weathered occasions when there seemed
absolutely no hope for him. Oribb often punched away
while Dunn, half dazed and dog-weary, couldn’t hold his
hands up to shield himself. The M.L. man went to
pieces with a run, while Dunn’s preparation enabled him
to pick up a little every time Cribb stood off, preliminary
to making another furious onslaught. The punching on
either side was not over stiff, and it was painfully evident
that America did poor Cribb no good. Whether the two
hammerings and one defeat at ’Frisco-hoodlum Thur-
ston’s hands, or the allurements of the country were
responsible doesn’t appear, but it is beyond question
that Oribb on Monday night was not the Cribb of old.
* * * *
Some hours after the fight trainer M'Carthy,
lying alongside Oribb at the Cliff Hotel, Bondi, experi-
enced a sensation of cold from the other’s body, and, fail-
ing to arouse him or induce any symptoms of life, called
in a doctor, who said he hid been d.-ad for some time.
The cadaverous appearance of Crihb’s body and his pallid
face were noted by the most unobservant at the match.
Most Dunn money went on b cause it was contended that
two terrible poundings by Frisco Thurston must have
broken the Australian. He looked nothing like his former
self when he returned. During the fight all steam
and punching power completely left him. Time
after time, Dunn was absolutely at his mercy, but Cribb
couldn’t get sufficient powder behind the blows to send in
anything effective. After the fourth round Dunn was
unable to use his left, yet he still stood up. This was not
Cribb as Sydney knew him when he defeated Sturgeon,
Dunn and Co. last year. M’Carthy says the dead man had
complained of severe pains in the back and around the
kidneys, which were so distressing that he had to use
something that would act as a pad before he could lie on
his back, and M'Carthy had frequently to rub that region
with embrocations. Also, now told by Johns and Stone-
ham (recently back from ’Frisco) that Cribb suffered
much long after his second Thurston battle. Sugges-
tions that the match be postponed, or that he should
wear a plaster, found no favor with Cribb, for he
was very stubborn. At time of writing post-mortem
hadn’t been held, hence the exact cause of death
was unknown—but it would be surprising if actual
injury in the ring were at the bottom of it.
* * * *
’Frisco bruiser Riley met Charley Frost at
Golden Gate A.O. (Syd.), Saturday night. For two
rounds Riley held his own, then Frost worried him, and
slugged him right out. Riley was gritty, came up thrice
when it seemed all over, and it is generally opined he
should get into good fettle and try again, as it is evi-
dently in him to make a much bolder bid.
* * * *
It was a short, sweet battle at Vic. Democratic
Hall on Monday night, 15th inst., when Tot Higgins, a
light-weightwithapast, met Jock MTvor, a lighter weight
with a future. The “ boys ” are the choice spirits of rival
Port Melbourne pushes, and fought for love and the pot,
especially for love, and the mill went merrily from the
call. MTvor proved himself the master man from the
first prod, and hammered half the grit out, of his
opponent ere the initial 3min. were spent. Nathless,
Higgins kept things going at a great gait during six
rounds, being knocked pretty silly two or three times
before he decided that MTvor’s peculiar dropping left
and his swinging upper-cuts were very indigestible.
MTvor won his best battle without taking any particular
damage, and improved his position vastly by the vim and
science he displayed. Jack M’Gowan’s next shake-up
with MTvor ought to be a stirring engagement.
* * • *
Sydney Sports Club, in the coming State
amateur premiership, will rigidly enforce the strict defi-
nition of an amateur for the first time in N.S.W. boxing
history. Closing of entries is some weeks off, but names
already sent in indicate the tourney will extend over
several nights. ’Varsity, Scotch, and other colleges are
the largest contributors, there are also pupils of Larry
Foley and George Seale. The management is looking
round for a large and suitable hall, as space available at
the Sports Club will be nothing like adequate. So far,
N.S.W. has not had a genuine amateur champion, though
many have, at different times, assumed the honor.
Apropos, the personnel of this year’s batch of Bananaland
top-notchers doesn’t include one who owned the distinc-
tion before ; no previous winner entered.
Obsequies of Peter Jackson in Roma (Q.) at
night were weirdly picturesque and most impressive. A
lengthv procession followed the hearse to the station en
route to Brisbane. The Mayor and prominent town
people acted as pall-bearers. Members of local athletic
clubs, headed by the town band, carried flaming torches.
All societies and bodies of the district were largely rep-
resented. Shops along the route closed and put up their
shutters. The main street was thronged with people.
Funeral aortege in Brisbane, where the dead was person-
ally almost unknown, extended three-quarters of a mile.
The body was interred in Toowong Cemetery on a hill
overlooking the city.
• . * * *
“ Friend ”: Noticing Peter Jackson’s death last
week, Sydney Evenin’ Nooze lifted over a j-col. from a
’Frisco paper by a whilom Sydney scribe now in Yankee-
dom. Fight between Jackson and Dooley (upwards of 16
years since) was referred 1 bo, and deliberate misstatements
made about Peter guzzling beer at Canterbury Races in
the afternoon of the convincing day, and late Mr. George
Hill (alleged hacker of Jackson) remonstratively ad-
dressing Peter as “you black blanker,” “you black
beauty,” &c., and Jackson replying “ I ain’t goin’ to fight
to-night, sah,” &c., &c. The tale is a fabrication. George
Hill never backed Jackson on that occasion ; so far from
supporting Peter he was on the other side. No man ever
dared address Jackson as a “ black beauty” or “black
blanker.” Peter would have resented it right off. Again,
there was nothing of the “ corner man ” about his style
of speaking. His English Was acquired in an English
school from infancy and under British rule. Jackson
never trained harder for his biggest fight, recognising as
he did that the outcome would either make or mar him.
* * * »
Sydney Referee appeals to the continent for a
monument over the grave of pugilist Jackson. And
Peter Was Worthy of it. He Was a man among men, the
greatest boxer and one of the straightest goers ever
known. Also, he Was actually Champion of the World
twice ; first, through John L. Sullivan, after signing
articles, failing to meet him j and, secondly, when Jim
Corbett also thought better Of it. Though not native-
born, Peter always claimed Australia as his home, on the
grounds that he came an unsophisticated lad and
acquired his world-famous art here. Something after the
style of the memorial stone over the grave of his great
prototype “ Gentleman ” Jackson, or that of Tom Sayers,
in England, is proposed.
* * * *

refused to go further when his man was palpably help-
less, as witness every fight he engaged in—notably those
with Lees, Cardiff and Slavin.

NEW SOUTH WALES
PATRIOTIC E UNI).
REPORT OF EXECUTIVE COMMITTEE FOR YEAR ENDING 30th JUNE, 1901,
PRESENTED TO AND ADOPTED BY THE GENERAL COMMITTEE ON 18tII JULY, 1901.
Your Executive Committee have met fortnightly throughout the year now reported on, added to which th«.
chairman has been almost daily consulted by the secretary.
Since the initiation of the Fund, seven hundred and seventeen (717) individual cases have been dealt with.
Many of the applicants have applied more than once for assistance, and many of the cases had to be temporarily dealt
with, and considered and reconsidered, before final decisions were arrived at.
Over eight hundred (800) circular letters for reference have been sent out in connection with these applications,
while, in addition thereto, the results of applications from the country have entailed a further amount of outward
correspondence.
The reference replies, military and medical reports in each instance, have been carefully checked, and every case
indexed up, and dealt with on the merits disclosed.
Much of the time of your Executive has been taken up, and a considerable amount of labor thrown on the Secre-
tary and his Olerk, by the full reports necessary in dealing with applications for “ Further Relief,” by far the greater
number of which your Executive have had to refuse.
The wide scope of the Fund, wisely provided for at its initiation, has allowed of relief being afforded to members
Of the China Contingent, as well as to all the New South Wales Contingents to South Africa.
The total amount of Donations to the Fund since its initiation is £50,730 14s. 3d., and interest £1629 16s. 2d.
The amount disbursed in relief from Ist July, 1900, to 29th June, 1901, was
£12,184 15 0
Prior to that it was 255 6 0
Making a grand total since the initiation of the fund of .. . .£12,440 0 0
Thus made up :
13 Widows ..£1,184 15 0
65 Other Dependents .. .. ... 2,132 0 0
639 Returned Wounded and Invalided 9,123 5 0
£12,440 0 0
Leaving a balance to the credit of Fund (inclusive of Fixed Deposits) of £39,536 Is. sd.
The Executive Committee have to express their thanks to the Military Authorities for their assistance in giving
Military and First Medical Reports, and for the courtesy extended to the Secretary when making necessary inquiries
at the Barracks ; also to Captain E. J. Thomas, Transvaal and China Contingents Inquiry Officer, for his assistance
in making inquiries,
Thanks have to be accorded to the Sydney Chamber of Commerce for the use of their office, and to Mr. David
Fell (0.A.A.) for again gratuitously auditing the accounts.
During the year it was found necessary, in dealing with applications for Further Relief, to appoint a Medical
Practitioner, to whom the Executive could send applicants for “ Further Relief ” to be examined as to their ability
or otherwise to work.
As provided for under the Bylaws, a Statement of Receipts and Expenditure has to be published in the month ol
July in each year.
This Statement, duly signed by the Trustees and Audited by Mr. David Fell (0.A.A.), is now placed before you,
and will be published as required by the Bylaws.
(Sgd.) AUGUSTUS F. ROBINSON,
Chairman of Executive Committee.
Sydney, 29th June, 1901.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
King Ned is to receive from the British
Treasury £470,000 a year—an increase of £85,000
on Queen Victoria’s screw. In addition, he gets
£OO,OOO from the Duchy of Lancaster. The Duke
of Cornwall and York gets £20,000 and the
Duchess £lO,OOO a year, and the Jook’s revenue
from the Duchy of Cornwall is now £70,000 per
annum, so that this young couple have to struggle
along on £lOO,OOO a year. The King’s three
daughters get £6OOO each ; Duke of Connaught,
£25,000.
From Melb. Age :
The late Queen had a passion for shutting up rooms in
which relatives, friends and favorite servants had died.
The spacious apartment occupied by John Brown at
Windsor Castle has been rigorously closed for 18 years, and
a brass tablet, recording the date of his demise, lamenting
his loss, and commemorating his virtues, was put up on
the wall by the Queen. This room has now been re-
opened and cleared out, and it will be entirely re-
decorated.
King Ned seems to be a most irreverent person-
age.

Dr. Andrew Wilson, the well-known writer
on popular hygiene, recently, in Lloyd’s Weekly,
recommended to dyspeptics the use of Australian
wines especially the clarets. As Lloyd’s
Weekly has a circulation of a million, this should
be a good advt. for our winegrowers.
At a Temuka (M.L.) private hospital
lately, the surgeons removed four inches of nerve
with a tumor from a man’s leg ; a sheep was
killed on the premises, and a nerve extracted and
grafted into patient’s leg. The grafting proved
successful, and the man is now hobbling about on
a stick.
Ed. Bulletin. — A correspondent (8.,
8/6/’01) states that N.S.W. was the first country
in the world to adopt the system of prepaying
letters by means of postage-stamps. Now, post-
age-stamps were first used in England in 1840,
whereas they were not introduced in N.S.W. till
’49.—J.W.C. [Wrong. N.S.W. had its first
postage-stamps in 1837, and that was the world’s
record so far as is known. They were then sold
as impressed covers or envelopes. The adhesive
stamp came in 1849 — Ed. B.[

More evidence tendered to the Victorian
Shops and Factories Commission in Sydney :
John English (foreman draper at Edward Hordern’s and
vice-president of the Early Closing Association) said that
the Early Closing Act had proved itself of immense
benefit to shop assistants, while it caused neither loss to
the employers nor inconvenience co the public. In
clothing factories he knew of apprentices who were
started at as low as 2s. 6d. per week, and there were
numbers of female workers, first-class hands, who, after
five or six years’ service, were only getting 10s. per week.
The only remedy he could suggest for that state of thing-!
was minimum-wage legislation.
God bless the Foreign-trade doctrine of Cheap
ness ! Behind every cheap coat there is a cheap,
man, or a cheaper woman.

Mr. W. G. Hearne.—Dear Sir,—l am writing to tell you
about the wonderful cure your medicine has effected in
niy case. About three years ago I began to cough. At
first the cough was not severe, but it gradually got worse,

TT is an acknowledged fact the world-wide over that no
single individual or combination of men can he ab-
solute masters of every phase and branch of art, science,
medicine, or other professed avocation of a limitless
sphere of operation and magnitude. At best the greatest
scientists of the day—men like Lord Kelvin and Edison,

HO WA RT> FREEMAN,
Sydney’s Leading Specialist.
Professor of Electricity as applied Medically, Member

Director and Founder of the Institute.
for instance, whose fame is universally known —class
themselves as infants in scientific invention and research,
and modestly tell us that they have hut a smattering of
knowledge of the possibilities, extent, and development
We have only to look at the fine buildings that occupy
the corner of Elizabeth and Bathurst streets to gain some
idea of the extent and importance of their professional
work. But to have the privilege of being shown through
the building by the Specialists themselves, and to see and
learn something of the real character of their wide prac-
tice, is a treat that only comes the way of a fortunate in-
dividual like the writer.
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for eyeing
and examining the polished steel articles with which so
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. The collection in this room is in every way up-to-
date. Whenever a new instrument comes to the front,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Gaiffe
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary in main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence department is a veritable eve-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
glass doors. Half a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are known the names of the persons throughout Aus ralia
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the W’ork of one department is a
mystery to the others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, for safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feelings and
sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are in
view.
What a practice is here revealed and what a consola-
tion must it be to these eminent specialists to know that
they are permanently curing and restoring to health and
happiness thousands of people whose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. The pro-
digious nature of the work accomplished by these
specialists necessitates —not the stereotyped morning and
evening two hours’ consultation ticket, but their constant
attention at the institution from early morning till late
into the night. “ I can assure you,” said Dr. Wallace,
“ we don’t put in these long hours because we are fonder
~
'
*•>
Premises : Oor. Elizabeth and Bathurst Sts. (Opp. Hyde Park, Sydney
(All Trams Stop at the Door).
of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of the un-
told stores of strength and wealth wrapt up in this
phenomenal power.
Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions—that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “gullibles” are not so plentiful as they used to
be, the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
ability.
What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why ?
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at once
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows.
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
he pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
graetice as that conducted by the Freeman-Wallace
lectro Medical and Surgical Institute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-
conveniences for our patients’ sakes.”
Noble sentiments, thought the writer, as the time came
for his departure, and the Specialists returning to their

The Vic. artists have in part atoned for
their snobbish action in creating “ academicians,”
by their decision to throw open their galleries free
to the public on Sunday nights, during the cur-
rency of the present exhibition. This is a slap on
the eye for the Sabbatarian crowd, who prevent
the opening of Vic. National Gallery on Sunday. ,
A few years back the Sabbatarians swore that the
people of Melb. were too godly to visit the Na-
tional Gallery on Sunday, and dared the authori-
ties to open it, as a test. The challenge was taken
up, and an average of 5000 people swelled in each
Sabbath. The pious brigade immediately shifted
their ground, and pleaded that the challenge wins
made by a wholly unauthorised person, and exerted
so much influence on Sargood, Harper, and Go.
that the galleries were suddenly shut, and have
ever since remained so.
Still waiting. Vic. Parliament ran-out
Labor-member Findley on the ground that a paper
with which he was-nominally connected published
a “seditious libel.” If it was a seditious libel
there is plenty of law in Victoria to get at the
real offender—the editor of the Tocsin. The
Peacock Government shows no sign of gomg-for
the offender in the law-courts, where he can
defend himself. If this is because it knows that
the article complained of was not a seditious libel,
then the Peacock Government in general is a liar.
If it is because it doesn’t like to fight in an arena
where the other party can defend himself, then it
is only a coward. If it is because it doesn t really
care, despite all its recent gas, whether the
is libelled or not, then it is a hypocrite, blow,
which is it ? It can be either of them without any
extra charge.

offering 2s. Bd. for prime, and 2s. 7d. for f.a.q. per bushel.

Brisbane wool is quiet. London reports firm market for
fine merinos. Marsupial Skin market steady. Grey
kangaroos inclined to harden, prices in sellers favor
Scrub wallabies slid down another 3 d. per skin while

doz. Hair, horns and bones, dull and dower than last
week In sheepskins, well-dried samples had a steady

Recent scientific inquiry on the alcoholic
question shows that good whisky provides nourish-
ment and increases vital action. In other words,
it supplies both fuel and steam for the human
machine, whereas bad whisky merely provides a
distorted liver and a dishevelled mind.
fulfills the essential condition—it is a good whisky,
and taken judiciously supplies not only force and
fuel but keeps the human machine well oiled, re-
gulated, and in good repair. It’s quality is known
by the intellectual calibre of the veterans who
prefer it to all other brands—the whisky of the
wise.*
“ Z.” : Are the benevolent asylums to
be crowded with starving pianoforte-teachers,
robbed of their living by the pianola? (liy
the way, I notice that “a Swiss gentleman,
thorough musician,” advertises in S.M. Herald,
“ two piano lessons weekly for Is. od. )
To outward seeming the pianola is a
double - headed sewing-machine, which fastens
its teeth on to the front of the piano, the space
between the two covered heads being occupied by
a rolling pin, round which revolves the mss. of
the music, which is represented by runs and dots.
The machinery was carefully explained to
me, after which, of course, it was as great a
mystery as ever. There remains the fact, how-
ever, that the player may merely move the
treadles with his feet, his hands in his pockets,
and invisible fingers will be heard chasing them-
selves up and down the unseen keys. If he wants
expression as well as accuracy, however, he moves
little bars up and down with his hands, his feet
still working at the treadles, passages being played
in a manner which the most accomplished, hand-
work would never execute. A week’s tuition on
the instrument will make anyone a delightful
player, without any previous knowledge of the
piano.

At Poverty Point.
Miss Leonore Orelli, who was engaged as chorus lead
and understudy for Williamson’s Italian Opera, died sud-
denly of heart disease, the other Sunday. Some years
ago, the fair-haired Melbourne girl had a remarkably fine
voice, and given good health she might have become one
of Australia’s famous singers. But from tne time of her
first amateur appearance on the concert platform Miss
Orelli had bad luck. She caught a cold, which brought
on rheumatic fever, and the fever returned more than
once. Hence the heart trouble, of late perceptible in her
speaking tones. The Firm gave her a first professional
show as the Fairy Queen in the original production of
“ Djin Djin. Last Xmas she was another Fairy Queen in
Musgrove’s gorgeous panto, at Melb. Princess’s. People
used to doubt the genuineness of Leonore Orelli’s name,
though it came to her naturally through a Swiss-Italian
father.
Died last week, at Sydney, Fred Hiseocks, who came to
Australia nearly 30 years ago as an advertising-map can-
vasser. He was afterwards associated with the sainted
“ Jamie ” Allison in a theatrical spec., and, later, with Al.
Hayman in the management of the Mammoth Minstrels —
combined, in ’BO, with the Loftus Variety Co. Hiseocks
took the combination to England, the late “ Dick ”
Thatcher going as agent; but, as the enterprise was not
a success, H. returned to Australia and organised the
Federal Minstrels, which had a long and successful run at
the Castlereagh-st. Academy of Music, upon the site of
which the Tivoli now stands. Afterwards he brought out
the London Pavilion Co., of which John Fuller (present
lessee of the Empire) and Jolly Nash were members.
Hiseocks made money with the Federal Minstrels, but
was afterwards unlucky, and his latter years were not
too prosperous. One of his daughters is married to Harry
Hodson, a well known actor.
The man at the other end of the cable, apparently,
thinks that what Australia wants isn’t so much the
world’s news as prompt information when some obscure
Australian in Fog Oit.v gets a job, or a cold, or any other
small thing. The other day he wired that an actress from
this end had got an engagement. It was only after hard
thinking that the average Sydney or Melbourne man
could remember that he used now and then to see that
lady’s name in some small part, with long disappearances
between. The London manager who has engaged her is,
apparently, an obscure individual, and the kind of part
or the kind of salary she has got is left to the imagina-
tion.
Dear Bulletin. —Am rather a lover of the genuine
“ coon ” song, but Mr. Billy McClain’s co.ored ditties at
Sydney Tiv. are- too darned realistic ! Will Rickards, or
Leete, or Barrington, or somebody, kindly invite Mr.
M'Lain to sing or talk something the average person can
understand? His latest ditty sounds something like
this :—“ Booooarrrr —whooh —vowh ; bioarrr eloooarrr
—bedoooo—ah ; meee—voooo—shrooooh—doooeeee—roo-
loooarrrh.” —6 x 8.
“Jim Belmont,” the drama - comedietta which Miss
Jennie Maynard and Co. produced at Melb. Princess s last
week, is a pretty little thing—just what might be ex-
pected from the authoress (Mrs. Oscar Beringer) of A
Bit of Old Chelsea.” Unconvincing but pleasing ; bright
but essentially stagey ; therefore a most difficult piece to
be tackled by an amateurish company. The more ex-
perienced of them—Miss Maynard, Frank Hawthorne,
Carden Wilson, and Ludlow couldn’t preserve the weakest
members from the cheerful derision of the ‘ 1 gods, though
they made the story interesting. The main character,
Jim, is a music-hall girl, and it suggests the question of
Marie Lloyd being able to play it. Are there any possi-
bilities of serious emotional acting in Marie ? Certainly
Jennie Lee of yore could have given a clever sketch of the
“serio-comic” who comes to impossible unhappiness
through marriage with a baronet.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent:. The frost of
Irving’s “ Coriolanus ” was foreguessed by a super, at one
of the rehearsals when Sir ’Enery, Sir Lawrence Alma-
Tadema and Sir Alex. Mackenzie were yarning in the
stage-centre. “ Three knights ! ” sneered the supe., and
that’s just as long as the play will run. He was
just about right. The Sydney-sider, A. L. Baird, who has
been actor and manager on a melodram. tour is moving
along. He goes out “provincing” to play in ihe
Awakening ” and other society comedies. Ada Coljey,
yclept “ The Australian Nightingale,” is at the Empire ;
Harry Gribben, fat and unrecognisable, disguised as a
“ prosperous English gentleman ” is being.nibbled at by
the Gaiety Co., and the Permans are booming.”
Writes “ Phelo ” from Kalgoorlie : Charles Arnold has
been doing bouncing biz here with “ What Happened to
Jones.” Sheridan has also scored well with the immortal
“Widow,” though Rignold, following on John L. s heels,
fared but indifferently. Rignold, at the request of gold-
fields “ culchaw,” sandwiched a night or two ot
“Othello” between melodramas. Next, the Janet Wal-
dorf Co with “Ingomar” provided, at least, merriment
for the “gods and lastly the Dryblowers have been
sampling the alleged phenomenal feats of the Georgia
Magnet and the vibrations of Wyld and Freedmans
British Biograph. There is a great opening in the West
for a manager with a good co. and good plays, who could
judiciously divide his time between the fields and Perth.
But third-rate stuff won’t go down here.
Brisbane Evenino Observer applied a couple of most
ancient “ chestnut” yarns to the recent Musgrove Grand
Opera Season. One was the story of how a woman in the
dress circle, who hadn’t expected a sudden silence of the
orchestra, was heard saying to her fair neighbor, “Well,
I always wash and iron Fred's shirts myself. „ nc *
other was the hoary Dublin anecdote of the gallery boy
crying, “ Begorra 1 Hell’s full,” when Faust and Mephis-
topheles stuck half-way in their trap-door journey down
the hole where the steam comes from. The Observer
man must have bought a book of “ Funny Tales for the
Fireside.”
Bland Holt, who paid £5OO, plus a percentage of the
gross receipts, for the Australian right of producing
“ The Great Ruby,” is not making haste to stage any of
the other new and expensive melodramas which figure on
his list. But he will revive “ The White Heather at
Melb. Royal when “A Million of Money” has been put
through.
“P.H.0.": During the performance of “The Second
in Command ” it was noticed that the Broughs invariably
pronounced the word “ veldt ”as “ pfeldt ” which is, no
doubt, correct —while Cecil Ward stuck to the “ v sound.
What a subtlety it would have been if Ward had pro-
nounced it incorrectly only in the first three acts that
is, before he went to the S.A. war—and as pfeldt in
the last act, it being understood that a Boer had taught
him the correct pronunciation in the interval ! But per-
haps not two people in the house would have noticed it.

Messrs. Lesleigh Mullarky, Russell Rogers, and Golden i
and by the Misses Carle Doyle, Rose Guest, and Violet
Gillespie. Mr. Leonard Gill, of M.L., is manager.


TWENTY YEARS
KIDNEY'-LIVER TROUBLE
SICKENING GNAWING ACHES.
ENERGY, FLESH & STRENGTH RESTORED
BY CLEMENTS TONIC.
The Case of M r. G. HARDIN.
- (By a Dunedin Reporter.}
People who become nauseated with
their trades and professions and leave
them for the purpose of launching out
into a new sphere of action find their
paths bestrewn with many obstacles,
the existence of which they little
dreamed of. Although probably com-
petent to fill positions which they apply
for, they usually learn that preference
is given, to those possessing previous ex-
perience. An applicant with five years’
insight into a business commands an ad-
vantage over one with twelve months’
knowledge, and so on; therefore, when
we find a gentleman with twenty years’
experience on any subject, he is always
worth listening to. Our reporter held
this view when he called on Mr. George
Hardin, of No. 33 l'orth-street, Dune-
din, who stated that he had suffered for
twenty years from'kidney and liver dis-
uses, and that many complications had
risen therefrom.
An account of your troubles ought
very interesting,' Mr. Hardin.
•™you P 1 * 9 * 6 t 0 them?”
% .all means, as I am sure I am in
possession of facts which will prove
beneficial to others. It is just as much
py duty to put folks on the right track
p regard to disease as it would be to
direct ayight a, stranger who was on the
wrong road to the destination at which
he wished to arrive. To begin with, I
may state that I am an engineer by pro-
fession, and I am of opinion that through
working for so many years in extremely
hot atmospheres accounts for my kid-
neys becoming diseased. My back com-
menced to worry me with sickening,
gnawing aches, and before long I fell
nto a very low condition. I was afflict-
ed with a distressing sensation which
made me feel as though I would rather
i® down than stand up, walk, or any-
thing else. A scalding pain was ex-
perienced when urinating, the water
oemg olouded with a peculiar sediment.
1 used to suffer dreadfully with my
head. It was not an ordinary head-
ac " e which continually attacked me, but
a throbbing, thumping pain that threat-
ened to drive me mad. As a matter of
tact, 1 do not believe there was one por-
tion of my body free from some kind of
o g °? y ' , , My faoul ties seemed to be
-leadened by the excessive torture after
my meals, which I took very irregularly.
L have seen myself go for days and
want nothing to eat, then, quite sud-
denly, I would sit down and eat like a
gormandizer. After theso heavy re-
pasts my life was not worth, living, for
the terrible pains which came behind
my neck, close to the shoulders, were
more than I could bear in silence. A
lunocating feeling very often nearly
made me faint. This arose from the
dead weighty fulness in my chest,
which f®lt as though compressed within
1 -!7 oe l * Th , en 1 a S ain went for days
without food owing to the merciless tor-
■ tures I was put to,”
‘ ■ Under these circumstances yon must
aaye been considerably seduced in
weight!”
J C i?A r . fcainly l W !f- I was almost

myself, as I was not able to walk to
the scales. You have no idea how weak
4 got, and considering I could not keep
anything on my stomach I was not sur-

had the least indication of being indi-
ces lble, yet it was all the same to my
poor weak stomach, Up it came again
ilmost before it. had got down, and then
the cruel pamsnn my head were intensi-
Ped. I was m such a terrible state that
I was nearly off my head. All day long
I was sleepy and dull, without any d«£
sire to try and shake off the feeling of
despondency under which I labour-
ed. I seemed to give myself up
lor oke remainder of my days to
rm existence entirely destitute of
aappmess, the secret being that I
aad not enough vitality or will-
>ower to .continue to live in hope.
Worse than allrl was unable to sleep at
aghfc. For dayg and nights I have seen
niyself go without getting one hour’s
sleep, and nave been in such a bad state
when lying in bed that I have been com-
pletely doubled up with the paralysing
pains in my back. There was one period
in the history of my sufferings when I
used to go off to sleep regularly every
third night, but for all the good it did
me X would have been better awake.
These slumbers were embraced simply
through overpowering fatigue, and
while they lasted I dreamt the most hor-
rible dreams. I did not feel the least
bit rested or refreshed on awaking, but
was always bathed in a cold, clammy
perspiration. An extraordinary giddi-
ness used to suddenly seize me, when
my head seemed to spin round like a
top and everything became indistinct
before my eyes, which were swollen and
weak. The rest of my ailments were
added to by a total collapse of my ner-
vous system, which made me very sen-
sitive and irritable whenever the least
noise was made. I used to feel my
hands and limbs quivering and trem-
bling like a frightened child’s. My tem-
per was very excitable and repellent,
the most trifling detail of imaginary
neglect being magnified into a serious
wrong. I was living in, Queenstown
; when my illness started, but soon after
| I came down to Dunedin, where I met
|an old friend who said : ‘ Hello! What
|is the matter with you? You look as
I if you were dying!”
, You probably felt like it, to judge
I from your sufferings!” remarked the
! reporter.
~ “ Ind f ed 1 did. Many a time I
! bought I would never get better. I
waa wonderfully changed in appearance
being without the least colour in my
face, besides having my cheeks sunk
right in. My palate had a most dis-
agreeable flavour, even a glass of water
having a bitter taste, and my tongue
was coated white with an odorous mois-
ture. As I grew older the more acute
were my sufferings. My doctor said he
did not know whether my diseases were
getting any worse or whether I felt the
effects more severely through m v svs-
tern getting weaker with the advance of
[ age. He used to say I could not expect
to be as we l at fifty as I was at twenty •
but now I feel as well as ever I did in
I my life.”

flow was your health restored?”

ionic, even after my doctor’s treat-

ong since concluded there was no hope

1 wa * struck with astonishment

thought if other folks could be cured

chance for me. You may rest assured

Victorian Lands-Minister Duggan has one
brilliant idea, anyhow. He is promoting a Bill
under which, when any big landholder dies and
his heirs declare the value of his land for the pur-
pose of paying probate duty thereon, the Govern-
ment may compulsorily buy the land for closer settle-
ment purposes at their valuation with 10 per cent,
added for disturbance. If it passes it will not only
help on settlement, but it will abate the atrocious
frauds perpetrated on the State by the under-
valuation of land forprobate purposes. Of course
it won’t pass if the House of Rich Landlords can
prevent it. The landlord class always swindles
the State on the probate business.
“ H.P.” : When the coal mine at Outtrim
(Yio.) was being opened-up several leading union-
ists from the other mines near by got employment
there. They were promptly presented with bits
of ground on which to build humpies. Result:
Union leaders were silenced and their former
aggressive policy knew them no more. In Vic.
Railway Department leading union officials have
frequently obtained good billets—thereafter they
have been as silent as the grave, and new leaders
had to be sought. ‘ Same kind of thing is heard of
in England and elsewhere. Most people have
their price—and what a lot of darned cheap people
this world does hold 1

LIVER LOGIC
The Liver is the mainspring of the system.
When active it is the great purifier.
When sluggish it easily develops a poisonous
tendency.
The Liver both secretes and excretes.
It secretes about two or three pounds of bile daily.
It excretes impurities and waste materials.
The Liver is the sewage farm of the body.
If the Liver is sluggish it cannot exoel its refuse,
and as it must go somewhere it is absorbed back into
the blood, causing biliousness and often jaundice.
The Liver also makes from the blood a kind of
sugar, which, if healthy, is used up as nourishment.
If the sugar is excessive or inferior it cannot be so
used, it then escapes into the urine, causing diabetes.
A Torpid Liver develops gout and rheumatism in
predisposed subjects.
The absolute, incontestable remedy for all this,
and the one which never fails is Nature’s Great
Restorative, Kutnow’s Improved Effervescent Carls-
bad Powder.
This Powder is made by an ingenious expedient
from the health-giving medicinal springs of the
Continental Spas.
It is improved by removing all drastic or lowering
properties.
It is gentle and pleasant, but the greatest of all
Stimulators of the Liver.
