The Pruning-knife.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Randall had come into the hospital two weeks
before—a nervous wreck of a man who would
have been a museum of diseases had he really
possessed all the ailments he fancied. His hypo-
chondriac imagination brooded over the thought
of imminent Death.
“ Enlarged liver,” said the Doctor, as he dug
his thumb remorselessly into the patient’s ribs
with a callousness born partly of familiarity with
pain (in others), and partly of that inheritance of
contemptuous brutality common to the physically
perfect. A toothache drove him half crazy, but it
seemed to his patients that he could have cut his
father’s heart out with less compunction than one
would feel in killing a chicken.
The men’s ward was empty when Randall
arrived, and he lay there during a fortnight,
pining for the sympathy that a recital of his ills
might bring, and telling himself the tale of a
sickly past during the long, sleepless nights. The
awful harshness of the Doctor choked his speech.
He felt instinctively the repulsion existing
between them. His feeble plaints had met with
cheap discouragement. Another patient would
have been a godsend ; but none came.
Daily, at regulated hours, Randall underwent
the torture —at first on his back in the bed ; later,
standing up and holding to a familiar and hated
strap, hanging close to the head of his bed.
Tincture of iodine was applied with an unmerciful
brush to a patch on the right side, sensitive now
as the raw of a freshly-cut wound.
On that day the Doctor came in, carelessly
cutting a pipeful with a pruning-knife. He was
fond of gardening, and had just been trimming his
rose-trees. He greeted the patient with a sharp,
“Get ready!” as he laid pipe and knife down.
The man moaned, half-rose in the bed, and, with
an invalid’s pettishness, “Can’t you leave it be
to-day, Doctor ? ”
“No! I can’t!” Then, with a fierce look of
contempt at the miserable wretch, “ Damn me, if
I think you’re worth saving ! I’ve never seen
such a crawler in my life. Look sharp ! ”
Randall raised his meagre body, wasted by
disease, tottered across the yard or so of floor, and
took hold of the strap. His effort at self-repres-
sion made the veins stand out on his forehead like
water-washed roots; his teeth were set, his eyes
closed and breath held shudderingly. His whole
body was bent back into expectant rigidity.
The Doctor looked at him, irritated. “Good
God ! man, anyone would think you were going
to be hanged ! ”
The brush met Randall’s flesh almost at the
same instant. The part touched quivered sepa-
rately ; the body suddenly relaxed ; and Randall
moaned despite his will.
“Oh, Doctor, it’s horrible! I can’t—stand—
it! ”
“ Pish ! man, it’s nothing. Come on ! ”
“ God ! what have— I done —to —deserve—
this ! ” Randall slowly grasped the strap, and
assumed position. The Doctor paused with up-
lifted brush : something in the strained attitude
amused him, Randall’s eyes opened and looked
reproachfully at him. He answered their appeal,
his lips parted in a grin : “Pooh ! mam. why—
don’t—you—whistle '! ”
Just then Randall’s eye caught the open pruning-
knife, and all the reserve force that lay in his
weakly frame concentrated in two movements,
made so quickly that they seemed one single
action. The knife went home just where Randall
had dreamed of painting the Doctor with his own
iodine ; and when the matron entered the ward a
few minutes later she screamed and fainted.
They found Randall collapsed two miles down
the road —he had not the strength to go far ; and
he was hanged duly. They had to support him
to the scaffold, but on the drop he stood up
bravely. The hangman said that he smiled like a
man with happy thoughts.
V.
L.M.D.
Violet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Shb was small, with meek, watery blue eyes ; the
expression of her countenance was angelic in its
sweetness and idiotic in its blankness; and her
name was Violet. Like her namesake, she ex-
haled an odour, but there the resemblance ended
abruptly, for the perfume belonged to a dubious
bundle which she fetched along with her. She
took up her abode with us for the space of two
unhallowed months.
“She doesn’t look up to much,” mother re-
marked; “but then she seems agreeable, and is
not at all of a muscular breed. I was always
afraid to say anything to that last girl, lest she
should knock me down.”
Violet was most unassuming. She wouldn’t
presume to lay the cloth on the dining-table or
take the skin off a potato unless her harassed mis-
tress was there to tell her there was no harm in
either operation. As time went on she became
confidential, and gave her opinion on various sub-
jects. She had a strong aversion to clergymen’s
wives and policemen. She had the misfortune to
reside with a clergyman’s wife once. But never
again would she be misled by one, however lady-
like she was in appearance. (Violet was an
authority on good-breeding, and never tired of
putting the afflicted family in their proper places.)
This particular person had spoken disrespectfully
of Violet once, and the injured maid had her ear
to the keyhole at the moment.
“When I ’eard ’er speak o’ Me hin sich un-
sightly terms,” remarked the Flower, bristling
with indignation, “Hijistup and tells ’er, ‘me
mother ’ll ’ave somethink to say to yer for that,’ I
sez, an’ hoff I goes an’ brings me mother down.
My heye, didn’t she jist walk inter ’er ! ” She
drew in a long breath of heavenly satisfaction at
the recollection. Then she went on to relate how
the clergyman’s eldest and unfeeling son came to
his mother’s rescue, and kicked Violet’s odorifer-
ous bundle down the steps, and threatened to do
the same to her mother if they didn’t get off the
premises, which they did, after heaving two
lumps of blue metal at the front door. “But Hi
’ad me revenge; mother summonsed ’em for a
week’s wages, an’ they ’ad to pay-up,” Violet con-
cluded, triumphantly.
After that little recital the family spoke of her
with bated breath as “the young lady of the
house.”
Violet had a deep contempt for the social status
of the family and its friends, and she never
failed to give vent to it whenever opportunity
offered.
‘ ‘ A young gentleman at the door wants to know
if yer wants any pertaters,” she would say; or
else —“ There’s an ole gentleman wants a little
assistance.” But when a respected friend of the
family would arrive, she would remark carelessly,
* ‘ There’s an ole bloke out there wants to see
yer.” . . . . ..
The members of the family were just young
men” and “young women” to her, while the
charwoman was the “ washer-lady.”
Weeks passed ; the middle of the second month
had come, and still Violet bloomed on the pre-
mises. Yet a secret uneasy feeling was rising in
each breast. There was no disguising the fact
that the Flower had a prodigious appetite, and it
was steadily increasing. Supplies were doubled,
and still the demand slackened not. The afflicted
family anticipated the gloomy and not distant
day when the cash would run out and they would
have to beg their way from door to door. Not
the least remarkable part of the affair was that
she remained the small, thin Violet she was before
her appetite developed.
With closed door and cautiously modulated
voice, mother conferred over the phenomenon.
“I am positive that girl is robbing us,” she
whispered. “ How else could she dispose of four
loaves of bread daily, as well as large quantities
of tea, sugar and butter ? She couldn’t possibly
eat it. She’d be dead in a week if she did.
We’re keeping her old mother’s family as well as
our own. I’ll put a stop to it.”
Mother drew herself up, the fire of battle in
her eye, and turned with a firm step to leave the
room. Father arrested her progress in wild
alarm.
“ For Heaven’s sake be careful what you say,
my dear,” he expostulated, “or you might land
me in the Criminal Court for slander.”
“Don’t be afraid. I’ll do nothing rash. I’ve an
idea. I’ll see how it works.”
Acting on the idea, mother put every edible
article in the house under lock and key, and doled
out supplies with her own careful hand. For a
few days the Flower’s appetite became normal.
The family, alarmed at its own temerity, trembled
in its united shoes ; but beyond glancing askance
at her moderate portion, Violet made no sign.
Then the family resumed its usual cheerful aspect;
things are not alwavs as bad as they seem. At
the end of one short week, however, all was
changed. The mistress still presided over a
locked cupboard, but supplies melted mysteri-
ously out of that cupboard when the key was in
its owner’s pocket. The Violet no longer looked
askance at her food, but ate it with an air of
resignation touching in the extreme. The mys-
tery explained itself one day when mother came
upon Violet making a hurried exit from the
before-mentioned receptacle, carrying sundry
packages which she bashfully endeavored to con-
ceal beneath her apron. In her haste she had left
a duplicate key in the lock !
The reproacnes that followed did not disconcert
her in the least—not they. She simply elevated
her nose and voice, and, backing judiciously the
while towards the kitchen door, gave her opinion
of a woman who would dare to lock provisions
away from a “pore” harmless girl, and subject
her to a process of slow starvation. She said that
mother’s parentage was doubtful, and that some
of her forbears had suffered the final sentence of
the law. Then, on reaching the open air, she
took a violent fit of hysterics, and called on the
neighbors to witness that our treatment had
reduced her to a skeleton, and that she
was going to bring mother to her assist-
ance. At this point male aid arrived, and
Violet calmed down slightly, but reiterated her
determination of bringing mother on the scene.
She was offered five minutes to pack up her
belongings and depart. After that, she calmed-
down still more, and begged permission to finish
“ washing-up,” a process she had apparently begun
before the outburst occurred. She wept so
bitterly at the idea of going away without wash-
ing-up that, at last, her period of grace was
extended to half-an-hour. When Violet learned
that she might complete the washing-up, she wept
tears of thankfulness, and said, “Gorblessyer 1”
A few minutes later, the whole house was per-
vaded with a smell of burning rags and sundries.
Everybody rushed to the kitchen. Violet had
fled, but she had left her mark behind her. The
sleeve of mother’s last new blouse protruded from
the range, and, as she flew to rescue it, she trod
on the shattered remains of six bread-and-butter
plates, belonging to the best tea-set ! Also, the
gas was turned-on everywhere; two pounds of
butter had been put up the chimney ; the cruet
was full of kerosene, and a new belltopper stood
on the floor full of coal.
N.s.w.
EVELINE DOWNEY.
“Holdar M.” again voices a venerable
complaint :
For funereal slowness, Sydney Barrack-st. Savings
Bank would be hard to beat. There’s only one man to
receive deposits, and his failure to cope with the daily
rush is pathetic. And at the paying-out counter it’s just
as bad, though there two Reginalds officiate. Generally
takes present writer 15 to 30min. to lodge or withdraw
cash, and no verbal severity has any perceptible effect
upon the languid pen-suckers who condescend to be
aware of the surging mob. Also, most of the clerks dis-
appear for lunch between 1 and 2 p.m., the only hour
when many of the humbler depositors can visit the bank.
Likewise the push-and-struggle tactics employed by
impatient clients to get before those who have waited
longer is another matter calling urgently for reform by
the institution of the “ queue ” system. Now that Dam-
chicago Dibbs has finished that walking-stick for King
Ned perhaps he’ll look into these base ordinary matters.
“R.R.”: Vulcan is in the metropolis.
He is employed by a hardware firm to do odd
jobs in the way of iron-forging and smithy work.
He labors in a cave-like ruin beneath a rotting
building where the beams bend inwards and the
brickwork shudders every time his sledge-hammer
hits a jarring piece of iron or steel. Quite re-
cently the manager of the establishment looked
in on Vulcan and saw the dangerous condition of
the underground workshop, but knowing that the
leasehold expires within a short time he isn’t in-
clined to spend £lOO or so in bolstering up the
premises. He thought it fair, however, to warn
the unthinking Vulcan re the tons of lumber that
would come down in case of a collapse. Where-
upon Vulcan stared hard at the crumbling beams
and the bulging brickwork, and remarked that if
things did move down nothing but his anvil would
come out alive. Whereupon he refuse d to strike
another blow unless he received an extra half-
crown a, week. He got it. The manager looks
upon the extra money as an in urance fund
and his conscience is quiet. The employees
exhibit a certain skittishness when they are told
to take a job down to Vulcan —they stand afar off
with the job in their hands, and throw it in
the direction of Vulcan’s vault, shouting out
the necessary instructions. But Vulcan, with an
extra half-crown in his mind, hammers on. Some -
times he stops work to look up at the dreadful
beams when they cry and shiver more than usual,
and hunches his back as though getting ready.
Writer is putting his m 0 ney on the 200 tons or so
of lumber. There will be a terrific struggle beside
the anvil one of these days.

Forced Draught.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The tail-rods leap to lift her,
A-singin’ as they rise,
And turnin’ swift and swifter
The smooth white shaftin’ flies.
The great cranks sink to shove her,
And as they sink they vow
That tho’ they drive they love her
From heel to chatterin’ bow;
So set your fire-doors bangin’ 1
And, trimmers, give us coal
To keep' the shovels clangin’
And shaftin’ all a-roll!
By roar of flue and furnace
And throttle-valves a-gape
We’ll show the craft who’d turn us
What speed she’ll have to shape.
With air-shafts full and suckin’
And ventilators slammed,
With every crank-pin throwirH
A spray of oil, and glowin’,
And doors shut tight and jammed,
We’ll set the good screws buckin’
And stoke like sinners damned.
More steam ! the “ Chief ” is callin’,
For though she’s racin’ fine
From twenty-two she’s fallen
To twenty-one point nine.
More steam ! I guess she’s divin’
Clean through the sloppy swells,
She’s that dead set on drivin’
She thinks of nothin’ else.
She has no time to stagger,
. Nor yet to lift or swing ;
She only mocks the lagger
And hears her engines ring;
And far and thick behind her
Her inky smoke-train lies —
Tho’ smashin’ rollers blind her
She laughs, and scorns to rise.
Proud in her speed that steadies,
Thro’ sea and night she sails,
And where the tide-rip eddies
The spray will kiss her rails.
Her mast-head vane’s a-quiver,
Her funnels blist’rin’ grey ;
The big fish hear her cornin’ —
With mad propellers strummin ’
A sea-song—up the bay ;
And by the town-lit river,
“ Well-steamed indeed J” they’ll say.
There goes a journal gratin’,
A bearin’s just a-squeal—■
But every greaser’s waitin’,
And oil is good for steel
When swells are vainly tryin*
To teach her how to climb—
Or down with engines flyin’—
She nearly raced that time !
Again she lifts, and chokin’,
Her sorews are drivin’ dull—
And now she races, smokin’,
To test her strainin’ hull.
Oh ! hear the ‘ ‘ Second ” curse het
(She seems to understand)
But he has got to nurse her—
Her throttle’s in his hand.
lldll nurse her as she scatters
The water in her track,
He’ll check her when she’s divin ’
And soarin’, —set her drivin ’
With thrust-blocks all a-rack ;
He damns her when she chatters,
And hears her answer back.
The cranks roll fast and flashin’,
And mighty-thewed as gods,
A-thunder all and crashin’,
Oh ! hear the laborin’ rods !
And through the tunnel moanin’
The cold, white shaftin’ spins,
And every standard’s groanin’
And reck’nin’ up our sins.
The gauges jump and twinkle,
Full twenty-two she’s done ...
But hear the bridge-bell’s tinkle
“ Half-speed ? ” We ain’t begun !
“ Half-speed ! ” and feel her swingin’
Across the river bar,
With tail-rods loose and ringin’
And cross-head guides a-jar.
And up the sleepy river
We’ll go at easy steam,
With big screws gently spoonin',
And engines softly croonin’
A sea-tune to the stream,
That dreams with breast a-quiver
A lover’s peaceful drea/m.
Oh ! when the mottled water
Is boilin’ angry aft,
Where two wild screws have caught her
A-swirl with extra draught,
It’s good to hear the thunder
And moanin’ of the steel;
But, oh ! to toil down under—
It teaches men to feel.
There ain’t no sea-breeze singin’
Around our lips and brows;
We hear no water dingin’
And sprayin’ at her bows.
It’s steamy heat and labor,
And sweaty, oily reek,
And cursin’ with your neighbor,
And faintin’ if you’re weak.
But though we loudly cursed her,
Her god-like song was wild ;
And though we toiled we loved her,
And every rod that shoved her
To fight the seas up-piled.
And oh l the “ Second ” nursed her
As though she were his child.
M.L.
QUILP N.
The Land of Tinned-Dog.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear Bulletin.— For my sins, I live in the land
of Tinned-Dog, which here I attempt feebly to
describe.
This land lies, roughly, 200 miles north of
Broken Hill, and 250 miles west of the Paroo.
Its chief city contains 18 houses—lo of them
empty ; a gorgeous court-house and police-station,
and a still more gorgeous post-office ; all, like the
rest of the city, of cut stone. For of straight
timber in this land there is none ; while of stone,
it stinketh. And weatherboards, corrugated iron
and co. fit not on a camel’s back. Its inhabitants
total 23, of whom 17 are civil servants. One is a
publican, and five are applicants for old-age
pensions. The civil servants spend their time in
cutting each other’s hair and cursing their fate.
For, while there are a police-magistrate, C.P.S.,
postmaster, mining-registrar, telegraph-operator,
school-teacher, stock-inspector, inspector of water-
ing-places, two constables, pound-keeper, &c.,
there are neither beef nor mutton, milk nor eggs,
nor any butcher, baker, barber, bootmaker or
blacksmith.
The town had a creek, also a waterhole a mile
long and 15ft. deep, but sand-storms long ago
filled both up.
It has wheel-tracks across the desolate plains—
the people cried out for a road, but the Keystone
replied that the existing roads were good enough
for the people who lived there. But then, unless
Coghlan lies, we are but one man to thirteen
square miles ; and for M.L.A. we have one of
whom we hear nothing and see less.
Three tracks —from Bourke, Wilcannia and
Broken Hill —converge on the city of Tinned-Dog;
each more damnable than the other.
On the least damnable, after 40 miles of misery,
I reached Torrowangee. Here I found seven
houses and nine typhoid patients. The post-
master had died the week before of a broken
heart caused by watching two pepper-trees which
wouldn’t grow.
Appalled, I fled to the next stopping place—
Euriowie. When I got there I wished to God I
had remained at Torrowangee.
Then for 40 hours a never-ending nightmare of
vast drought-stricken plains, stretching out for
miles to sandhills magnified by the hazy atmos-
phere into ranges, and strewn thickly with glit-
tering white lumps of quartz, changing after a
dozen miles to red, and then to black, iron-stone,
with queer, jumbled outcrops of dark barren
rock.
Packed in between these stony tracts lay great
wastes of soft, shifting, choking sand. Of grass,
not a blade ; patches of saltbush here and there,
and a little, stunted, half-dead mulga, lopped
long ago for the last of the starving stock. Of
wild animals I saw not one; of birds, none but
crows.
There are two or three God-forsaken camps
strewed around miscalled goldfields, peopled with
the queerest old men I ever dropped across.
Literally starving, absolutely hopeless, driven
into semi-idiocy by their ghastly surroundings,
they still refuse to exchange their humpies of bags
and boughs for Liverpool or Parramatta, and live
upon the hope of getting one last drunk on an
pension. Three things strike a stranger
chiefly : First the number of watercourses
created by the annual average of Sin. of rain j
secondly the thin sinuous snakes of low gum-
trees, crawling for miles, and showing out vividly
green against the mournful barrenness of the
plains, there being a strict Government regula-
tion a ga mst cutting timber in water-courses 5
thirdly what in the name of Heaven the rem-
nants of sheep live on ? I stopped my conveyance,
and crawled painfully over the broken stones to
where a small mob was apparently feeding. I
found no sign of any green thing. The driver
said they were licking up seeds. But—seeds of
what?
Still, even in this ultima thule the people con-
gregate and hold horse-races—three in ten days,
within a 30-mile radius; with the usual trim-
mings of monte-men and other assorted spielers.
Five started for the Cup. The betting was 2to
1 on, 6 to 4 on, evens, 2 to 1 against and 3 to
1 against—real Ikey Mo.’s.
J-he winning jock (needless to say, not the 2 to
1 on show) wore a red shirt and moles, one hand-
kerchief round his neck, and a second round his
left leg—what for, I couldn’t guess. After this
came the usual scrap between Wonnaminto Joe
and Flaming Ned; while a station-manager
knocked a man down for calling him “Tom”—
“ like his b- impudence! ” The shearers, stirred
up by their leaders, are spoiling for a fight, but
it is evident there will be none in Tinned-Dog
District this year, for the station hands can shear
all the sheep alive.
Even in this, the direst of droughts, the national
flower of Tinned-Dog—the gidyea—flourisheth
and bloometh—yea, and smelleth like unto a
Chinaman’s garden afar off, or a bullock that has
been dead a week.
That I may soon again sniff the Chinese tobacco
and opium of Lower George-street is the humble
prayer of yours truly,
Milpabinka, N.S.W.
YARRAN.
Dear Bulletin.—.Re “Gipps ” (B. 6/7/’l).
From Annual Register, 15th April, 1812:
On Wednesday evening an extraordinary investigation
took place at Bow-street. Oroker, the officer, was passing
along the Hampstead-road when he observed, at a short
distance before him, two men on a wall, and, directly
after, saw the tallest of them, a stout man about six feet
high, hanging by his neck from a lamp-post attached to
the wall, being that instant tied up and turned off by the
short man. This unexpected and extraordinary sight
astonished the officer; he made up to the spot with
all speed ; and just as he arrived there the tall
man, who had been hanged, fell to the ground,
the handkerchief with which he had been sus-
pended having given way. Oroker produced his staff,
said he was an officer, and demanded to know of the other
man the cause of such conduct. In the meantime, the
man who had been hanged recovered, got up, and on
Oroker’s interfering, gave him a violent blow on the nose
which nearly knocked him backwards. The short man
endeavouring to make off, however, the officer pro-
cured assistance, and both were brought to the office.
They said they had been together on Wednesday
afternoon, tossed up for money, and afterwards for
their clothes, the tall man, who was hanged, won
the other’s jacket, trousers, and shoes ; they then tossed
up which should hang the other, and the short one won
the toss. They both agreed in this statement. The tall
one, who had been hanged, said, if he had won the toss,
he would have hanged the other. The magistrates ex-
pressed their disgust, and ordered the man who had been
hanged to find bail for the violent and unjustifiable
assault on the officer, and the short one for hanging the
other. Not having bail, they were committed to Bride-
well for trial. “ Nothing new under the sun.”
